THE RED PAGE.
I WOULD like to see a picture of “ Human
Evolution ” painted by an artist with the
skill and imagination of Max Klinger. To
right of the centre should appear the vivid,
„ sunlit figure of a man marching—the Man
to-day. Behind him and a little beneath
should be the broadening company of Shadows
from whom he is descended—the parents,
the ancestors, the primitive men—the ape-
animals, dog-animals, swine-animals—all the
array of brutes which- succeeded the fish-animals
and reptiles—and so down the scale of hereditary
life until the Outlines of shape, clear at first, grow-
ing gradually vague, should lose themselves in
gloom of the Beginning. Before him and a little
above should be the company of Shadows to
whom he gives birth—his children, his grand-
children, his descendants in illimitable genera-
tions, expanding fanwise more and more darkly
till they should vanish in gloom of the End.
Around all the sand-glass—symbol of the lapse
of Time. Below the centre, the tropical beach
where we think Life first may have begun. Above,
the face of supreme Man, Beyond-Man, Man of
the Future, with his mouth of sweetness, eyes of
dream, and brow of power.
[The artist might offer the central Adam an Eve
for mate, but woman is unessential to the concep-
tion. Sex is an evolutionary detail. The woman
gives Life extension, not intensity—makes for
perpetuity, not progress. It is true that many
men who walk down George-street still clutch a
stick—a movable tree-bough—a reminiscence of
the ape-ancestor; # but many more women carry
parasols—nearly every woman is uneasy without
something in her hands—some holdfast in case
she drop from the hereditary branch. And the
man in George-street straightens his spine; the
woman stoops more—she has been more recently
erected from all-fours. Sitting is a concession to the
quadruped in both sexes; but woman is the seden-
tary sex. And woman, the wilder animal, holds
more of the wild-animal’s vitality ; she lives longer
than the man, so that in compensation for stand-
ing up last she lies down last. All this being
seductively divagatory, diseontinuously episodical,
unnecessarily irrelevant, and ten more long words
implying bad art. Comes of watching local
painters sacrifice art to idiosyncrasy.]
In European houses where family portraits have
been preserved for generation after generation,
* The secondary use of the stick for defence does not
affect the fact that, as an ornamental companion, it is
usually a badge of weak character, scanty intellectual
acquirement, superficial civilisation. Henry Parkes dis-
dained umbrellas; but the crooked appendage of the
vacuous English “ Johnnie ”is proverbial. The later use
of the bough as a weapon, a club, is seen among the more
primitive, or more vital, races and individuals. The
Irishman’s shillelagh thwacks the same broken-skulled
moral as the Australian aboriginal’s nulla-nulla. There
are other local examples besides that of George Dibbs
who presents a ring-gidya stick to the British king. In the
hands of G. Bibbs the stick would typify club-usage • in
the hands of King Edward it will typify cling-usage—the
transference reversing the process by which the weapon-
symbol of the virile monarch of yore became the orna-
mental sceptre of the weakling modern monarch. When
war brought peace, the second usage of the stick became
the third usage as a badge of authority or office—as in an
usher’s wand, a Speaker’s mace. But through all changes
it is the atavistic bough—the ape-man’s heirloom.
“ Imperial Csesar, dead and turned to clay
May stop a hole to keep the wind away ”
and the supports of our ancestral forest-home become the
dude’s solace, the king’s sceptre, the jest of a Melbourne ,
bagnio. These he evil days.
the scion of a historic stock may sometimes trace
his own lineaments in a pictured ancestral face.
hich of us has not noticed the reproduction by
a child of some trick of gait or gesture not seen in
the parents, referable perhaps to a grand-parent
perhaps seen only in a distant kinsman ? How
often in a family, is there observed some son or
daughter with traits dissimilar from those of all
known relatives. The records of breeders of
animals contain instances of individual reversion
to a type which disappeared from the line scores of
years before. Weismann’s theory of the germ-
p asm explains these and analogous cases by pos-
tulating a halving of the germ-elements - one
set becoming active in the production of
a new body, while the other set is handed
down as a latent force which may become
active only after the passage of generations
On this theory, every human being holds
some trace, however infinitesimal, of the influence
of every one of his human and other ancestors
right to the beginning of Life. Stand, then,
before a mirror, and see the shadowy faces of the
Past peering over your shoulder in an army
which would over-fill the earth—since, even
to the time of the Norman conquest, a man
of to-day may count 86 million forbears who have
mixed their blood in his. More, see those faces
peering through you—looking througli your eyes,
giving that shape to your forehead, that turn to
your nostril, that line to your lips; and realise
how insignificant is any single individual in the
line of evolution. So little of himself is his own,
to so very, very small an extent is he able to per-
petuate the acquirement of his brief life, that the
individual becomes in the scientific aspect hardly
more than a germ-bearer—one whose function in
the universal process is merely to hand on to
another the flame of existence.
Then consider the apparatus of thought and
emotion which climaxes in the brain. Just as the
fleshless hands of millions of ancestors have given
to your hand its slowly-acquired prehensile power,
just as every peripheral organ is an inherited pro-
duct tardily won by myriads of beings that once
had active life and are now dissipated in the
mouldered dust of ages, so the central nervous
system and its resultant of conscious thought are
your legacy from long-gone cycles of cen-
turies. The human infant is an ape with
potentialities, the human child a barbarian
capable of civilisation; and you, the man
your brain repeats the ancestral brain as
your body repeats the anoestral body—it conforms
with a type which varies only minutely in thou-
sands of years. As you think, then, before the
mirror, see the pale wraiths of ancestral thought
come floating from the far past to merge with and
alter the thoughts which your own experience has
originated ; listen to “the savage turning in his
tomb”; and, once more, at the height of your
pride of individuality, be very'humble in the
presence of that tremendous debt. You are
“yourself” ; but how much yourself? or, rather,
how inconceivably little !
A new theory of the development of ideas sug-
gests that, in inheriting the ancestral thought-
machine, we inherit also a number of latent
ancestral thoughts. That is, the hereditary
brain-cells, though they be composed of en-
tirely fresh nerve-plasma, are yet arranged
after an ancestral plan beyond our control, and
can spontaneously generate thoughts in which in-
dividual experience has no part. The theory is
but an extension to conscious thought of a power
well-known to reside in hereditary nerve-cells
when automatic impulse is concerned. Conscious
individual brain-acquirement, for example, can
have no share in the nerve-control of the fcetal
heart. N. S. Shaler, Harvard professor of geo-
logy, who writes of this and kindred subjects in
his recent book about “The Individual,” relates
his endeavours to capture the spontaneous
thoughts which seem just to rise to the level of
consciousness—to be always flitting over the
edge of the conscious horizon ; and finds that one’s
capacity to think in a way apparently independent
of his own experience can be strengthened and in-
creased by deliberate effort.
Making the befitting literary application, this
theory furnishes interesting explanation of the
irresponsible character of lyric poetry. From the
earliest times the poet has been regarded as
“possessed” by some external power. In this
“possession” the priestess of Apollo raved, the
Italian improvisators chanted, the Maori tohunga
writhed and foamed. From the earliest times the
poet’s chief themes have been love and death, the
beauty of skies and woods and waters,—aboriginal
passions and the natural phenomena familiar from
the human cradle. We know now that the power
resides in the poet’s brain, that the poetic
“possession ” is mere escape of brain-centres from
their normal control; and We infer that lyric
poetry is essentially the product of inherited
emotion. ,
Sometimes the escape is conscious, the inherit-
ance realised. You feel a detachment, a duality
in your brain, as if some primal breath had blown
across it. In our small local sphere, Hoderic
Quinn tells me that there are times when his own
individuality seems to sleep; when he fancies
himself standing on a Donegal cliff under a
wild sky, gazing through driving sleet at the dark
Atlantic heaving’ below; and strange alien
thoughts come teeming, crowding. Between
dreams and waking Will Ogilvie, bred
on the Scottish border, has imagined him-
self tieading a reivers’ band across the
Tweed, and the picture has recurred with a vivid,
an intimate detail which seems never to have
been learnt through his own senses. And Louise
Mack says that “When I write verse I am not
conscious of words —the feeling and the thought
are almost dropped on the paper. The moment
X am conscious, think of a word—the poem is
dead, and I stop—can’t hear it, don’t feel it I
always write poetry as if it is someone else’s that
I ve half-forgotten, and slowly am drawing down
from the recesses of the brain, driven to it by
some tide of feeling.”
Thus is justified the belief which Macaulay
reached on other grounds, that civilisation implies
the decay and death of poetry. The more We
read, the more we remember, the less opportunity
we give to the primal ancestor within us to pic-
ture the emotions which he felt when red blood
surged through a virgin brain in the vigorous
youth of the race. It nas been written that every
savage is a poet, however imperfect his power of
expression. It may soon be written that every
civilised man is prosy. Only here and there some
young and vital brain, escaping from the worry of
modern life, the weight of bookish ages, survives
to astonish us with the keen sight, the full emo-
tion, the glowing picture, born in some prehistoric
group of brain-cells perchance ten thousand vears
ago.
The moral is that a man should be kind to his
ancestors now they are old. If you are a Murphy,
before you read “The Decline and Fall of the
Roman Empire,” reflect that the mass of Gib-
bon’s resounding facts may bury Red Murtagh,
the B.C. bard, under a weight which will make it
impossible for him to emit one spark of the fiery
inspiration which gladdened the hearts of his
sept. If you are a Macpherson, think that the
study of medicine may murder an Ossian
within you. Your brain is the tomb of your
ancestors; but they have been buried alive —
if you listen you may hear them squeak-
ing. Conceive the array of personal
ghosts putting mournful heads out of their nerve-
cells when they wake up along with you each
morning, and hear Lah, the cave-man, remarking
to First-up-a-Tree, the ape-man: “He read a
society novel last night ! That was a barrowful
of clods on Johnson. Johnson’s dead ! ”
No ; don’t read so many books ; don’t acquire
so much experience; don’t worry your over-
burdened brain. Go out into the sun and the rain,
and let your ancestors talk to you. Love, fear,
hate, curiosity,—all your most vital emotions, —
they share them all. It may be, if you are a
weakling denizen of cities, that they still feel
them more keenly than you can feel with your
little bit of individuality. Reverence old age,
especially your own old age within your brain,
because that typifies the youth of your race, and
you can never be so young and joyous, so young
and strong, so young and loving, as your race has
beeu. It lias been said that to little children
belongs the kingdom of Heaven. It can be more
truly said that to little children belongs the king-
dom of Earth. And this is the gospel and the
Law.

Re “Our Future” : I havo watched the long
procession of the critics of our own Australia file
past. One genuine factor of Australia’s future
they nearly all ignored—the Bush child. Some-
one, I believe, casually pitied him. I have known
him intimately for years, and never was pity more
misplaced, for lie is one of the happiest little crea-
tures under the sun. 111-clad, uncouth, expres-
sionless to the eye of the cultured outsider he may
be. But his first impressions are of air, sunshine,
and liberty. His meals, if little varied, and not
always of the best quality, are frequent and plen-
tiful. I have never seen a hungry bush child.
Later on he is promoted to a little shirt, made
from a flour-bag, and two shapeless evolutions
from his father’s moleskins, and the dogs and the
old pony (lean and uncomely, but to him models
of symmetry) become his cherished companions
and possessions, and the“crik” his happy hunt-
ing-ground. Such work as he is called upon to
perform is light and changeful, with frequent
intervals of relaxation.
His parents are unburdened with any feeling of
moral responsibility concerning him ; they merely
cuff him promiscuously when he becomes a per-
sonal nuisance. His mind is never disturbed by
conventionalities or religious difficulties, although
the minister is an occasional object of criticism
through a friendly chink in the kitchen door ; Und
when his eye aches the bush child goes outside
and criticises the minister’s horse, often with even
greater reason.
And he is at liberty to express his opinion (if
any) on all subjects with refreshing candour. Yet
the State school (an object of indifference or aver-
sion in some older countries) is regarded both by
him and by his parents with a pleasing sense of
proprietorship— “ our school ” and “ our teacher.”
And if the bush teacher be fairly capable, or,
better still, a “lady” teacher and prepossessing,
the bush child regards the teacher with admiring
curiosity.
The bush child is wonderfully persistent, won-
derfully adaptable, wonderfully full of resource—
when he chooses. But he does not always choose.
—J.B.

[Several letters in defence of Plagiary held over.]
“Mr. Alfred R. Coffey . . . has been travelling
in Europe. . . . The young painter writes . . .
that he was especially delighted with Madrid, and
could scarcely tear himself away from the prin-
cipal picture gallery, which actually contained 62
Rubens, 10 Raphaels, 46- Murillos, 64 Velasquez,
43 Titians, 22 Van Dycks, &c.” No ;we have n’t
these in N.S. W. Gallery; but we have their names
(ay, and more names !) carved outside the Gallery
building.
One gets tired of all the fuss about the Mitchell
library, and the “splendid generosity” of the
Sydney donor who offers his “magnificent collec-
tion of books” to N.S.W. in exchange for per-
petual remembrance. What better can Mr. Mit-
chell do with his books ? They are of no use to
him, for he is n’t an original worker ; he Can only
keep them and gloat over them while he lives,
and when he dies he can’t take them with him.
If he turns them into money, he can’t spend the
money on personal enjoyment. There is nothing for
him to do but give them where they will be of use.
His action is to be praised, of course, as an ex-
ample to others ; but there is no generosity about
it—the gift will cost Mr. Mitchell nothing, from
his point of view. From the public point of view
the gift should be thankfully taken, and proper
accommodation should be provided for the books.
But N.S.W. Govt, has no money ; it has wasted
so much in riot and extravagance that even in-
creased revenue hasn’t helped it much. Well,
if IS.S. Wales won’t find accommodation, let Mr.
Mitchell offer his books to Victoria, or to the Com-
monwealth, or wherever he likes. They’ll be
just as useful in Melbourne as in Sydney.
The Kiama ghost isn’t thoroughly laid yet.
Kiama School of Arts has been considering a
solemn protest by the parish priest against one of
Joseph Hooking s novels, characterised as “an
outrage to Catholic instincts.” Joseph and his
brother Silas are both Methodist ministers, both
fifth-rate novelists, both panders to the illiterate
Nonconformist. If there is any difference be-
tween them, it is that Joseph is slightly the better
Methodist, and slightly the worse novelist. All
that the Kiama P.P. has secured is bold advertise-
ment for Joseph—to whom advertisement is the
breath of Silas’s life.
THE BOOKFELLOW.
THE REIVER’S HEART.
Across the Cheviot passes
Rode those rough sires of mine,
To lift the Southern lasses
And drive the Southern kine;
In epic and in idyl
Those days are but a dream,
Yet hand to Fancy's bridle
I ford the Boumont stream.
When all the world is sleeping
Beneath the Night's warm wing,
And the pale moon comes sweeping
With vanguard glistening,
And slow, in open order,
The starry scouts advance,
I cross the misty Border
To raid the Realm. Romance.
I force them to my bidding
Beneath the Jethart axe >•
I rob the kye-full steading
And fire the golden stacks ;
I lift the soft-eyed maiden
Across the champing steed ;
And so, with love light-laden,
Ride northward to the Tweed.
I see the stirks together
Crowd on before the steel;
I smell the dewy heather
That sweeps my rowelled heel;
I watch the red flames leaping
Above the ruined farm,
And hear the lone maid weeping
Below my bridle-arm.
A moor-cock, warned of battle,
Crows welcome in the mist :
Once—for our footsore cattle ;
Twice—for our maids rough-kissed.
Ho ! this is royal barter
For one short night in 'mail:
See ! Dawn upon the Carter l
And yonder’s Teviotdale l
Though now the hot hoofs thunder
No more down Coquet-sidc,
Nor South for white-armed plunder
The wild moss-troopers ride,
When Beauty wants a warder,
When fight and foray start,
We, bred upon the Border,
Have still the reiver's heart /
WILL H. OGILVIE.
THE FISHER FLEET.
I had been walking under stars
(How beautiful our Autumn nights t)
And close beside me gloomed the sea ,
With far-off, tossing, top-mast lights.
The wide, dark waters seemed to cry
For one to hearken their distress,
And on the waves a Wonder walked,
That strangely wrought my soul to guess.
A little while H was calm, and then
A wind with wetness on its wings
Came quietly and charged my blood
With memories of many things.
I saw a long-lost herring fleet
Sail landward in a sunset glow ;
But what the sea it sailed upon,
And to what land, I did not know.
A silver tumult in the boats
The clean, sweet-smelling herrings seemed;
And, here and there, was one that died,
The while its red gills gaped and gleamed.
With song and call the boats sailed on,
While night and dark began to creep
Upon the reapers of the sea
Who harvested the crowded deep.
But ah ! as one by one they filssed,
My heart, grown cold, scarce seemed to beat;
For one, who wore my eyes and hair,
Was Captain of the Fisher Fleet.
I stood in silence on the sand,
And all was clear as noon to me ;
In ancient days my soul had found
Dark kinship with the midnight sea.
N.S.W.
RODERIO QUINN.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To Our Old Mate, Fortune.
You come not to the ranges, as in the days of old,
To flood our hearts with gladness, and fill our
hands with gold ;
You graced the New Land diggings with all we
held most dear,
Our sweethearts fi - om the Home Lands, our glad-
ness and our beer.
We met you first at Fryer’s (you may recall the
day
Dick “ shouted ” in your honor in a large histone
way)
To F'xviehawk you led us ; and thence to Myer s
Flat;
Then a golden reef you pegged for us on busy
Ballarat;
And when the shoot grew faulty we acted on your
“tip,”
We registered a company and gaily sold the scrip.
• * * *
You’ll own we acted squarely, “ Here s fortune,
boys ! ” we’d say,
And drank your health in liquor vile some twenty
times a day ;
Yet when—all through our faith in you—we
bought the “ Royal Prince,”
Without a word you left us, and haven’t shown
up since.
Now, is it mere divine caprice, or things that
Dick has said
(When in drink he’s disrespectful) that makes
you cut us dead ?
But anyhow, we’ve sought you long, and sought
you all in vain,
To say “ All is forgiven ; come and board with us
again 1 ”
v.
FRANK BELLMAN.
Walgett.
Where the Namoi, drifting slowly,
Joins the muddy Barwon stream ;
Where the ground is dry and holey
Want and hunger are supreme—
There, in all its dusty glory,
Stands the perished Walgett town,
Setting forth a piteous story
Typed in letters baked and brown.
Weary horses blindly wandering.
Seeking vainly for a bite ;
Workmen, at their wits’ ends, pondering
How to best maintain the fight.
Streets deserted, business stagnant,
Starving tradesmen void of rent;
Banks that clamour to the heavens
For the money they have lent.
Dried-up tanks, and rivers failing,
Scrub-trees coming to an end.
Wells worked-out with endless baling ;
Poison-weed down in the bend.
Jumbucks by the thousand dying,
Cattle bogging by the score ;
Creditors relentless, crying
As they’ve often cried before.
Ghostly spectre gliding slowly
Round the blighted homestead fence,
Where the ground is dry and holey
Through the ring-barked timber dense ;
His domain is wide and lonely,
And a goat, some haggard curs,
And a loony cow drift only
O’er its whited sepulchres.
Wandering swagmen limping forward
To the land of God-Knows-Where ;
Dust-storms flying westward, nor’ward,
Sand and heat and bones and glare ;
Pallid folks whose hearts are broken —
Track these indications down,
And you’ll know by that same token
You’re approaching Walgett town.
N.S.W.
D. H. RICHARDS.
S.M. Herald advt. :
TTAVE those you slighted me for, and I will love
someone else. To you, from M.
Evidently a philosophical and accommodating
person.
The sea-serpent begins to gain an air of
probability. The latest is that one of him—
probably a member of the sub-marine push—made
an unprovoked attack on a fishing-smack off
Corner Inlet (Victoria), and bit it so severely that
he left three teeth in the boat. The teeth are
there right enough, so the experience wasn’t all
fiction or all whisky.
There is some excitement at present in
Otago (M.L.), at a small rock-strewn waste some
eight miles or so from another melancholy spot
known as Middlemarch. A certain woman began
giving accounts of a man who made periodic visits
to her house, stealing, breaking, etc. About the
same time sheds, barns, and other structures of
the forlorn locality began to get mysteriously
burnt, with the result that the insurance offices
rose up in wrath and refused to “ take ” any risks
in the place aforementioned until the violent man
was captured. As usual it came to light that the
woman had done all these marvels herself. She
alleged that she was tired of the rocky wilderness,
and thinking others might be tired also thought
she would put a little excitement into their dull
lives. If she had been an Australienne she would
have added the Man with Violent Intentions to
her tale —but she was only a simple Maoriland girl,
and she did not think of that.

THE SENATE,
Senator NEILD hoped the Senate would not
• agree to the motion. It was a most deliberate;
attempt to belittle the House, and troat it as
occupying the position of the Legislative Councils
of the different States The House was appar
ently to be treated by the Ministry in the matter
of publio expenditure simply as a Chamber oL
revision. % y
Sir J. Madden not only draws a salary of £3500
a year as Chief-Justice, but he also draws £5OOO a
year as Libutenant-Governor, bringing his official
income up to £B5OO, in addition to which he is
provided with an official residenoe at “ Stonning-
i !"?*?» Toorak. His Excellency does not occupy
otonmngton ” just now, on the plea that it is too
from Melbourne, but he is at liberty to use it
if he felt so disposed. The Government is anxious
Jto bring the matter to a termination, but the
.leisured run of tho correspondence is stringing the
negotiations out for an unconscionable time. Steps
are now being taken by the Ministry to ascertain
wbat the Secretary of State for the Colonies in-
tends to do in the matter.

■House of Representatives of Mr. King O’Malley,

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia.,
Westralia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
€l, with 3s. 3d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of lid. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage—lid. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tiib Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and ail Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, managing director.
Literary communications to be addressed to J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand _ that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Blade and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JUNE 22, 1901.
The Jap. on the Horizon.
A few weeks ago news reached Australia that the
British Government had vetoed the Queensland
Act providing that State aid should be given to
certain sugar mills with the condition, that no
colored labor should be employed therein—in
other words, that the Government, as the party
who found the money, and was, therefore, the
virtual employer, claimed the right, like any other
employer, to engage only white workers if it felt
that way inclined. Now an explanation comes by
cable :
Mr. J. Chamberlain, the Secretary of State for the
Colonies, in the House of .Commons last night, in answer
to a question as to the 'reason of the Royal assent being
refused to the passing of the Queensland Sugar Works
Guarantee Act Amending Bill, said that the Imperial
Government considered that the Queensland Bill
proceeded on objectionable grounds. The principle
of the policy of disqualification was based solely on
the place of origin of colored labor, and excluded the
Indian subjects of Great Britain solely for the difference
of race and color. Moreover, the disqualification was
offensive to the Japanese, who were placed in the cate-
gory of Asiatics subject to the bill regardless of their
civilisation.
What a clumsy lie ! Australia doesn’t care whether
the Asiatic was born in Asia or in Sheol. It doesn’t
care whether he is black, or brown, or bright-
green with red feet and a blue stripe down the
back. So far from excluding the Asiatic solely
on account of his race and color, neither his race
nor his color have anything to do with the matter.
Australia objects to' the whole Asiatic, African
and Kanaka tribe because they work for wages on
which only a person far lower in the scale of
civilisation than the white Australian can live,
therefore, where they are numerous, the white
man, in order to get work, has to come down to
their wage level, and, in consequence, to their
civilisation-level. It objects to them because they
introduce a lower civilisation. It objects because
they intermarry with white women, and thereby
lower the white type, and because they have
already created the beginnings of a mongrel race,
that has many of the vices of both its parents and
few of the virtues of either. If Judas Chamber-
lain can find a black, or brown, or yellow race, in
Asia or Africa, that has as high a standard of
civilisation and intelligence as the whites, that is
as progressive as the whites, as brave, as sturdy,
as good nation-making material, and that can in-
termarry with the whites without the mixed
progeny showing signs of deterioration, that race
is welcome in Australia regardless of color.
* « * *
Is Joseph _ Chamberlain said, as reported by
cable, that it was proposed, in Queensland or
any oth"~ oart of Australia, to put disabilities on
anyon< mse of his color, then Joseph Cham-
berla s shamelessly perverting facts. If he
said, as reported by cable, that it was proposed to
put disabilities on anyone by reason of the place
of his birth, then Joseph Chamberlain is
lying over once more. The Australian would
sooner be mulatto or Eurasian than white—if the
mulatto and Eurasian were a better man than the
white. If the Englishman in India will cease to
regard the native as an inferior; if he will look
upon the Eurasian as an equal and treat him as
such ; if, in an Empire which is 90 per cent, nigger,
he will let the black man have his fair share of such
billets as that of Viceroy of India, Commander in-
Chief (of the army which is furnished by the
mostly-nigger empire), colonial Governor, &c.; if
he will get into such a frame of mind that Queen
Victoria could have married an Indian rajah and
given a half-caste heir to the British throne with-
out causing any objection or disturbance—then
Joseph Chamberlain will have a right to say
things. If Britain’s Ktpling will cease to kipple
derisively about the half-bred race which forms
the outermost fringe of the European population
in India, and other English writers will mitigate
their scorn about the mongrel peoples of the
Levant—the “scum of the Levant,” as they are
often called—with the blood of Greece, Syria,
Egypt, the Archipelago and possibly of Italy in
their veins, then Joseph Chamberlain may look
less of a liar and a smaller amount of a fool.
If the same Joseph who prates of Japanese
“civilisation” as entitling the people of Japan
to enter Australia on equal terms, will show
that he believes there is a real Japanese
civilisation by seriously considering the ques-
tion of a royal intermarriage with the Japan-
ese reigning House, then he will also look a
more desirable character. Britain wants badly to
cement its alliance with Japan. British royalties
are many, and desirable alliances for them are not
always easy. And Joseph O. holds that the
ordinary working Jap is a civilised person whom
the ordinary working Australian has no cause to
regard otherwise than as a desirable mate and
brother. Let him consider how long his billet
would last, and how long he would be in a position
to find desirable jobs for his relatives, if he pro-
moted a marriage between a royal Jap and a royal
Briton. Let him propose it to Edward VII., and
then let them size-up their joint opinion about
Japanese civilisation.
m m 9 *
The Englishman has a profound scorn for
colored races, even when he is only an intruder
in the colored man’s country. He believes
in Parliamentary Government, but in every
colony or possession where the black population
is in the majority he has either so fixed
the franchise that Parliamentary power is
reserved to the white minority, or else got over
the difficulty by having no Parliamentary au-
thority at all. If the colored man started to
invade Britain on a large scale, and cut wages
there, and aggravate the people whose votes keep
Joseph Chamberlain in office, this scorn would
probably develop into active hatred. The Briton
is strong upon the right of the colored British
subject to travel within the empire wheresoever
he pleases; as that colored fellow-subject is de-
barred by the rigorous climate from swamping the
United Kingdom, that is just the sort of right
that the Briton would be likely to be strong upon.
The Australian, who is liable to be swamped,
doesn’t see where the black man’s right to travel
all over the empire comes in, but he is shocked to
some extent at the high-handed tyranny of
the Chamberlain gang in refusing the black
man the inalienable privilege of political freedom
and Responsible Government. Still, when
Chamberlain and co. explain that circumstances
at their end make it imperative to deprive nine-
tenths of the King’s subjects of political free-
dom, Australia is quite prepared to let it go at
that. Equally urgent circumstances at this end
make it imperative to deprive the black man of
his alleged right to free locomotion throughout the
Empire, but Chamberlain and co. refuse to see
it. A fair inference seems to be that Chamber-
lain and eo. are an aggregate hypocrite as well as
an aggregate perverter of the truth.
m m * m
It is impossible to have a large colored alien
population in the midst of a white population
without a half-caste population growing up
between the two. India proves that; would prove
it much more conclusively only the white popula-
tion isn’t large enough to be a very extensive
?arent to the Eurasian mongrel. Spanish and
ortuguese America show it. The United States
show it. Queensland shows it already to an alarm-
ing extent. And Australia thinks highly enough of
its British and Irish descent to feel a desire to
keep the race pure. This may be a mistake, of
course. It may be that the British race isn’t
worth keeping pure. Joseph Chamberlain evi-
dently thinks so, and certainly if all the British
race was like Joseph its preservation would be
very doubtful policy. When there were no re-
strictions upon the influx of Asiatic immigration
it flowed into Australia at a rate which, if it had
not been checked, would have monstrously
adulterated the scanty white population of
this country. Between 1881 and 1891 the half-
caste Chinese in Victoria increased by 500 per
cent., while the white Victorians increased by
32 per cent. But for the early restrictions put
on the Chinese influx, whereby there were not
enough Chinese fathers to go round, Victoria
would apparently be almost a wholly mon-
grel State by this time —and Victoria is in a
better position than the other mainland States.
A continent with only 4,000,000 white in-
habitants, and with 800,000,000 colored people
at its back door, is easily mongrelised,
unless it shuts that back door hard. Aus-
tralia doesn’t want to be mongrel. It values its
British descent a great deal more highly than does
Renegade Joe of Birmingham. And if, through
Britain’s ignorant and brutally-selfish course of
action, it ultimately loses its white status to a
great extent, then the Briton will be the first to
jeer at it for doing so, just as he jeers at the
Eurasian mongrel and the Levantine.
* * * *
And yet Australia doesn’t care a straw for mere
whiteness, and when Joseph Chamberlain says
that it objects to any race because of its color or
birthplace, then Joseph Chamberlain is lying.
The fact that Joseph Chamberlain is lying can’t
be too strongly impressed. What Australia val ues
is the fact that the white man leads the world in
almost everything. If Chamberlain can show a
black or brown race with higher civilisation, and
higher mental capacity, and a greater ability to
lead the world, than the white, then Australia
wants to import 10 millions of that race as soon as
possible. But there is no such race. The Japan-
ese is probably the nearest approach to it, but
even he doesn’t fill the bill. If Britain is shocked
at Australia’s desire to maintain its pure Euro-
pean descent, then Britain doesn’t know the value
of its own white status, and is only fit to be a
nigger, and a very poor kind of nigger at that.
The Examp’e of Ontario.
“ A good deal was being said about the reduction of
members of the Leg. Assembly, but instead of this he
would advocate the abolition of the Upper House as being
unnecessary and useless. If it was desired to save money,
let them abolish the Leg. Council as being altogether
superfluous. (Cheers.) The party would still work for
this principle regardless of what Government had the
credit of doing the work. Any Ministry could have the
honor and the glory.”— N.S. IF. Labor-leader M'Goioen.
To the above The Bulletin says Amen. Or,
rather, The Bulletin said these things first, long
before M‘Go wen or the Labor-party appeared in
N.S.W. politics, and M‘Gowen says Amen. Not
that it matters much who says Amen. Almost
every person and party in N.S.W. is agreed that,
since the labors and responsibilities of the State
Parliament have been so much reduced through
Federation, a less cumbersome legislative machine
should now serve the State’s purpose. And
when the cutting-down process begins it is obvi-
ously advisable to cut off the most useless thing
first. The Leg. Council of N.S.W. represents
nobody except itself and the dead-and-gone Mini-
stries by which it was appointed. Many of its mem-
bers were sent to where they still are that they might
support the views of Ministries of the ’Bo’s, the
’7o’s, even the ’GO’s—Ministries that are buried
personally as well as -politically. The N.S.W.
Council doesn’t even represent Property —it re-
presents tombstones, or places where tombstones
used to be. It is unpaid, though it costs a great
deal in free railway-passes and expenses. It is
seldom there; the Council sits very little,
and when it does sit niast of its members are
absent. When it is in a good mood it passes Bills
just as they come from the Assembly, which the
Y.M.C.A., or the Band of Hope, or the Mothers’
Sewing Meeting could do quite as effectively. It
requires no labor or intelligence to say “ ditto.”
When it is in a had mood it alters or rejects Bills,
and if the matter involved is one of any impor-
tance whatever, a howl goes up from the Assembly
about “ infringing the rights of the People’s re-
presentatives,” or “defying the Popular Cham-
ber,” or “resisting the expressed will of the
People.” The complaint always directed against
the Council is that it is not useful, and it can’t be
useful by agreeing to everything the Assembly
says and does, and when it doesn’t agree there is
Sheol to pay.
* • * •
Of course, when the Council disagrees it
generally disagrees the wrong way. It either
doesn’t interfere at all, when it is useless, or else
it interferes in the cause of Toryism, when it is
injurious. In other words, the complaint is that the
Council ought to be more liberal and progressive
than the Assembly, and it isn’t. But no sane
person could possibly expect these stationary old
men, who are responsible to nobody, and who doze
far from the madding crowd of influences which
help to keep the Assembly up to the mark, to be
the more progressive body of the two. If they
were, their active interference would still be re-
garded as an insult to the Popular Chamber and a
gross interference with the rights of the people.
The Council originates practically nothing—it
doesn’t get a chance to do so. Money Bills can’t
be first introduced in the Council, which knocks
off half its possible usefulness. Money Bills
can’t be altered there, so the Upper House,
if it did want to check the awful financial
vagaries of the Assembly, could only do so by
stopping Supply altogether, which would bring
down on its head a storm of rage such as a much
less ancient and infirm body could hardly be
expected to face. Very little even of the Govern-
ment’s formal business is originated there. The
few Bills the Council starts on its own account are
only small private measures of hardly any import-
ance. The Council has no voice in deciding by what
kind of Ministry the country shall he governed.
It is a House of Review, which isn’t allowed to
seriously review anything. It is kept in existence
mainly that it may, now and then, oppose the
Assembly, as an inmate of the Old Men’s Home
might oppose a large, healthy prize-fighter; so
that the Assembly may get a chance to talk
largely about the sacred rights of the people, and
show its valour and resolution and democratic
spirit by biffing the feeble old obstacle out of its
road. Yet if it never got in the road a rubber-
stamp would do as well, and the Council would
presently be told so with contempt. An unrepre-
sentative body of old men, who are jeered at for
being old and for being unrepresentative when
they can’t help either circumstance, and who are
not allowed to be useful and are scorned for being
useless—that is N.S.W.’a Legislative Council
nowadays.
* * * *
It is beneath the dignity of N.S.W. to keep
this helpless butt and Aunt Sally on its premises
any longer. Anybody who keeps an aged and in-
firm relative in his house to be a subject of
derision is regarded as something less than a man,
and that is exactly what N.S.W. does with its
Upper House. The Council might be made a
paid and representative body, with the same
power, and elected on the same liberal franchise, as
the Assembly, and only differing from it in the
fact that it would be chosen by the whole State
as one constituency, so that it would repre-
sent the State as a whole, in contra-distinction
to the other body which represents the State in
125 parts. Or, as the Federal Senate pretty well
fills that bill now, it might be better for it to be
abolished altogether. There is any amount of
British precedent for its abolition. The great
bulk of the provinces which make up the British
empire are run with only one legislative body
apiece. Where there are two, it is generally the
case that the Upper House is a body of nominees
preserved as a relic of the despotism of the old
Crown Colony days, in order to prevent the power
falling wholly into the hands of the people, who
might do something rash. The process is usually
one of evolution. First, there is one House of
Legislature nominated, or mostly nominated, by
the Crown. Then there is reluctantly added an
elective House,-the old nominee Chamber being
preserved to sit on its head and keep it in subjec-
tion. Finally, there is the stage when the nomi-
nee Chamber wears out and becomes useless
for its evil purpose, and a mere object of
contempt, and by that time its abolition is
due. Five- of the seven provinces of Canada
(New Brunswick, Ontario, Manitoba, British
Columbia, and Prince Edward Island) with a
total population of over 3,000,000, get along
peacefully enough with an elected Assembly
apiece, and no Upper Houses whatever. The
great North-West Territories of Canada also
struggle on with only an Assembly, and there the
people even elect the Government. Ontario, the
doss province of Canada, has 222,000 square miles
and (in 1891) 2,114,321 people, against N.S.W.’s
310,700 square miles and (in 1891) 1,132,234
people ; yet though Ontario has nearly twice the
population of N.S.W. and a very large area, its
single chamber of 94 members answers all require-
ments. It answers so well that Ontario has
actually no State debt whatever, being almost the
only section of the British Empire so situated.
The Bulletin has an idea that this big Canadian
province is a reasonably good precedent for
N.S.W. (with its two Houses and its 184 legisla-
tors) to follow. Canada and Australia are the only
two Federations in the British Empire. Canada s
subordinate States are the only ones in the British
Empire which can serve as a precedent for the
subordinate States of this Commonwealth. And
when five out of the seven have adopted the one-
chamber system there is an overwhelming pre-
ponderance of precedent in that system’s favor.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
These Billets.
After reading the extensive explanations of
Sydney Telegraph re all the appointments which
the Lyne Government bestowed upon people con-
nected with it, the situation appears to be this :
The Telegraph, after violently opposing Lyne and Co.
for many years, started to support them rather cordially
on the ground that they had agreed to let the fiscal issue
slide till the Federal Parliament took over the tariff ques-
tion, therefore their fiscal principles didn’t matter.
The Lyne Government simply rained valuable public
billets and concessions on people connected with the
Telegraph.
The Telegraph did not support the Lyne Government
for the sake of these billets and profits.
The Lyne Government didn’t give the billets in pay-
ment for past support, and as a corrupt inducement
towards future support.
It was all an-unfortunate and beastly coincidence.
The See Government is composed almost wholly of the
same materials as the Lyne Government, and on the fiscal
issue has exactly the same claims to the Tele-
graph’s support. In fact, it has even greater claims, for
the fiscal issue is more completely dead in State politics
now than it was in Lyne’s day. The See Government
doesn’t appear to have given or offered the Telegraph
crowd any billets worth mentioning.
The Telegraph is frantically hostile to the See Govern-
ment and demands that it be wiped out—principally
because its members are mostly Protectionist, just as they
and Lyne were in the days when the Telegraph sup-
-1 orted them.
Tiiis attitude has nothing at all to do with the question
of Government billets either.
And now the Government has no cause to complain, so
far as the billets were concerned, for if all these valuable
concessions were not given to the Telegraph people for
the corrupt purpose of buying that paper’s support it
can’t grumble when the article which wasn’t bought is
not delivered.
And it is difficult to see that the Telegraph has any
cause of complaint either. When it practically ceased its
hostility towaius an enemy of such long standing as Lyne,
and Lyne started to bestow billets on its people with such
Tins Waggon Government.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ The Boer Government now consists of Mr. S. Burger,
the “Acting” President, General L. Meyer and Mr.
Smuts, the ex-Staats Attorney, and Mr. F. W. Reitz, ex-
State Secretary of the Transvaal. The members of this
so-called Ministry are reduced, since at no time in the
Transvaal can it remain for a consecutive fortnight in
safety, to trekking about the veldt. The whole outfit of
the ‘Government’ consists of five waggons and a Cape
cart.”— Cablegram, daily paper, 10th June, 1901.
Lo, the “ Ministers ” of Britain, in the merry month of
June,
Sitting in upholstered chambers, comfortably, to com-
mune 1
And the theme that claims attention (Jingoes well will
understand)
Is the Glory of the Empire (and the ruling of the Rand—
Is the burning-off of homesteads and the ruling of the
Rand).
But from out the lanes of England—ringing sweetly with
the notes
Rushing, gushing forth in gladness from the thrushes’
mellow throats—
Comes a moan of maid and mother, comes the Nation’s
mournful cry
With the wailing of her widows for the men who fought
to die.
Do the statesmen hear the moaning ? And the mourning
—do they see ?
Ah, the walls of State are padded with a wealth of luxury !
For they fill up “To The Empire,” to the brim they fill
the cup;—
Millions more for war are wanted—we must put the taxes
up;
We must show the mighty powers with the weight behind
our guns
How the people’s sons take vengeance for the people’s
butcher’d sons;
How the Britisher for freedom with the God of Battle
quests
While he fights for King and Country (and for Vested
Interests).
So they fill up to “ The Empire ” —they are Englishmen
and brave—
Fill and drink and shout for glory while the soldier fills—
the grave.
But the statesmen of the Transvaal ! Have they gilded
halls of State ?
Yea !—a caravan of waggons in a country desolate.
But they fly the flag of Freedom, though their strengtn be
almost spent,
As they trek across the kopjes with their “ Waggon Gov-
ernment.”
’Tis not theirs to shout for “ Empire ” ; ’tis not theirs to
toast a king ;
’Tis not theirs from sweated workers taxes for a war to
wring ;
Only theirs—the four undaunted—onward now to point
the way,
With their rifles for their warrant and their freedom for
their pay.
Past the graves of heroes fallen—hark the women’s woe-
ful wail!
Past the burnt and blackened homestead, staring from
each veldt and vale.
Answering the stern invader with the Mauser ringing
true—
They are trekking into hist’ry as “ The Ever-Dauntless
Few.”
For the cry from St. Helena and from Ceylon’s prison
shore,
And the thinned commandos fighting as they never fought
before,
Give them hopes of other nations—other nations white
/ and free—
Stretching out a hand to help them to a newer Liberty.
No, not theirs the cushioned chamber, and not theirs to
toast a king ;
Never theirs from sweated workers taxes for a war to
wring;
Only theirs the hope of Freedom, though their strength be
almost spent,
As they trek across the kopjes with their “ Waggon Gov-
ernment.”

Australia’s “ City Beautiful.”
“ Wurrym ” offers a design for a Federal capital:
The Oit.v Beautiful—that’s not necessarily my name,
but it’s my idea for the Federal capital. For fourteen
years I have been dreaming it, planning it, and tearing
up the plans. I submit it now to you (quantity of com-
plimentary remarks struck out here. —Ed. B.), in the hope
that, if there is one reasonable suggestion among much
husks, you will impress that suggestion on those who
are going to build City Beautiful.
Our City must be builded on many little hills. It can-
not be entirely beautiful if it be not. So much being
granted, the man who designs it must be one of
broad ideas, an artist capable of thinking broad effects,
not a niggler. The thoroughfares must be long and wide,
say two chains between kerbs, with sidewalks 15ft., or
even 20ft., in breadth, for we are going to build a city for
all time. Midway in the street, at intervals, shall be
islands for foot passengers. It is on the islands that the
electric-light poles will be set. The streets will be at right
angles, so that the city will be cut up into a series of squares.
Now only alternative squares will be leased for building
purposes, so that every row of residences will face an
open square at least an acre in extent, for the intersec-
tions of streets will be 10 chains apart. There will be 24
residence-sites in each block, for no residence shall be
built on the allotment of the land-shark, where health and
common decency are not. The building blocks in our
city shall be large and roomy, 300 ft. x 55ft., therefore he
who builds may, nay must, not approach his neighbor by
nearer than 15ft. The cheap suburban terrace shall have
no place in our city, and the semi-detached cottage shall
be a thing to be utterly abhorred. So it shall not be
possible for you to be awakened by the snore of your
neighbor, for if the neighbor shall have more than a 15ft.
snore then he shall he outlawed. The residence blocks
will be intersected by a road one chain wide, to give
access to the back premises or the houses, but no road
shall intersect the garden blocks.
The business-sites need only have a frontage of 22ft.,
but shall be not less than 84ft. in depth. This will be
arrived at by running two intersecting streets one chain
wide across the block. The Houres of Legislature must
occupy an eminence in the centre of the city. Near them
will be the markets. Close at hand, also, will be the law
courts and the offices of government, with the city lying
all around to the outskirts, where the sewage farm, and
the incinerator, and the crematorium, and the bonemills,
an 1 tanneries will be on the one side, and the boiler fac-
tories, and foundries, and sawmills on the other. At one
boundary of the city the makers of stinks, and at the
other the makers of noises.
The government of the city shall he in the hands of its
citizens—subject, of course, to the supreme control of the
Federal Government. To begin with, the residents
around each square —I adopt the English term for a
vacant space surrounded with houses—shall be, by virtue
of their tenancy from the State, members of a guild which
shall conduct the management of the square of which
they are the guild. They shall elect one of their number
as representative to the council of the ward, or collection
of, say, 10, 20, any number of guilds. Each ward-council,
of which there shall be eight, shall elect from among
themselves a member of the council of aldermen, but the
citizens alone can elect a mayor. It shall be competent for
any 25 citizens, three at least from each ward, to demand
a referendum on any subject, so that if an alderman be
considered unfit for the position after attaining it, he
may, by a majority of one, at a referendum of not less
than three-fifths of the citizens, be cast out of office.
There, I’ve set down things that may look like dreams,
but we Australians do want a City Beautiful and a City
Healthy.
A Slump in the contingent Business.
The Jingo business is not what it was in Aus-
tralia. Listen to this horribly muddled oration
by ex-Minister “Joey” Carruthers, of N.S.W.
(Sydney D.T. report)
But the Government in recent times has shown a disre-
gard of true public opinion. So far as the sending of
contingents away is concerned, a great public service has
been served. . . . As a mere advertisement, if for nothing
more, it has done incalculable benefit to N. S. Wales and
the rest of Australia. Contingent after contingent was
sent away— we even sent one to China, God knows why—
but how different the going away and the coming home !
(Loud cheers.) When these men went away they were
told that those whom they left behind should be well
looked after and made the care of the people, but we find
to-day that notone but scores and even hundreds have come
back,'lost their billets, and are trudging about the streets
of Sydney trying vainly to get work—(cries of “ Shame”)
—and vainly pointing out that they went to fight the
battles of the Empire and to represent Australia. They
are almost denied admittance to the public oDices. (Loud
cheers.) I say that any Government, if it rightly inter-
prets the wishedof the people, cannot have all the gain
and glory of the movement without being prepared to
fulfil the obligations. The Government has had all the
honor, and it cannot afford to neglect the men whom it
has used for the purpose. No matter if they be the veriest
■ragamuffins, they have done good service for us, and we
certainly are indebted to do a little more than the mere
3s. or 4s. per day which we have to pay them. (Cheers.)
Kipling’s lines—“ 1 It’s Tommy here, and Tommy there,’
and ‘ chuck ’im out, the brute ’; but it’s ‘ saviour o’ his
country ’ when the guns begin to shoot ” —are particularly
applicable just now. I say that this Government, having
got all the honor and glory and continuance in office by
reason of these men, they should keep their promises to
these men, and keep, too, the public to their promises.
(Loud cheers.) I do not advocate that there should
be any lavish expenditure or waste, but I would
recommend the Government, even at this late hour,
to appoint some board of responsible and reputable
men, to take in hand the cases of those soldiers, and see
what can be done to help them into positions, and thus
save them becoming loafers on the State. (Cheers.) We
subscribed our money to a patriotic fund ; but we know
what difficulty we had in getting enough money to keep,
in this electorate, the wife and family of a mandat the
front whose draft had gone astray. (“ Hear, hear.”) The
men who risked their lives, and the lives of their children,
in our behalf, having soundly and well advertised Aus-
tralia—to put it even on the lowest basis—having helped
to cement the Empire—let them find that their country
recognises their services, and keeps faith to the letter.
(Loud cheers.)
Now, just let ex-Minister Joseph Carruthers
(who aspires to the N.S.W. Premiership) come
outside and look at himself. First he says that
N.S.W. has gained incalculable benefit by the ad-
vertisement its contingents have given it. How
did it get the benefit ?—not in increased commerce,
nor in increased manufactures, nor in enlarged
agricultural production, nor in lower-priced loans,
nor in enlarged population; the contingents
didn’t help in any of these matters. They cost a
huge lot of money, killed or crippled many valu-
able men—and the visible result is one or two
titles, and the broad hint by Joe Chamberlain
that this country must cease to exclude the cheap-
labor Jap because Britain wants to keep on good
terms with Jap-land. Where is the incalculable
benefit that the blatant Carruthers is talking
about ? Then Carruthers sneers at the latest con-
tingent—the Chinese one —and says : Hod only
knows why it was sent at all.” Great Scott.
Wasn’t it sent to gather in the incalculable
benefits that Carruthers was just talking about t
He must be taken to suggest roundaboutly that
the contingenters are the “ veriest ragamuttins
which is a coarse and brutal and hideously un-
grateful way to speak about the men who brought
back these incalculable benefits. He says that
the Government has shown a disregard of public
opinion in not finding billets for the returned con-
tingenters, and if it “rightly interprets the
wishes of the people ” it will find them billets.
Also he says that public opinion cares so little
about the matter that he could hardly raise
enough money in his own electorate to support
the wife and family of one soldier, and he wants
the Government to “keep the public to their
promises” in the matter. If the public are to
keep the Government up to the mark and the Go-
vernment is to keep the public up to the mark, then
who on earth is to keep both of them up to the mark.
And Joseph says that the contingent business
has been of “incalculable benefit to N.S.W. He
also says the Government has had all the gam
and glory of the movement,” and these state-
ments can’t both be true. Also it seems that the
noble contingenters are people who will become
“ loafers on the State ” unless the State finds
billets for them, The State has already found
them a steady job for 12 months or so at about
twice the pay they were earning in the backblocks
these hard times. And other men, who haven t
had a 12 months’ steady job, have to hustle for
billets themselves. Does Carruthers mean that
the contingenters are such a helpless lot,
and so utterly lost to honest industry,
that they can’t find jobs for themselves in the
open market as ordinary men have to do, espe-
cially in a State which is prospering so gloriously
under the advantages of Freetrade ? If they are
only as good as average men (and they are said to
be the pick of N.S.W.) they have quite as good a
chance as other men, unless public opinion hates
them so utterly that people won’t employ them at
any price. And in that case, what is Carruthers
talking about when he says the Government’s
neglect of them shows “ a disregard of true public
opinion ?” In fact, what is J. Carruthers talk-
ing about in any case. There appears to be a
dreadful slump in the contingent business some-
where.

Concerning' “ Tattersall.”
The Federal Government is asking for power in
the Post and Telegraph Bill to suppress Tatter-
sall by the simple process of stopping delivery of
his letters, and Neil Lewis, C.M.G., as Premier
of the circular spot with dampness around it
whereon Tattersall at present has his local habi-
tation and his gamble, denounces this as an un-
constitutional Federal interference with State
rights. In this matter Lewis hasn’t the ghost of
a case. Posts and Telegraphs are wholly a Federal
affair, and the authority which owns the Postal
Department always has the right to refuse to
carry objectionable matter within its dominions.
Also it has the sole right to decide what is objec-
tionable matter. But while the Federal Govern-
ment is absolutely within its rights in demanding
powers to stop Tattehsall’s correspondence
(which is the same thing as stopping Tattersall)
there will be grave doubts as to whether it is
absolutely in its senses if it uses these powers.
It can certainly make Tattersall move on.
N.S.W. suppressed him, and thereby lost a con-
siderable amount of postal revenue, and he simply
moved on, and N.S. W. gambled as much as ever,
sending its money to Tattersall’s new
address. Queensland suppressed him, and lost
much postal revenue, and didn’t suppress local
gambling worth a cent ; lie moved on again, and
local money went into his sweeps just the same.
If the whole Commonwealth suppresses him he
will doubtless move on to New Caledonia, or some
such place ; and the Commonwealth will lose
much postal revenue, and it won’t stop the vice of
gambling in the least. Unless the Commonwealth
takes power to stop all correspondence addressed
to Tattersall, or any other person in his line
of business, anywhere, it is only snuffling and
pouring water into a belltopper without any
crown. And if it stops letters addressed to
a respectable resident of a French or Ger-
man colony, carrying on a legal business
there by arrangement with the local Govern-
ment,, then there is always a possibility of trouble.
Yet the Federal Government must take the risk
of doing this ; or else let Tattersall remain
where he is; or else wipe him out by establishing
in his place a Government Tattersall so as to
make as much profit as possible out of the
gamble ; or else it must write itself down as a mud-
dling and inefficient Government that throws
away good revenue merely to allay the inward
shrieks of an ill-regulated conscience.

More South Foie.
Ed. Bulletin. —The Northern Pole of Thought
should find its opposite, its complement, in the
South. What is the good of the Equator if it does
not help us to find our own equation ? Are we
going to be a decimal to Europe ? or are we going
to get in front of the dot and have an integral
value of our own ? Let us be Ourselves. Any fool
could be someone else, made to order by the
mental tailors of the colleges. The old world is
already hard at work telling us what to do and
how to do it. Our elder sister, America, looks at
us superciliously (when she deigns to look at all)
measuring us by her everlasting dollar standard.
Our type is different, and should be made to differ
vigorously. Protectionists of local knowledge,
we should levy prohibitive sarcasm on the dear
elder worlds who would give us good advice from
their stores of prejudice. The ocean puts things
in proper perspective. The isolation of Australia
tends to individuality, aided by the decrease of
immigration since the superficial riches of
alluvial goldfields and early territorial con-
cessions have given place to the era of the
multitude with the limited income. It is this
multitude which has to cook a new joint or
compound a tough hash of the old materials.
We begin our National life with Upper and
Lower Houses both elected by the same manhood
suffrage. In this there is a world of hope, if only
the National mind will also start its estimates
from a manhood standard. We have found it ex-
pedient to poll-tax the Chinaman, and to quaran-
tine plaguey persons. A poll-tax, or at any rate,
a popular quarantine, should be inflicted upon
persons suffering from hereditary disease in their
opinions—the supercilious person, and (when she
can be identified) Mrs. Grundy, who, worse than
many Chinamen, demoralises the Silly Woman,
who is the Fetter of Nationality just as the sensi-
ble woman is the Crown. The supercilious Briton
is mostly of the Norman habit of mind—a narrow
and obstinate habit, mediaeval in the worse sense.
The social anomalies of the supercilious and the
obsequious must be got rid of. The infection of
this style of thought is great, as we see by the
modern Beerage. Our own wealthy class would
soon receive it if their tenure of glory were not
evanescent. The presence of numbers of un-
doubtedly vulgar people in high places here has
done much to preserve us from grovel, but it is
high time that the declaration of the social
independence of this new democracy should be
affirmed by people of intellect and cultivation.
The National style should be founded upon the
real Australian unconventional, easy-going, ready-
witted, reticent type, cultivated free of humbug-
weeds. Such was the manner of the Greeks in
their prime. It is a manner utterly opposed to
the Philistine or bourgeois frames of mind. The
Puritans took these to America, but Australia
was founded rather by the Robin Hood type, the
historic ancestor of the Gumsucker. Ideals are
much. If we can only get the National idea pro-
perly Australised in time to prevent our most
prominent classes from becoming Philistine or
Shoddily European, then we can keep expelled
the old chimera of aristocracy, plutocracy and
precedent, as we do the small-pox. Yes, to be
truly ourselves we must locate the Southern Pole
of thought, and maintain a friendly polarity
cheerfully opposed to the traditions of authority
beyond the Equator. So shall we best balance
the world and benefit the race.— Point Blank.

An agricultural man writes about a leakage: —
If the N.S.W. Lands Minister will direct his officers to
prepare a return covering the improvement leases disposed
of during the past five years, and will devote an hour to
an analysis of that return, he will come to some useful
conclusions. He will find that the vast majority of these
leases —probably four-fifths—have been bought by the
runhclders at the upset price—that is, without competi-
tion, and that the remainder have brought anything from
twice to five times the upset price. These latter will be
found to be mostly situated in the older settled districts
where the original runs are divided into numerous small
estates, and where small capitalists are plentiful. The
farmer will be found to be in districts where the land is
divided between the selector and big squatter.
One of the unwritten laws of the bush is that no squatter
shall encroach upon his neighbor’s run, therefore the
reason for this lack of competition becomes obvious. The
return will also show isolated leases even in the thinly-
peopled districts which have brought rentals double,
treble, or quadruple the upset price. These cases, where
the intrinsic value has been reached, are accounted for
by an outsider “ chipping in,” or by “ arrangements ”
having fallen through and someone “ turning dog” on his
neighbors. If the Minister will make judicious outside
inquiries he will hear of smart men who pocket
a frequent £5O or £lOO for simply being absent
when the leases are offered. It is to be re-
membered that there is no question of settlement
involved in this matter. The Lands department assumes
open competition by offering these leases to the highest
bidder ; the only consideration is one of revenue. Judged
by results, the present system is the worst possible one,
as the State gets far below the intrinsic value of the land,
and the public is demoralised h.v the intrigue, chicanery,
and blackmail the system developes. One line of improve-
ment would be to let by- tender. Many a small settler or
outsider would compete by secret tender, but though often
prepared to give twice or thrice the upset price, it
is now useless for him to inspect one of these leases, for if
he opposes the squatter at auction he can count on cer-
tain defeat. The run-holder with adjoining country,
water, and plant can afford more than the small settler
every time —when he is run to it. Knowing this, the out-
sider does not bid, as he only gets himself disliked for his
trouble, and thus the squatter isn’t rushed. The tender
system would largely block “knock-outs” and discount
“ arrangements,” but the auction is too heavily loaded
against the State. Further, the Minister might look to
his appraisers, for whilst inferior country is occasionally
ov-'f.valued better country is often scandalously under-
valued.
Sydney Daily Telegraph is furious at the tacit
understanding between the See and Labor parties
concerning the impending State election, and
loudly condemns “the unholy alliance.” Some
three years ago, when the support of over 20
Labor members alone saved the Reid Government
for months, the Telegraph was enthusiastic at
the “splendid assistance” afforded by the party,
and as a reward demanded that the “ Freetrade-
and-Liberal” executive should not oppose its
re-election. Another instance of the foolish posi-
tion the Telegraph has landed itself into by its
chopping and changing policy of the past few
years is its patronisingly-forgiving attitude
towards those members of the political Stiletto
Brigade (Haynes, Fitzpatrick, Bawler Clarke
and the like) who deserted Reid for Lyne in 1899.
The Telegraph has re-admitted these members
on condition that they atone for their disloyalty
hereafter by strict party allegiance. When it is
remembered that the Telegraph “ratted” simul-
taneously with the Stiletto Brigade, and did more
to kill the Reid Government outside than the
defaulters did inside the House, the brazen impu-
dence of its forgiving airs seerps about a record.

Styles, of Vic., gave the Senate an interesting
little history of how patent-swindles are worked
by Australian importers. He once bought an
engine, branded “patent” on every part, and
paid a price about 100 per cent, over its intrinsic
value, in consequence, apparently, of the protec-
tion afforded to the poor inventor. One small
part breaking, Styles tried to get it substituted
by local manufacturers, but one and all shied off,
fearing prosecution for a breach of the patent
laws. The importer at last agreed, for an extor-
tionate figure, to replace the broken bit, and
Styles had to wait the better part of a year to
get it. Subsequently he perceived a method by
which the machine might be improved, and, pre-
paring drawings, took them to the Patents’ Office
to protect his invention. A search was made for
the original patent, and it was found to be non-
existent. The big importing firm and the English
manufacturing firm had made huge profits out of
a simple game of bluff—charging fancy prices for
articles that would otherwise have been made in
Victoria for about half the price. Styles asked
that the patents which cover reapers and binders
and cream separators should be investigated, as he
had received an offer from T. Robinson and Co.,
of Melbourne, to make, for £2O each, 500 of the
former machines, exactly similar to those which
come into Victoria free of duty, and are retailed
by the blessed importing ring at £5O apiece. He
might have gone a little further without breaking
anything, and asked that the people with the
bogus machine-patent above alluded to should be
gaoled for getting money by false pretences.
♦
The Freetrade cry, that “ Protection makes mil-
lionaires,” is an absurdly premature yelp to raise
in Australia at present. Thus far not one pro-
tected manufacturer, outside the liquor-trade, in
any of the States is under suspicion of being half
a millionaire. The rich men, bar the brewers, all
have made their money from land—grazing land,
mining land, or city land, the wealth in the latter
case being created for them by city circumstances
over which they had no control. The strongest
indirect argument in favor of Protection is the
fact that Australia’s most prosperous producers of
manufactured goods are small capitalists by com-
parison with the importing middlemen who
mostly spend their profits in England. When
monopolistic boils and tumors begin to disfigure
the full-blooded bodies of local protected manu-
factures it will be time enough to deal with the
evil. Meanwhile the fat importers are the men
the Commonwealth needs to watch.

“ G.S.” writes from Perth (W.A.); —
In view of the Plain English par. in 8., May 18, isn’t it
time to impress on the Minister for Defence the necessity
for establishing, at as early a date as possible, Australian
small-arms and ammunition factories ? It is no use form-
ing rifle-clubs if the members have to content themselves
with shooting at bottles with shanghais or throwing
road-metal at fowls. In conversation with John Forrest,
some time ago, I broached the subject, and Forrest
went somewhat thoroughly into it, in an appreciative
manner. John’s own State, however, declines to recognise
rifle-clubs, the Panjandrum of the local forces, Major
Campbell, insisting on rifle corps, the members of which
perform drill, learn how to present arms, and go through
all the flummery that the average old swaddy adores.
Campbell belonged to some British regiment of
mud-crushers, and his ideas don’t rise above a
parade - ground. Meantime, there are dozens of
men ready to form themselves into clubs so that
they may learn to shoot straight. But even if they
could get the necessary authority, the lack of arms and
ammunition blocks the way, for most of the State arms
are lying in the stores at Capetown, or on the veldt.
Look at Queensland’s clubs and say why can’t the other
States have similar ones. In the light of late cables, it is
plain that it would be rank folly for Australia to rely on
the Imperial authorities, for ammunition at any rate. It
transpires that recently, at a most critical moment,
Britain’s own supplies almost wholly ran out. Nor do we
want to be compelled to draw our supplies from- British
manufacturers, who furnish goods “Made expressly .for
Australia” for by now we have got to learn just what
that means. Let us have our own State-owned factories
so that we may get supplies at a slight advance on cost
price, and at the same time have a standard of quality
and pattern thoughout the Commonwealth.

N.S.W. would-be Premier Lee on the subject of
borrowing for Temporary and Unproductive
Works: —
Mr. O’Sullivan made a pleasant little speech, in the
course of which he said that, during the past year, no
less than 499 works of a reproductive character had either
been completed or were approaching completion. I had
the curiosity to look into this, and I found that the list
contained 103 bridges THIS IS THE FIRST TIME 1
HEARD OF BRIDGES BEING REPRODUCTIVE. It
also contained over 100 harbor works. THIS IS THE
FIRST TIME I HEARD OF HARBOR WORKS BEING
CALLED PRODUCTIVE.— DaiIy Telegraph report, 17th
May, 1901.
Lee is either a man with a very short memory, or
a very deficient intellect, or a very inferior regard
for the truth, for the Reid Government, which he
consistently supported, and of which he was
ultimately appointed to be the tail in order that
he might keep the flies off the hindquarters of the
animal, put scores of bridges and harbor works
among the “permanent and reproductive” works
charged to loan funds. But that may pass. The
present matter is: Lee says that bridges and
harbor works (and he might add roads, gaols,
court-houses, lunatic asylums, benevolent asylums,
museums, public libraries, repairs to Government
House, &c.) are not reproductive works. If he
becomes Premier, and borrows money for these
things as permanent and reproductive works,
then he will be a fraud, a political swindler,
and a sham and impostor. Still he doesn’t
definitely promise that he won’t be that kind
of a fraud, and The Bulletin is waiting for
that definite promise. When it comes this paper
is with the Lee crowd heart and soul.

One bitter complaint that goes up from the re-
sumed area of Sydney is to the effect that the
Government has taken over properties which
were returning, in some cases, 10 per cent, on
their value, and is only allowing 4 per cent, in-
terest on the unpaid purchase-money. But after
all, the grievance isn’t so woeful as it looks. . Ten
per cent., less taxes, repairs, losses by occasional
lack of tenants or by defaulting tenants, and less
an allowance for the steady wearing-out of the
buildings, isn’t much different from a continuous
4 per cent, which doesn’t wear out and is subject
to no deductions. In ordinary cases, if a little
more haste was shown in paying the 4 per cent.,
there would be no genuine ground of complaint.
The persons who have a real grievance are those
whose properties were mortgaged at rates varying
from sto 8 per cent. If the State had taken
over the mortgages along with the properties, and
paid them off, and allowed the ex-owner 4 per
cent, on the balance of the purchase money, there
would have been little reason for grumbling. But
where the owner is getting 4 per cent, (or doesn’t
get it but has 4 per cent, owed to him), and his
creditor is demanding 7 or 8 per cent, cash, with
extra penalties for non-payment, the situation
is a hard one, and a disgrace to the slow,
muddling, incompetent Government which
brought it about.

Sydney Telegraph recently made the well-worn
observation that wealth was leaving Maoriland
under the influence of socialistic legislation, and
instanced the withdrawal, or partial withdrawal,
of those two big English money-lending institu-
tions —the N.Z. Trust and Loan Co. and the N.Z.
and River Plate Co. These seem to be rather
two most gratifying cases of debt leaving the
country. When a million is invested in Maori-
land by Smith, who habitually lives in Maoriland
and spends the interest there, that is Wealth. When
a million is invested in Maoriland by Smith, who
habitually lives in London and spends the interest
there, that is Debt. The difference is that, in the
second case, Maoriland has to send away (say)
£60,000 a year interest; in the first case, it has to
send away nothing. If the first Smith goes away
with his million that is a Misfortune; it leaves
the countrva million poorer. If the second Smith
takes away his million and he isn’t replaced by any
like individual that is a Blessing; the country owes
a million less. The reasons why this country wants
a very big absentee-tax are: (i) to discourage the
second land of Smith ; (2) to prevent the first and
desirable kind of Smith transforming himself into
the second and undesirable kind of Smith ; (3) to
lighten the tax burden on the first Smith at the
expense of the second Smith, the first Smith
being more deserving of encouragement; and (4)
to make it worth the while of the second Smith,
whose investment is simply debt, to transform
himself into the first Smith, whose investment
represents wealth. The Smith question now sits
down to take a well-earned rest.
