Scrap from a recent S.M. Herald leader re N.S.
Wales State elections:
The decision turns upon the fiscal issue. We are no
longer called upon to settle the question of Freetrade or
Protection in Che State Legislature, but we are certainly
called upon to remember that N.S.W. is the leading Free-
trade State within the Commonwealth, and that it would
be hardly worthy of us to say one thing in the Federal
Parliament by a majority of our Senators and Representa-
tives, and something entirely different by the fiscal faith
of the Government in the State Legislature. That con-
sideration defines the issue we have to fight out in the
com ng campaign.
Exactly! The facts that N.S.W. has about
£6,000,000 of accumulated deficits of one kind and
another, that its revenue is fully £1,000,000 a year
short of its legitimate expenditure, that there is
another £400,000 a year to find for Old-Age

urgently wanted, also a new State Constitution, a
Greater Sydney scheme, a Closer Settlement
policy, a solution of the huge Unemployed diffi-
culty, and a few other trifles, don’t matter in the
least. The one purpose of N.S.W. Assembly is
to be a big ad vt. for M‘Millan’s imported calico.

Any man with a heart in him must have been moved by
the sight he witnessed in Washington when Coxey’s army

policy.
Mr. Sydney Smith : It is no use the hon. member talk-

knows very well that America has had Protection for
years, and in ISS3 Cleveland was not in power. The
condition of affairs which I have described was brought
about by Protection (Hansard, page 721).
Now, as it happened, Coxey’s army of unem-
ployed entered Washington in 1894—not in 1882
—and Cleveland, the Freetrader, was President
of the U.S. then, and it was under Cleveland’s
brief and disastrous attempt at Freetrade, or
rather Revenue T.tviffism, that Coxky gathered his
workless armyand made his famous demonstration.

Athknjkum Hall Charles Bertram,
Hawtrey’s newest production at Sydney
Palace, “In the Soup,” takes its name from
a genuine tureen supposed to be filled with
the substance above mentioned, and the thing
that is put in it is Mr. Gillibrand’s sleeping
draught. Mrs. Gillibrand inserts it in order that
three painfully complicated persons may go to
sleep, and thereby give her time to think. When
these things occur Mr. and Mrs. Gillibrand are
professing to be out, and are passing themselves
off as their own servants—who have left indig-
nantly through inability to collect their wages
The newly-married Gillibrand is in the difficult
position of having an insufficient income which he
forfeits if he marries before the age of 30, in which
case the money goes to his long-absent uncle, the
angry Indian Judge. Having great need to re-
trench, they decide to let their Hat, and do it
successfully—Mrs. Gillibrand lets it to the Indian
Judge without knowing him, and Mr. Gillibrand
lets it to the Frenchman who is married to the
woman that the Judge used to love. It is at this
st a ge that the Gillibrands hurriedly disguise
themselves in the clothes of their own
servants, who have just departed with much
opprobrium, state that they are not at homo
and proceed to wait lamentably at table, and leave
all the people who have taken the flat to sort
things out. And if Mrs. G. didn’t put the sleep-
ing draught in the soup, and her husband didn’t
swallow part of the soup and fall asleep standing
with a plate and a sardine in his hand, and the
frenchman didn’t come in to find his wife slum-
bering at the dinner table with her head on the
shoulder of a strange Judge and his daughter and
a man-servant and a plate with a sardine on it all
unconscious together opposite them, and if he
didn’t demand a divorce and insist on the Judge
being co-respondent, and the Judge didn’t by
mistake hire his nephew to defend the case and
give him £5OO to bribe himself with, things might
have been different.
**• £ .
“ Soup ” is not a first-class comedy, but it
has one excellent act, and one good act and
another act. The author gets away from the old
stock joke of concealing people in boxes and back
rooms, and his complications have a certain
novelty about them. r lhe eight leading characters
range from good to excellent. Those who may be
set down as excellent are Hawtrey, Miss Ruby
Ray, H. Stephenson, Herbert Ross, and Miss
Nellie Mortyne, though the latter has only a small
Kas the inflamed and unpaid cook. Misses
ge Hope and Winifred Austin and Frank
Peachey (as Miss Mortyne’s unpaid husband) also
deserve to have kindly things said of them. The
all-round goodness of the co. and the excellence of
the stage management make “Soup” go for a
S;ood deal more than it is worth, especially in the
ast act, where it tails off considerably and might
be called Ox-Tail Soup.
* • * *
A one-act drama, “ For Charity’s Sake,”
has so many good points about it that it forms’ a
valuable addition to the Palace bill. Miss Roxy
Barton, who has improved wonderfully since
Sydney first began to know her, is a pretty and
pleasing heroine in a back room behind a vege-
table shop; and Miss Winifred Austin appears
to advantage as a small boy who is somewhat
reminiscent of the boy who hadn’t the cough
in “ Two Little Vagabonds.” It is clearly
proved at the end that Hawtrey, as the oily
city missionary in the black suit with the snail-
tracks on it, was the person who stole the money,
and Miss Barton is cleared of all suspicion!
Hawtrey’s city missionary is an excellent work of
art in a brief tail coat, and Percy Walshe supplies
a good rendering of Nubbles, the kind-hearted
greengrocer, who lives by having money owed to
nim for coals by indigent neighbours. There was
on Saturday a tendency to confusion, gabbling,
and inaudibleness about the young man who mar-
ries Miss Barton after it is proved she didn’t
steal Nubbles’ money, but possibly he may im-
prove. If he doesn’t this paper will be driven by
a stern sense of duty to speak to him in print
about it.
** # *
Somebody is alleged to have said that you
“smiled at the ‘Private Secretary,’ laughed at
‘Charley’s Aunt,’ and positively roared at
‘Nurse.’” This scribe pleads guilty to the first
two charges v but is innocent of the latter indict-
ment. “Nurse,” which appeared at Sydney
Royal last Saturday, is a comedy of especially
good dialogue and situations, and equally
especially the reverse, the close of the first act (the
play boasts only two) being without any strength
whatever. There is a good deal of irrelevant
cleverness in it, and a number of loose incidents
that could be worked up to better advantage.
The main idea—the Family 'Erald motive of the
two young people refusing to love each other un-
seen, and then doing so violently when meeting
each other as two other people—is left very much
as it is ; and the other motive—of the robust
invalid seriously ill of nothing whatever—is also
only made humorous here and there. There is a
lady with two admirers, and a husband with a
gun and a tendency to descend into the hero’s
apartment by the roof. There are also some
male friend, of the hero who do nothing
particular, and a writ-server who goes into an ice-
chest for no great reason, and remains tfiere
t irougliout the play til] the audience has com-
pietely forgotten him. “ Nurse ’* is a corned y that
may work up better as the nights progress At
present, it is a play witli spotf of unusual
brightness and also some other spots of
no doubt wholesome and disciplinary dullness’
Carden Wilson is excellent as the writ-server, and
Henry Ludlow, who appears to he a young actor
love 1 to y «r USlng , a ? the “'valid who makes
love to every girl he meets, leaving also a strong
mission when his heiress-nurse annexes hinf
that he will continue through married life to do
the same. Miss Jennie Maynard never misses a
point, nevertheless she creates an opinion that
pure comedy is not her forte. This idea is con-
her acting of Saucers in the after-piece,
at her toUches show Mis 3 Jennie M.
* * • *
A Bit of Old Chelsea” is the one-act
story of the rescued flower-girl who will not per-
mit the sculptor who rescues her at two o’clock in
the morning to send her home in a cab, as she will
thereby lose her character, but offers to sleep on
his hearthrug all night, which, in her rank of life
is evidently a mere circumstance. The sculptor
has only one room, and his visitor’s settled deter-
mination to repose for the night on the hearthrug
of that apartment is the chief motive of the plav.
Ihe mora l, evidently, is that when a respectable
artist finds a frozen flower-girl on the pavement
outside, his best plan is to call a policeman. It is
a quaint and pretty little playlet, but the flower-
s' 1 / 3 . c ' ress 13 immaculately clean when she is
lilted from the street; also, when she leaves the
studio she forgets to take her hat with her,
though it is probably her only headgear, and
an obliging galleryite earnestly entreated her oil
Saturday to come back for the neglected asset.
Next Thursday (27fcli June) Shakespeare’s
grim old comedy, “The Merchant of Venice,”
will commence a three-nights’ season at Sydney
Royal. Mr. I. M. Alexander, the dramatic and
vocal trainer, who is running the show, takes the
part of the blood-thirsty mortgagee, while the
original lady-barrister, Portia, is to be represented
by Miss Edith Pasca Page. The remainder of the
characters will be done by 14 students of Mr.
Alexander s Shakespearian Society, and the show
is under the patronage of Chief Justice Harley
and other personages of great weight.
** * w
On farther investigation the domestic
drama at Sydney Criterion has a moral still
more out of gear than was at first notice-
able; and the working man who does not
support his wife, and yet objects to the
other fellow doing so, is guite undeserving the
plaudits which an undiscriminating gallery awards
him, when he speaks large tearful paragraphs in
prison about his lost honor. A great deal is made
of the affection which the ruined man shows his
child, but as we see him first stealing the coverlid
from her cradle for drink, and later escorting 1 her
over the town late at night (when she should be
in bed) in order that she may see him mur-
der her mother, his paternal affection is
clouded over with considerable doubt. The other
characters on the stage, mostly working people,
are also rather curious. When the working man
murders his Annie, her girlhood’s friend gently
remarks that, in his queer, odd way, he must have
loved her. The good working man preaches to
the wicked aristocrat on the heartlessneas of the
world which stones the woman and lets the guilty
lover alone. Meanwhile, he helps in his escape
the man who not merely stoned the woman in
question, but killed her outright. Most women
would prefer the metaphorical stoning of a heart-
less world to the large physical rock of an
annoyed husband. According to the “Milestones
of Life” the working man is moral to brutality.
Smashed windows and the coverlid stolen from
baby’s bed do not excuse a wife in leaving her
husband’s roof. The wronged husband is still
entitled to give her her quietus, though he has
strenuously refrained all through her married life
from giving her anything else.
* * * •
In addition to all this, great annoyance
is occasioned to the present unpretentious pagan
by the behaviour of the good working-man’s wife.
She is a very unpleasant, preachy young person,
and does not stand by her school-girl friend to
any extent worth mentioning. When the dving
woman sends a message asking her to command
see her for the last time she asks her hus-
band what she shall do, and when he tells her
to consult her own heart she goes virtuously and
with an unnecessary halo of nobility about her.
An ordinary pagan would go to a dying comrade
as a matter of course ; a stage Christian always
does these actions with much virtue and bowls her
saintly halo along with her as if it were a hoop.
Little Edna Seaton deserves credit for getting
completely away from the ordinary angel-child
the one who comforts the tipsy father in adversity.
Edna makes not the slightest attempt to uplift
her father. She takes her parents and their on-
slaughts upon each other in a cheerful spirit, and
tells the good little boy, when he affirms that he
has never stayed in a work’us, “’Ope as yer never
knows yer luck.”
** * *
Herr Pagel, the “German Hercules,”
burst forth upon the Sydney public at the Empire
last Saturday. As an exponent of the drearily
wonderful art of moving heavy substances to
places where they are not wanted Pa«el is not to
be sneezed at. In fact he is not to be sneezed at
on any subject, unless there is plenty of space for
a run. Then it might be done, for his hind legs
do not look as though adapted for bust travelling.
The advt. says Pagel is the only genuine strong
man Australia has yet seen, and the matter is not
of sufficient interest to be worth arguing about.
He elevates a bar-bell, said to weigh 2501b5., right
up above his head with one hand. A small pony
with a small boy on its back are next waved aloft
as an Australian contingenter might shake a
’possum in the face of the Boer nation. Pagel’s
final performance is the whirling up of his wife on a
/olb. dumb-bell in his right hand, while his left
holds up another 751 b. dumb-bell for her close
inspection. The Herr is decidedly impressive,
and he challenges anybody to come and do like-
wise, loser to pay £lO to sundry charities. So far
no one has taken up the challenge, although
several people go on the stage every night and tug
at the weights, and smell them, and look at them
side-ways, and by other methods satisfy them-
selves that they are genuine. The other new
arrival, Denis Carney, calls himself a Yorkshire
comedian, and some remarks about him are being
saved up for next week.
*# * *
As mentioned last week, “ Florodora,”
at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), is gradually trans-
forming itself into still another new opera
under the influence of many additions. Eileen
Boyd, one of these additions, belongs to the
infantile brigade, and if it is a fair thing that
children should be on the boards instead of in
bed, Eileen has as much right to be habitually out
of her little cot at a late hour as any other of
her persuasion.

Next Saturday night the “ Nightbirds ”
will be replaced at Sydney Lyceum by a horsey
melodrama entitled “The Favorite.”
** * *
Snazelle is still at Sydney Queen’s Hall,
though the time for his departure is said to be
drawing near. In this place it may be as well to
warn loyalists that the monarehial tradition will
likely come to its end through the machine which
Snazelle is permitted to convey through the Em-
pire. The Cinematograph is one of the greatest
anti-monarch arguments up to date. There have
been a good many anti-monarch arguments
in past ages, and many a potentate has had
to leave so suddenly by reason of them that
he left his kit of reigning tools behind him. But
as a slow, persistent, corroding argument the
Cine, has no equal. When the public come to
realise that an invention which ought to be show-
ing them the wonders of earth and sky and
humanity, is being utilised more and more to
bring before them long, dreary funerals of dead
monarchs, and long, dreary processions of living
ones, painfully nod-nod-nodding to great masses
of shadows, the public is liable to grow tired. For
which reason it is to be hoped that Snazelle will
not be weary of ill-doing, but will put on a few
more royal funerals, and keep them on.

It is getting nearly time Harry Rickards
introduced a little variety into the “ first part” at
Sydney Tivoli. The spectacle of the well-fronted
ladies marching in and round about to their chairs,
the promenade of the black men, the ambling of
the two couples, followed by four artificial negroes
rushing to the centre and yelling “In gay Puree !
I* l guy Paree ! ” is becoming a trifle wearisome.
I he rest of the first part is not calculated to stiinu-
late a jaded appetite, though pretty little Pearl
Lovell and Arthur Crane put some brightness into
it. The “olio” is much stronger. The polyphone’s
rendering of the ancient but tuneful “Rocked in
• 6 C r adle t * ,e -^ ee P ” 13 a decided success, but
its other numbers are clangy. Polyphone items
want careful selecting. John Coleman returned
unexpectedly on Saturday from the place where
he has been for a long time, and received an ova-
tion for his Scarecrow Dance. When the audience
brought him back to do it again for the severalth
time, his wife, a fac simile of himself in everything
except size, also appeared and made a second
Scarecrow. John made a speech in which he said
that he hadn’t been in several places where he was
accused of being, but as to where he had been he
offered no information worth mentioning.
#* # *
Ella Russell’s engagement with George
Musgrove was for £5O a week, and, as it turned
out, she would have been a good investment at a
much higher salary. The arrival of the buxom
lady shook up the Melbourne season wonderfully,
though her voice took about three weeks to recover
from the effects of the sea voyage, and was a dis-
tinctly old voice at its best. Declamatory force,
backed by a big professional reputation, gave
Miss Russell an importance that had been want-
ing in the individuality of other ladies of the co.
l a P r * llla donna can convince her audience that
she has been in the habit of holding a foremost
place on the stage or platform she is all right.
Albani, years ago, was a financial success in Aus-
tralia because she gave them all that is expected
of a famous singer—except the voice that won the
fame. The general public felt satisfied as to the
voice having been in the artist’s possession at one
time or other, and they tried to think it was still
there.
** * *
Amusing to notice the chagrin of a number
of Melbourne musical people, when Miss Olive
Kingston, unknown to them even by name, came
out and took her place as a minor star vocalist at
the Gerardy concerts. The management needed
a fresh young artist, something different from the
styleless ballad warblers who crowd upon the
market and hustle for opportunity to appear at
suburban halls —and Miss Kingston, the apt pupil
of Marchesi, was just the soprano to fill the
position. ’Cellist Gerardy’s Co. includes only
himself, Herr Scharff, the pianist, and Miss
Kingston, so the debutante (she has now gone to
Adelaide with the party, and will probably
go to Sydney later) got a distinctive start
in professional life. Too nervous to do herself
justice on the first night, she sang the
well-known waltz song from “Romeo and
Juliet ” admirably enough. At the next
concert she shone in a scene from “Lucia,” and
before the Melbourne season was over the new
soprano had knocked ’em with her bright voice
and skilful execution. Miss Kingston is the most
promising of all the Melbourne girls who have
been heard in public for some time past. Her
light voice, which will go on improving with age,
like good wine, is of the kind that takes kindly to
culture. She has the makings of a valuable
platform personality, and all the essentials to
success as an artist. The ordinary empty-headed
concert-singing young lady who hangs around the
music-shops and revels in cheap notoriety is not
in the same class with unobtrusive Olive King,
ston.

At Her Majesty’s (Melb.) the sacred lamp
of Italkn Opera continues to burn brilliantly.
“Ernani,” on Saturday, was accountable for
another great house, and vast demonstration.
Simonsen’s first co. played “ Ernani ” on the same
stage 14 years ago, since when Melbourne has
heard nothing of Verdi’s old opera, as far as the
present historian can remember. A deliberately
tuneful opera throughout, it is remarkable for not
supplying the name character, or even his best
girl, with a very sweet solo. They sing much that
is pleasing, but nothing that goes straight to the
uneducated soul of the people whose “encore”
is law. The basso gets the principal
sugary plum of the performance, also
the baritone has a toothsome item all
o himself, and no specially attractive burst of
music is allowed to transpire without one, or both,
o these heavy men assisting largely in the work.

The Cobar Chesney mine, Cobar, have lately
placed an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Com-
pany for a pair of powerful winding engines to haul from
2000 feet. These engines are rapidly approaching com-
pletion, and will be delivered about first of next month.
The same firm of engineers have also supplied the Occi-
dental mine, Cobar, with a handsome pair of winding
engines, as well as a boiler. This machinery was delivered
in very quick time and should give great satisfaction.*

Gentleman having only a limited time at their
disposal can obtain the practical instruction in assaying
and metallurgical work, necessary to qualify them for
obtaining good positions, in the ore testing works of Gil-
fillan and McCreery, Stock Exchange Buildings, Melb.*

If you want to borrow money, go to N.S.W.
Mont de Piete Co., Ltd., 74 Castlereagh-street, 74.
Eustace Bennett, Manager.*

The Financier anti Bullion ist of 2nd August,
1900, writes re the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance
Society, Limited : “ Considering the world-wide opera-
tions of the Society, it is astounding that, with all its
gigantic ramifications and temptations to accept new
business of a risky nature, it has passed through the
ordeal not only scathless, but with almost unparalleled
progress.’"*

About the best Brand for Sheep now in the
market is Kemp’s Branding Liquid. It goes further and
is considerably cheaper than any other. It will dissolve
in the scour, and should certainly be used by those who
hope to get a good price for their wool. E. Rich & Co.,
Sydney and Brisbane, hold stocks.*

All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on up-
to-date lines include a number of Luhrig vanners. The
South and Junction companies each have about a dozen,
no slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Vanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Coolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances.*

Ab Melb. Bijou old friend “ Monte
Cristo ” is working out his slow vengeance to-
night (Thursday) for the fifth and last time this
season. “Monte Uristo,” as Dampier produces
it at the Bijou, is an improvement on what it was
at the Alexandra in days of yore, and a moderate-
sized audience took the good with the bad of the
performance, and applauded the manager vigor-
ously on Saturday. To-morrow (Friday) “ Ham-
let ” goes up for one consecutive occasion, any-
how. The advt. at time of writing didn’t state
whether Ophelia’s best boy would hang on to the
bill for a longer period, or make way for another
drama next night. Seems to the present scribe
that the policy of administering Shakespeare to
the public in homoeopathic doses is a mistaken
policy, because it doesn’t catch the large heavy
section of the public which never gets news of a
show except from somebody who has lately seen
it.

The Darbos, whose charm of novelty has
departed, and left them in the shadow of the
Lauri-Carrie Moore burlesque, are the latest
“ star turn ” at Rickards’ Opera House, where
graceful Celina Bobe continues to treat the
xylophone with two inspired hammers, and make
clever music from her violin. Austin Rudd is
back again for a brief period, also, the ultra-flexible
Rosie Aquinaldo. And the new first part con-
tinues to be more cheerful than the old first part.

At the Town Hall the Imperial Concert
Co., consisting of Madame Belle Cole in the
first place, Avon Saxon, Mdlle. Cheron, and a
youthful .Paganini who is said to be younger than
he looks, have been giving popular concerts at an
unfortunate time. At the Athenaeum Hall, the
remarkable card tricks and conjuring of Charles
Bertram are going fairly strong, but the objection
to Bertram’s superabundant patter is a matter
worth considering. More magic and fewer remarks
made Dante’s entertainment a brighter affair.

This is saying nothing of the fact that over £12,000,000 of
ordinary 7 expenditure has been charged to loans at
different times, and is now being so charged at the rate
of about £1,000,000 a year. It is a beautiful deficit, yet
N.S.W. is already talking about the surplus it is to have
under the Federal tariff, and wondering what on earth to
do with it; and the S.M. Herald, in a leader last Tuesday,
urged the public to take “ the wisest measures that we
can for dealing with A SUPERFLUOUS AND RATHER
DANGEROUS INCOME.”

During the last 17 months New South Wales
has borrowed nearly £5.000,000 —£2,000,000 in Sydney and
£2,000,000 in London on Treasury 7 Bills, and about
£1,000,000 from the Savings Bank or by the issue locally
of 3 per cent, inscribed stock. As regards the Treasury
Bills, just consider these results :
Amount
received for
Where Issued. Interest. each £lOO

In each year the local market gave a better price for 3J
per cent, stock than the London market did for 4 per
cent, stock. Under these circumstances the action of the
Lyne-See Government in going to the expensive London
market looks like a dismal example of ignorance and con-
fusion. The question which is never answered is this:
Why should the British lender be offered 4 per cent,
while the local one is only offered 31? If it is because
the Australian is a poor, degraded animal who doesn’t
deserve so good a return for his money as the lordly
Briton will W. Lyne please say so, and end the contro-
versy.

The Grand Hotel, Limited (Melbourne), is show-
ing a considerable improvement in its prospects. Results
for the last two years are as follows :
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
31st March, 1900.. £2699 .. About 2 p.c. = £562 .. £15,750
„ 1901.. 4608 .. 3 „ = 927 .. 19,431
The profit would be only £3161 if the same method was
followed this .vear as last; in 1900, 10 per cent, de-
preciation was written off furniture out of profits, while
in 1901 the 10 per cent. (£1447), instead of being written
off, is carried to replacement reserve. Even on this
stricter basis, however, there is a good increase in the
profits, which are equal to over 10 per cent, on the paid-
up capital of £30,913. The reason why larger dividends
are not paid is that the property (put in the balance-sheet
at a book-value of £102,999, which is probably much more
than the present real value) was burdened until lately 7
with a huge mortgage of £70,000. This has been
reduced in the last two years by 7 £4501*, and now
stands at £65,500, and it will evidently be necessary to
apply the bulk of the earnings to repayment of this liability
for many years before it is wiped off. All the same the
Grand Hotel, Limited, is earning splendid profits in pro-
portion to its capital, and if the shareholders are prepared
to plod away till they lift their mortgage, the earnings,
with over £2OOO a year saved in mortgage interest, should
one day place their concern in a position to pay 7 12 per
cent, dividends with ease. The Grand Hotel Co., by the
way, has a strange and motley collection of reserves—
ordinary reserve fund, £69; replet'ement reserve, £2197 ;
mortgage reduction reserve, £4soi\ contingency reserve,
£10,613; and undivided profit, £2052; total, £19,431.
Why not carry, say, £1931 of the undivided profit to the
ordinary reserve, and make it £2000? A reserve fund of
£69 is painful to contemplate.
Some remarks from Toronto Economist re the
Independent Order of Foresters, now, unfortunately,
doing life assurance business in Australia: —“ In May,
1896, there were about 90,000 members in the order,
carrying upwards of 100,000,000d01. of life and endow-
ment assurance, with an accumulation of less on an aver-
age than 19dol. to the credit of each lOOOdol. certifi-
cate. A large number of certificates have matured, and
no doubt new ones will fall due each year hereafter. As
the cost of carrying the risk increases very rapidly from
age 55 to age 70j the premiums paid between these ages
are insufficient to carry the bare insurance without any
provision being made to meet the endowment of 900dol.
per lOOOdol. policy at age 70. The endowment must
therefore be paid out of the accumulations of the
members who have not reached an age when the cost
of carrying their risk is not equal to or greater than the
premiums being paid. How long can this robbing
Peter to pay Paul system go on before all of Peter’s
money will be exhausted? Some years ago the Supreme
Chief Ranger used both argument and invective against
any person who would dare to dispute the sufficiency of
the premium rates then being charged. They were exe-
crated as the emissaries of old line insurance companies.
Notwithstanding all his previous arguments to the con-
trary, at the last Supreme Court meeting held in this
city in 1898, without an apology to the members or a
blush of shame, he coolly produced a bran-new scheme of
assessment to go into effect on the Ist January, 1899, but
to apply only to members who joined after that date.
This produced the incongruous spectacle of two members
each, say, 44 years of age at entry, the one paying a pre-
mium of 88 cents per month, the other paying 2dols. per
month. Could anything be more absurd? Outside of a
friendly society could anything more unjust be found?
Can any but a plausible argument be used to justify its
continuance?”
Apropos of the above, by the way, a Dr. Mon-
tague has been lecturing in Victoria on the blessings and
advantages of insuring one's life in the remarkable
1.0. F., and Kyneton Observer reports him as saying
proudly that “the 1.0. F. had £1,000,000 invested in
Government bonds.” This is very beautiful. The blessed
1.0.F.’s published balance-sheet shows total assets
of less than a million, and of that amount the
bulk is in mortgages. Dr. Montague is also reported as
stating that no member of the 1.0. F. is allowed to borrow
money on his policy. This was actually put forward as
an advantage. No member could borrow on an 1.0. F.
policy. When money is lent on a policy it is lent on the
surrender value, and the 1.0. F. policy has no surrender
value. If the insured fails to pay a premium even after 30
years of membership, the policy is simply lost, and all the
money previously paid on it is lost also. It would be
about as easy to borrow on a brown paper bag as on the
surrender value of a policy that hasn’t any.
* * • *
The directors of Bendigo Cash Coupon Co.
waited upon Premier Peacock lately and asked him to
bring in a Bill to abolish the Cash Coupon business alto-
gether. The reason for this startling conduct on the part
of the Cash Coupon directors is that the Bendigo co. is a
purely protective company. Another co. came to Bendigo
and sold its coupons to certain traders only, and the
other traders found that their business was falling off.
First they tried to induce their rivals to give up couponing,
but failed, so they banded themselves together and started
a rival co. A six months’ record of the new co. is in-
teresting : it issued £3OOO worth of coupons, but only
£BOO worth were presented for payment; so the co. not
only made a profit on the goods given away for the £BOO,
but made a further profit of about £2200 on coupons
which were never presented. But the directors say that
the coupon system is a parasite on trade and an injustice
to the trader. Their experience, and the ridiculously
exorbitant profits they make out of it, show that it is of
little or no value to the public; and their co. is quite
prepared to give up the big profits it has been making
provided the Government will wipe the whole coupon
business out of existence.
* * * *
M. Govt, is now taking on accident assurance
under authority of the Act of ’99. The pronounced
success of its life business augurs that the new line will
be profitable.
* • • •
During the first quarter of the year, N.S.W.
workmen’s own contributions to the Miners' Accident
Relief Fund totalled £4729. (Ninepence a fortnight is de-
ducted from each man’s pay, where 20 men are employed
at a mine, and the employer hands over the total to a
committee.) The relief paid was £1053. The workmen’s
contribution is a fourth of the whole ; the employer pro-
vides another fourth, and the State the other half. The
benefits are 12s. a week during total disablement; Bs. a
week for a widow or other dependent, and 2s. 6d. for each
child under 14 : £l2 for funeral expenses. Roughly, it is
estimated that the recent accident in Broken Hill South
mine, when six men were killed, will cost, the fund £l6OO,
though much depends upon the re-marriage or otherwise
of the widjws. This is about as much as the workmen
at this mine will contribute in four years, at the present
rate of contribution.
• * * *
Sixteen fatal accidents in and about Broken
Hill mines and ore-reducing works this year down to the
beginning of May ! The worst average in any year. Has
the fall in the value of the metals induced the co’s to
take more risks—or, rather, to consent to the miners
taking more risks ? For it is a deplorable fact that many
miners—perhaps the vast majority of them—will take any
risks bosses will let them take.

duction works.

m m m *
Most mining co. meetings are a farce when they
aren’t a tragedy for somebody or other. The directors
turn up, also the sec., and sometimes a shareholder whom
one of the board has just “ shouted ” for. Block 14 direc-
tors have had this experience so often that when, at the
half-yearly meeting, t’other day, an unknown person
moved an amendment requesting more information,
there wasn’t anybody in a position to second it, even for
the purpose of'getting the grievance discussed. Yet it
needs discussing—badly. The opinion among scores of
mining men at Broken Hill is that it doesn’t pay a co.
with so comparatively small a mine as Block 14
to smelt its own ores; and the grievance
is that the co., alone of all the Broken Hill cos.,
doesn’t give enough information to allow shareholders
to judge whether this is so or not. Anyhow, Block
10, with a less area and no carbonate ore, by selling its
mill product to the people whose exclusive business it is
to smelt, has made three times more profit than Block 14 ;
and the South, adopting the same policy of selling its
product, has made about thrice the profit, notwithstand-
ing the poorness of its ores so far as silver is concerned.
Talk of a lead combine, and a zinc combine, and
a silver ditto ; but nothing definite as to any. Operation
in silver seems unlikeliest, owing to the vastness of the
accumulations ; but something may be done in the others.
In fact, “ understandings ” between European zinc dis-
tillers have been common enough hitherto.
* * * *
The yarn about that lead-combine is probably
a lie, told for the purpose of adding a few shillings to the
prices of Barrier stocks, and to enable brokers to take
advantage. There is not much hope for lead. Stewart
admitted so on his return from London recently.
Often a good metal market is anticipated without even-
tuating. Writer never heard the anticipation of a bad
one proving incorrect.
• * * *
One of Australia’s most enterprising (and not
least lucky) mining men is the opulent Mr. James Dick,
of leather-belting fame, who has many big irons in many
fires. He lately became sole owner of that long-worked
and enduring mine, Mitchell’s Ceeek Freehold, which he is
now completely re-fitting with plant and further develop-
ing at a cost of about £20,000. The new mill will be one of
40 heavv stamps. The Mitchell’s Creek Freehold, which
has a phenomenally long and regular gold-chute, has for
some years made regular returns of from £20,000 to
£30,000, which Mr. Dick now means to largely increase.
During the erection of the new plant a full complement of
miners will be kept on in sinking and opening out the
deeper levels, so as to get a big ore reserve ready for
treatment by the new machinery.

• * * •
Mining at Cobar: Several of the mines of this
district are shut down for want of water. Many in other
N.S.W. camps are shut down for want of gold. It will
take some months for the other mines of Cobar to ex-
haust their water-supply, even if they have no ram
meanwhile. The mines along the lode are also being con-
nected bv railway, in the making of which much fat-
headedness is displayed. It was first intended to continue
the line from the big copper-mine, and some of the earth-
works in this direction were started. It was then
arranged to start the railway from a point about a mile
from Cobar railway station, and run along the foot of the
hills on which most of the mines are situated. Finally the
line is being constructed along the main street of
Wrightville, the name of the town formed within the last
three years. The result is that in one part of the
thoroughfare there is a railway-cutting Bft. deep and in
another an embankment of about same height. To carry
the railwav into the Cobar Gold Mines will cost about
£lO9O, and then it will not be nearly as convenient as if
the railway was carried along the hills. The same diffi-
culty applies to Cobar-Chesney and other mines. The
route taken will serve no good purpose whatever as pas-
senger-traffic is prevented by the line not running into
Cobar town. It is only another Govt, blunder at Cobar
where so many have been committed, and the only ex-
cuse given is that in building this line employment was
wanted for men who could only use pick and shovel, and
were not accustomed to blasting operations. Some
£20,000 has been expended in a Govt, water-scheme.
There is no water in the reservoir, which is soshallow that,
when it is full, more water will be lost by evaporation
than the people will use.
• » • •
Cobar Gold Mines : The 100-head stamp mill
and cyanide plant running continuously, but, as usual, no
returns given. Another lift opening up, carbonates of
copper showing in the bottom, and some ore of good
value in gold has to be put aside because of its copper-
contents ; but all the ore above the level is absolutely
free of copper, and from present appearances this lift
will, if anything, be better in gold than those above.
There are still some hundreds of thousands of tons of
oxidised auriferous ore above the copper zone, and the
levels now are of great length, with ore still in the
faces, although the lode there is not of the average
width. The filter-pressing plant for the treatment of
slimes, started some time ago, had to be shut down. A
big mistake was made in using pumps to force the
solution through the presses with water instead of com-
pressed air. Pumps were first tried at Kalgoorlie, and
had to be discarded. The pumps are now being taken
out and a compressor taking their place. There is no
doubt whatever about the system, and but for this mis-
take the slime-plant on the mine would now be dealing
with upwards of 200 tons a day. The extraction will
probably be between 80 and 90 per cent., and the cost
much less than at Kalgoorlie, where labor is higher and
extraction lower because of the salts in the water absorb-
ing cyanide. » * *
Occidental: Reduction-plant shut-down for
want of water, but mine splendidly equipped for a big
output, and occasional divs. when crushing is resumed.
The new shaft and winding-plant will enable the ore
around the old shaft to be taken away and the mine
worked to a greater depth. Undoubtedly a payable pro-
position—cheaply worked, economically managed, many
years’ ore in view, and probably a copper-mine when the
oxides are exhausted.
» • » *
Young Australia: Struck what appears to be a
continuation of the surface chute below the 200 ft. level.
A few small crushings were gouged out, averaging 14dwt.
by amalgamation, and leaving an equal amount, if not
more, in the tailings, which are too coppery for cyanid-
ing. Main shaft going down for another lift, making a
little water, which is troublesome for want of a pump,
but which can be easily handled by baling as soon as the
shaft is down. It will then probably be found that the
ore contains a higher percentage of copper, and will not be
amenable to battery treatment, and to send it. avvav to
Dapto or Cockle Creek will mean that nearly all that is in
it will go in freight and smelting charges.
* * « *
Great Western : Under exemption, and the
mine can stand a lot of it without capital. It certainly
can’t be worked on a decent scale without, and it is about
time its management realised that it will take, say, £20,000
to make it a payable concern.
* « * *
Mt. Pleasant: Worked on tribute by the owners
of the Great Cobar copper mine for the sake of the
silicious copper ore found below the 20')ft. level They
have the right of purchase for £5OOO at the end of their
tribute, and the manner in which they are working the
mine gives the impression that they intend to exercise
the option.
* * * •
Cobar Chesney : Still spending a lot of money
without hope of immediate return. The mine is being
placed in good shape for a big production. The basket-
ware concentrating plant is to be remodelled. About two-
thirds of it is useless and will be thrown out, and other
plant, such as Wilfley tables and spitzcasens, will take its
place. That part of the plant for which no expert advice
was sought is all that is wanted. The boilers are of good
type, the air-compressor is undoubtedly the finest in the
district, the driving-engine power is also the biggest in
Cobar, and the new winding-plant will also deserve that
designation. Much money has also been wasted in bad
mining. The levels at 360 and 460 ft. have been driven
far off the lode all the way. In some places
it has been found necessary to crosscut over 20ft. before
the western or foot-wall of the lode was reached. Just
think of anyone being unable to keep levels in such a lode
as the main lode of Cobar (having an average width of
over 40ft. !) The mistakes made in mining and
machinery on the Cobar-Chesney mine are simply appall-
ing. It may work out its own salvation some day ;
already nearly £lOO,OOO has gone in the attempt.

Q, Govt, has just sent 50 unemployed, giving
them 10s. a week, to hunt for gems at Anakie (0.Q.),
Anakie yields a prolific crop of stones—sapphires, zircons,
etc. ; but up to now the bulk have been like land-bank
assets in ’93, bad or doubtful.

Croydon notes to May 30 : Late developments
prove Golden Gate lode to be continuous and nearly two
miles long ; the regularity of the shoots is now being
proved by thorough and systematic prospecting. Latest
discovery is in Golden Gate 12 South, tribute party strik-
ing a 2ft. reef similar in appearance to famous G.G. 3 and
4 South. Recent crushings : Golden Gate and Nancy
Lee United, 540 tons, 844£0z. ; Cuthbert.’s Golden Gate 5
South, partial clean-up, 431 tons, 5900 z. ; Golden Gate 3
and 4 South, 540 tons, 1963£0z. ; Mountain Maid P.C.
(owned by Victorian capitalists), 54 tons, trial from 7ft.
reef, 470 z., worth £3 Bs. per oz. ; Jubilee Consols
(True Blue) lease, 140 tons, 1790 z. 18dwts., reef
and gold improving in deep levels ; Rogers’ Golden Gate,
272 tons, 322£0z. Recent important developments
on Content line, connecting link between Golden Gate
and True Blue lodes. Good gold struck in Kitto’s claim.
Block shaft being sunk by Fred Cuthbert to cut reef at
250 ft. By the way, Cuthbert is gone for a trip to Glasgow
Exhibition. Made his pile in Croydon mines, and is still
a “ sticker.” Walked to the field from Normanton in the
early days. Went away sick with fever ; walked back from
Cooktown. Went into mining, and prospered ; now re-
garded as the “ Lansell ”of the North. Recently 7 took up
a big slice of Queensland loan. He’s all right. Croy-
don’s outside “ shows ” are being again taken up. When
capital oomes along, there are reets outside the “ Golden
Corner,” with millions of tons easily 7 getatable, that will
be grabbed up and crashed wholesale—the only way they
can be made to pay.

Known as the Fig Tree, and lies about 15 miles north-

the whisper of a few dwt. colour being found is a great
draw for diggers. The month of May saw several “ pic-
canniny” rushes on dwt. rumours, and a few score men
sank holes round the lucky colour holes—in all case*
duffers.

BRAMDY
Whether Australia shall hold an Exhibi-
tion or not within the next few years (and such a
commercial machine could not be properly engi-
neered with less than several years’ preparation)
the suggestion that it shall be started to com-
memorate the inauguration of Federation is par-
ticularly weird. Australia has been com-
memorating that inauguration with Dukes,
Princesses, titles, and fireworks, till the country’s
liver don’t know where it are. To all gigantic
showmen, who probably see something in such
razzles for themselves, Australia should unhesitat-
ingly say, “Give us a rest ! Get to work in the
ordinary industrial channels, and prove your
patriotism by helping to drag the land out of
debt.”
The Age makes moan over the fact that
Victoria has been deprived of the comfort and
consolation of a real Hall-marked vice-regal re-
Eresentative for close upon 18 months, and
athetically beseeches Premier Peacock not to
continue to risk the moral and material salvation
of the State by retaining a system that leaves its
“administrator with an inferior status to other
Australian Governors ” The fact that the
Imperial Powers show no particular anxiety to
fit Victoria witli a Governor seems to “wooden”
the Age with amazement. Can David really,
really be desirous of bequeathing a baronetcy,
and does he fear that the present Acting-Gov.
system is a poor medium for the transmission of
grovel ?
People begin to wonder what Gov.-General
Hopetoun is to make out of that Imperially-
calamitous fiasco, the Royal Visit. At onetime
it was thought certain that a dukedom would come
his way. But after the ignominious failure of this
Royal “ outfit "’ to impress Australia and the
heavy slump in Royal titles and presents, he will
probably have to put up with a good deal less.
Something like a Knight Grand Cross-ship of the
Ro,, al Victorian Order, for instance. The privi-
lege of tacking a few more letters on to his already
fej.r ome official title will be scant repayment for-
the expense and trouble of the visit, to say nothing-
of the worry and anxiety occasioned to His Ex.
by the humiliating exhibition which the coming:
K. has made of himself.
* * » *
Several of the Vic. State Ministers are in
Lord Hopetoun’s black-list. The trouble arose out
of York’s visit —what a curse that visit will be
yet! Seems that one of the many dinners given
by the Gov.-General was arranged in order that
the State Ministers and their wives might have-
an opportunity of grovelling before Royalty and
its missus. All the Ministers accepted the
invitation. Some of them, however, did not turn
up, and some of them turned up without their
wives, and Lord Hopetoun was very angry. Of
course it would have been more manly and cour-
teous if the Ministers who did not propose to'
attend the dinner had declined the invitation in
the first place; but still the fact that on second
thoughts some of them decided not to be there
gives hope that a day will come when even
Australian politicians will not all go down on.
their stomachs at the shadow of Royalty across,
their paths.
* * » •
The York visit to Ballarat was mainly
undertaken to lay the foundation-stone of the
soldiers’ statue ; 18 minutes being allotted for the
function. The Jook drove up, accepted the
jewelled trowel, said : “lam pleased to say that
this stone is well and truly laid,” and drove off,
the time occupied being 59 seconds. The authori-
ties had announced that offerings could be laid on
the stone, and expected at least £5OO. As, how-
ever, the crowd were too loyal to exceed the
Royal donation, which was nix as usual, not a
solitary copper was forthcoming. The Ballarat
loyalists are as whole-souled as they make ’em;
but when they reflected that the Imperial Parlia-
ment had just voted £40,000 for the Royal pair;
that the Duchy of Cornwall is turning in a cool
£60,000 per annum ; that the Australian trip costs
tiie Jook nothing ; that valuable gifts are being
showered on the visitors; and that the Austra-
lians (whose statue was thus inaugurated) had
died to support the thi’one the canny George may
one day occupy (though to back King Ned’s life
against his son’s wouldn’t be a bad wager)—their
loyalty received such a knock that it has had that
tired feeling ever since.
I here was a pleasing and instructive inci-
dent at Melbourne Old Scotch Collegians’ welcome
to Commander Tickell. The gallant Tickell told the
gathering that when in China he had not forgotten
the old school. He saw in a Buddhist temple at
Pekin a remarkable bell, and “ appropriated ” it
for the use of the college. He would be glad if
the Principal would accept it. Dr. Morrison
smilingly nodded his head, and the exhibition of
practical ethics ended with a promise that the
piece of loot from the heathen house of worship
should be utilised immediately to summon the
college-boys to morning chapel.

From Sydney D.T., 8/6/T :
ROYALTY AND THE RAILWAYS.
His Royal Highness the Duke of Cornwall and York,
when speaking to Mr. Oliver about the excellence of the
N.S.W. system of railways, presented him with a scarf-
pin of unique design, with an emerald set in gold, sur-
mounted by a gold crown. Mr. Harper, the chief traffic-
manager, was also signalled out for royal favor. The
Duke handed him a silver cigarette-case, with gold medal-
lions of the Duke and Duchess.
Good old regulations and discipline ! Precept’s
better’n example every time.

From Newcastle Chronicle (Eng.) :
It is reported that the Duke and Duchess of Cornwall
have, so far, derived marked benefit to their health by
their voyage in the Ophir. They are expected to arrive
at Melbourne about May 6. Antipodean loyalty is at
fever heat, and great preparations are on foot for the
reception of the Royal party and the festivities to he
associated therewith.
Query—What does the Jook look like when he’s
at home ? Also, has the fever subsided ?

• * * *
“P.X.” : The absurdity of public-holiday
arrangements was pretty well illustrated lately,
when, even in far-west N.S.W., schools, banks,
public offices, etc., were closed to allow the people
to join in the welcome of the Duke of York. I
spent the days in a township from which Sydney
can’t be reached in less than five days; but the
offices, such as they are, were glued-up all the
same.
* * * *
“ Royal visits to all parts of the British
Empire should be periodical in future,” remarks
a Sydney Jingo print. Judging from the effect
produced on the Australian mind by that most
inglorious fizzle, the Yorks’ tour, it would be in
the interests of the Monarchy if the Royal visits
suggested were made at, say, 200-year intervals.

In an interview with Mrs. 0 Neill, an old
Sydney resident who has acquired a sudden Dook-
o’-York vogue through having been in the late
Queen’s domestic serv .ee—Sydney Sunday Times’
“ Lady Special ” ask d the ancient daine’s present
entertainer: “Hov did you come to interest
yourself on Mrs. O’Neill’s behalf ? ” she received
in reply the following lovely squeak of bagpipe-
cum-penny-trumpet: “Because she remembers
my kinswoman, the Hon. Flora Macdonald, who
was appointed Maid of Honor to Her Majesty,
and later Woman of the Bedchamber. We belong
to the Macdonalds of Glencoe, the late Sir John
Macdonald, first Prime Minister of Canada, being
a relation of mine, and the present Lord Mac-
donald being chief of our clan.” How beautifully-
characteristic of the servant-girls’ organ !
* • * »
“ Hermes ” : Be the recent great Maori-
land hotel fire. When travelling in Maoriland
(in the North or Pig Island) I was always haunted
by a dread of fire, and carried a rope about with
me. No matter how late at night I arrived at a
strange hotel I made a point of learning
the geography and fastening the rope to
the bedstead, all ready to slither down in case of
accidents. Never found anything in the shape of
a fire-escape in any hotel in Wellington. On one
occasion I landed there at midnight, and was
shown into a room with only a sky-light. Porter
scouted the idea of tire, and declared it was the
only vacant room in the house, and chat all the
other pubs, were full. “All right, I’ll camp in
the street then.” And I went out and wandered
about for two hours till I got a bed in a room
which, in the event of a blaze, would not have
quite such a resemblance to a colonial oven. A
few years in a land of ancient wooden public-
houses makes you cautious.
• * • *
Stated that there were only two fire-escapes
in connection with the Grand Hotel (Auckland,
M.L.), recently burnt, and that both were death-
traps. Nearly every passage in the house led on
to the big circular staircase, the well of which
must have created a fearful forced draught once
the fire caught it.
* * * *
Australia’s only hippopotamus died in
Adelaide Zoo last week. The huge creature cost
the Zoo authorities over £lOOO, only last year. The
sight of the ungainly animal wallowing in the
mud was a useful object-lesson to the young, and
a warning that a misspent youth might end in an
aldermanic old age with a vote for the Legislative
Council.
* • • *
Melbourne's Board of Health t'other day
told the town Council of fashionable St. Kilda
that it didn’t approve of the practice of the
market-producers-going home with loads of
manure —camping in the main street and enrich-
ing the atmosphere the while they bought the
week’s supplies, and swore at the price of cauli-
flowers. YVhereupon, the Council replied that it
had taken great pains to attract said ‘ trade ”
from Prahran, and that it loved its collective
dung-cart too well to find anything objectionable
in the “bouquet” thereof. And the Board was
actually surprised.

The curious Australian notion of wh t
military discipline doesn’t mean was further
evidenced in Melbourne the other day, when over
100 troopers “ missed ” the s.s. Morayshire
apparently with the view of being trained to
Sydney. Only 20 of them, at all events, turned up
to travel by another steamer which the authorities
had arranged should take them along.
» » * *
Quite a crowd attended at a well-known
Sydney church a week or so ago, to witness the
wedding of a wealthy spinster and a young gentle-
man half her age who had boldly wooed and won
her. Guests were all present, even the best man
was there and the bridesmaids, but the hour rolled
by and the principals failed to appear. Finally,
no explanation being forthcoming, the guests had
to go home, and the breakfast, prepared by a
swell caterer, was left uneaten. Which, after all,
was perhaps the saddest incident.
* * * *
Great excitement in King-street, Sydney,
on Saturday when the balloon-vendor lost his grip
of his wares and the whole stock-in-trade went
sailing skywards.
» » * «
A prevalent popular misapprehension was
once more exemplified at the inquest on Joseoh
Fowler, who recently suicided at Sou tl i Melbourne.
Fowler’s wife found him hanging by the neck, and
ran for assistance. The next-door neighbor came
in, had a look, and went for another neighbor,
who in turn went for the police. When the latter
arrived, the “suspended” (possibly still alive
when first discovered) was, of course, quite dead.
When asked why they had not oromptly cut the
man down, the witnesses said that they “thought
a person had no right to touch a body before the
arrival of the police ” —a curious notion which has
converted many a half-strangled unfortunate into
cold meat.
* • * •
No sooner had Joseph Henry Grey, late of
Geelong, and later of Fiji, been committed for
trial on an involved and complicated charge of
forgery, than the Grown hastily dropped all other
charges against him. But the same “Crown”
was at the same time proceeding with great vigor
on each and all of about 20 different charges of
housebreaking against a couple of escapees from
Bayswater .Reformatory. Also, the week previous,
a trio of uninfluential larrikins, having been
ordered six years’ hard labor apiece for a serious
offence, were persistently docked again and atrain
by the Crown for trivial offences, the Judge shew-
ing what bethought of the proceedings by making
all sentences concurrent. Interesting to note
whether, in the not unlikely event of the forgery
charge failing, the other accusations against Grey
will be resuscitated.
* * * *
Rev. Bath, of Melbourne, states that if a
Speaker or President cannot reverently offer the
Lord’s Prayer when Parliament meets, he is “ un-
fit to represent the people and should acknow-
ledge it at once.” But why need the unhappy
official despair of learning to pray properly ? He
could take a short course of lessons. He might
go to Bath for instruction.

I*' w and * Troubles.
At a recent meeting of Sydney City Coun-
cil, Alderman Fitzgerald complained of the Board
of Health’s refusal (on the ground that the
nominee was very young, unmarried and quite
inexperienced), to recommend the appointment of
the lady-inspector (a 8.A., of the local University),
recently nominated by the Council. As the lady
was described as “young and charming” she
must be all the more fitted for poking round
Sydney slums, encountering the wrath of the
fierce female denizens thereof, and sniffing out
the noisome sink and the pestilential drain.
Shade of old Romance !

So far this year there have been 15 fatal
accidents in and about Broken Hill mines. In
five years 104 men have been killed.

One consolation from the “ Royal Visit.”
No Australian authoress will dare hereafter to
describe her particular “hero” as of “princely
mien.” Let us be thankful for small mercies.

Writes “Alba ” to The Bulletin
Russian war-ship Gromoboi, ram and general sea-jugger-
naut, lately anchored in Sydney harbour, iooks
wicked enough to knock roosting birds off Gibraltar.
The Russian navy is without traditions, save for a few
uninteresting encounters with Turkish monitors in ’76.
The Gromoboi’s crew are a deep-jawed, clear-eyed crowd
of young giants, ready to eat something as small, say, as
Japan. The other Sunday it seemed as though the whole
of Sydney were trying to hoard the big-beamed ram.
Women carrying children struggled bitterly to ascend
the steep gangway that led past tiers of black-snouted
guns. Almost at the foot of the gangway was the
Gromoboi’s cutter, packed with Russian sailors, watching
the people ascend. The fat women passed up without
comment, but when a thin girl started to climb up it was
the signal for peals of Muscovite laughter. The thin
Australian girl was so numerous, and the laughter so
indecent, that it evoked resentment from several male
visitors. Suddenly a golden beard popped from under a
steel barbette, followed by a pair of saucer-like epaulets.
“ Wunslci, knoutik !” it whispered ; “ Wunski, knoutik ! ”
and the 24 Russians in the cutter shuddered. There was
no more laughter. Then the officer with the golden
heard looked at the sky casually to see if there were pro-
spects of snow, and then down at the delinquents.
“ Knoutik 1 ” he said, with tender ferocity ; “ Knoutik ! ”
The boat’s crew shuddered itself almost beneath
gangway. Knoutik 1
A MATTER OF REGRET.
MRS. SMITH (reading in background).
“‘ Infants born on the date of the
Duke's landing will receive a gift of a silver
spoon ’ Oh, what a pity we didn't know!”
Melbourne city ratepayers are snorting
painfully over an addition of 4d. on their rates,
making the charge Is. 4d. in the £. The city
deficits have been long accumulating, and the cost
of the York flag-flying flare-up is a large lump on
top of the usual shortage. How wonderful is the
sobering influence of a little bill 1
♦ * * *
They are walloping a very sluggish animal
in Brisbane just now—the Queen Victoria Mem-
orial Fund, to wit. After two months’ laboring
and press-puff the result is £75 promised and £lO
in hand. Not more than would be collected in an
afternoon for an injured mannikin at a pony-race
meeting.
* * * •
“Melb.”: A mother of one of “Our
Boys,” providentially spared to come back from
S’Africa, writes to the papers asking for a
memorial service in the cathedral to thank Heaven
for his return. I don’t want to be harsh, but do
the mothers of the providentially killed and
mangled join in this ?
* * * *
Some curious evidence was given in a
divorce case lately heard in Wellington (M.L.).
The wife was the applicant. Reversing the usual
order of things, it was the husband who had gone
astray with the lodger. After the latter lady had
taken her departure he confessed everything to his
wife. Sheforgavehim, apparently, onconditionthat
the ex-lodger was offered up as a burnt —or rather
a spanked—sacrifice. So the siren was beguiled
back to the house by a telegram, and on arrival
was severely horse-whipped. According to the
wife’s story, it was the husband who flogged the
former object of his affections under her supervi-
sion ; the husband’s tale was that he merely held
the lady while the wife chastened her. Strange
to say, the reconciliation thus sanctified by sacri-
fice only lasted a few weeks.
* # * *
A novel charge was laid against a man in
Melbourne lately—that of “ taking a corrupt
reward.” He had offered to return, or put the
owner in the way of recovering, an allegedly stolen.
watch. Sec. 168 of the Crimes Act says : “ Who-
soever shall corruptly take any reward —under
pretence of helping any person to any money or
valuable security which shall have been stolen,
shall, unless he have used all diligence to cause
the offender to be brought to justice —be liable to
imprisonment for fifteen years." The most sur-
prising thing about the law is the number of ways
it has of catching the little fish while it lets the
big ones escape. With the latter the law always
says its mesh was too large, or the net wasn’t the
right sort for that particular fish, or it was look-
ing the other way when the shark went by.
There’s a close connection between the law and
fish.
• * * *
A budding Melbourne burglar struck a
new variety of householder t’other day._ Aroused
in the small hours, the housefather laid in a handy
poker and started to investigate. Finding a hat-
less and bootless stranger in a dark corner, he in-
quired of him how he entered. The burglar frankly
explained he had come by way of the verandah-
post, and through a bedroom window. Also he
said that.if let off he would quit the profession,
being convinced he had mistaken his vocation.
He assured the man with the poker he was willing
to depart quietly, and without needless publicity.
Whereupon the housefather escorted him to the
front door, watched him put on his boots and hat
and move down the street, and then went back to
bed. An officious policeman subsequently tried to
work up a “case” out of the incident, but the
citizen refused to take any interest in the pro-
ceedings.

was obviously a dreadful and blood-
thirsty anarchist, and as he was only a

expected to be knighted, but alas !
he explained. “My vife,” he said

cession can not see, and she at ze
back is, so I a way make so can she
see if it is true about the baby
that comes.” Katrina protested
volubly in Dutch to the same
effect, and the crowd laughed, and
we disgustedly dropped our anar-
chist.

From “ Watto ”
Dear Bulletin.— Apropos doctor-sweating. “ Wat Tell ”
writes what’s not correct. The doctor alluded to does
not receive 30s. per member. He receives 25s., and out
of this has to provide drugs and dressings—for which you
may deduct 5s. As far as I know he gets no extra fee for
operations. If another lodge-doctor be called in a con-
sultation fee of a guinea is allowed. The mileage rate is
not 2s. 6d. per mile, only beyond a radius of three miles
is the rate allowed, and it has to be collected by -the
doctor. He does not make about £300 a year. £200 is
probably a good deal above the mark. However, the
doctor has no cause to grumble at above rates for bona-
fide lodge practice. What he may complain of is thedact
that many wealthy people take advantage of the lodge
rates.

CONSTANT READER (in confidential tone): “Ha!

The principal hen-roost of the Melbourne
Ladies Bountiful is the Women’s Hospital Com-
mittee. These people indulge the common vice
of their type—namely, in ingenious
insulting the helpless who seek
their aid—by putting a distinguish-
ing label over the beds of the un-
married girls in the maternity ward,
This practically invites the atten-
tion of visitors, doctors, atten-
dants, married occupants of the
ward and tattling cats and hens
generally to the fact of
theirsingle butencumbered
blessedness. A vigorous
anti-bed-card agitation was
carried on for some time,
but with little apparent
effect on the she-Peeksniffs
of the hen-roost. It had
an effect on subscribers,
however. So many withdrew
their donations on high moral
grounds that the Women’s
Hospital is now in great
straits, and apparently the
hen-roost in charge will either
have to give up its cherished
insult or else see the institu-
tion gradually break up. At
present it looks as if the com-
mittee values its insult more
than its institution, and as if
the hospital may gradu-
ally dwindle and perish,
womanfully stating on
its bed-card to the last
that occupant Sarah
Jones ought to be mar-
ried but isn’t.
As a spectacular affair the ending of the
second trial of Mrs. Jane Smith for the Talune
alleged murder by poisoning was a distinct disap-
pointment. The court, as usual, was crowded,
and, lamentable to relate, a considerable propor-
tion of the audience was composed of well-dressed
women. Every other person turned to someone
and made remarks about “butchered to make a
Roman holiday ! ” and everybody expected there
was going to be a scene, and waited with intense
interest. Suddenly Mrs. Smith appeared through
the trap-door. Her face still looked round and
fresh, but the eyes showed traces of weeping and
racking anxiety ; yet she bore herself bravely,
and, guilty or innocent, she has enviable
courage. She sat down and bowed to her
counsel, and gave no signs of nervousness. The
court was filled with the hum of voices, and
a few shafts of yellow light came through the roof
windows and lit up the gilded cornice. Men
were jabbering about how she’d take it, what-
ever it happened to be, and some said she
was steeled to face the worst, and the only
thing that would knock her over would be an
acquittal. An awful voice crashed through the
hum, and as the echoes rolled away the battered
ears of the crowd realised that it had said
“Soilence!” And they were “soilent,” and
rose to their feet as a thin, hard-faced
man, with short, almost invisible side-
whiskers, and wearing a red skirt, which he
held up in a fashion that made him look like
a brahmapootra hen on a wet day, tripped up into
the canopied seat. The jurymen filed in and took
their places. Every eye turned on Mrs. Smith.
The plump, rounded bosom rose and fell a little
irregularly, but otherwise she gave no sign of
interest in the twelve. The small, grey-whiskered
man in the red skirt stood up and said to
the jury. “ I understand, gentlemen, you
have been unable to agree upon a verdict?”
The foreman replied that there was no
possible chance. The* Judge looked disgusted.
The foreman went on to say the jury had con-
sidered the case all night and in the early hours
of the morning, and could come to no decision.
The Judge looked bored, and started to adjourn
the Court, when the foreman stuck his voice in
again, and S’Onah waited impatiently. Then he
adjourned the Court till Monday, 10 a.m., re-
manded the prisoner to her former custody, and
began to trip out again, holding up his red dress.
The foreman once more pulled him up to thank
him and the Court-officials for their kindness, etc.
The Judge looked as though he would like to bite
the verbose foreman, but contented himself with
saying that the Court officials always did their
duty, but —er —were glad to hear that it was ap-
preciated. Then he got a fair hold of his dress,
and bolted. Mrs. Smith was talking to her juve-
nile-looking counsel, Mr. Mack. Her husband
stared at her very hard ; then he swished his
stick, and ambled out without throwing a kiss or
congratulation to the wife who had just escaped
through the jaws of death. Evidently a very self-
contained man. The accused is to be tried
again. There is a local precedent for such a
course in the Louisa Collins case, wherein, after
two juries had failed to agree upon a verdict, a
third jury was empannelled and arrived at a final
conclusion.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 18, 1901.
My Dear Arini,—
Didn’t someone once write an ode to Dullness ?
Anyway, I recall a line—“ For, born a Goddess,
Dullness never dies.” She’s goddessing in Sydney
just now to such an extent that some other place
must be neglected—probably Maoriland. At
afternoon-teas—there are plenty of them—you
may hear the flapping of the goddess’s wings.
A goddess has wings, hasn’t she ? I was at a few
teas last week—the usual thing. Women came
in from the eager, nipping air with blanched faces
and went out with suck red noses. There’s noth-
ing like a heated drawing-room and tea for rosing
your nose. Some of the women wore four-decker
hats. Really they looked as if they had four hats,
one over the other, with alternate brims of white
and black crinoline, the upper deck carrying a
freight of feathers and flowers And under the
hull, one had a bird and a bow. You’d think the
tonnage of these three or four-decker toques to be
considerable, but their substructure is so light
that they carry many cargoes of flowers and fowls
with great ease.
The afternoon-tea-lappers told each other in the
Orange Pekoe pauses that really the “ Voice that'
Breathed O’er Eden ” was having, or going to
have, considerable respiratory exercise. Miss
Rundle, a daughter of Mr. Bryce Rundle, is en-
gaged to Mr. Marsh, the agent for Mu mm’s cham-
pagne. I believe it was of this bride-elect that a
writer in a Melbourne weekly said : “She looks
just the kind of girl to whom Eric Mackay wrote
his ‘Love Letters of a Violinist.’” Mr. Ernest
Tyson—a thin, sallowish young man who lives at
the Australia in a private suite (his mother and
sister live at North Shore in ex-Speaker Abbott’s
former house) mariies pretty Miss Carrie Moore
in a few days. He isn’t a millionaire, or anything
approaching it, as has been given out by various
papers; in fact, he only inherits through his
mother, who is a niece of the late million-maker,
Tyson. She got £lB,OOO for her share of the
N.S. W. property. Miss Carrie will, after a short
honeymoon, return to the stage.
At one of these afternoon teas I saw a curious
veil—a black one with a thin, red pattern trailing
over it. It made the wearer look as if her face
had been carefully scratcuod with a pin. There
was another veil, a very fine mesh, with the
Southern Cross in big chenille spots three times
on it. One constellation caught the wearer over
the left eye, another oh the mouth, and the third
hung over an ear. I don’t think the S.C. ought
to be encouraged to leave its sphere in this veiled
fashion.
Already the recoil from mourning to coloring is
apparent. Shops are full of dawn-in-December
clothes (if you want anything brighter than a
midsummer sunrise I can’t direct you) and
“ Florodora ”-hued toques. One of the afternoon-
tea dames said that she had watched in vain for
the Duchess to wear one or any of those dresses
in which she was dressed in English fashion-
journals. “ And,” continued the scrutineer, “I
saw her on all her public appearances here and in
Melbourne and several times when she wasn’t in
the public eye. She never wore one of those
frocks that are described in Hearth and Home
or the Gentlewoman.”
I don’t see so many necklaced necks now. Blue
beads at 3£d. a string are within even the means
of the little girl who minds baby. Yet Potts
Point wore them under the alias “Venetian”
glass. It’s astonishing the number of women who,
since beads have been the wear, have relatives
and friends travelling abroad. “This necklace
was sent from Venice—Venetian glass. So nice
to get these things from the places where they’re
made, isn’t it ? ” And if you’re very horrid yoii’ll
ask “ Have Hordern’s a branch in Venice? ”
Referring to the prevailing style of dressing the
hair to resemble a large-sized onion, a lady re-
marked to me: “It takes a load off my mind to
hear that we can wear our hair uncrimped. My
husband said to me the other day, ‘ Lucy, I have
stood greases, I have stood curl-papers, but if
these fireirons you wear in your hair had lasted
much longer I should have been obliged to seek
another apartment.’ So I was pleased,” said the
lady, simply.
Duchess May is said to be very nervous at sea
in a storm. On the voyage out here the chief
stewardess of the Ophir (no, not a lady-in-waiting)
awaited on a couch at the foot of the Duchess’s
bunk whenever there was a promise of a stormy
night. One night a Wagnerian storm arose and
the stewardess sat up all the time, expecting the
Duchess to awake and add to the noise
without. Presently she did unbutton her eyes
and give a honi-soit-qui-mal-y-pense-ick-dien shriek,
and the stewardess was at her side in an instant!
“Thank goodness,” said the Duchess, “if you
weren’t here I’d have had a fit.” I chronicle this
remark merely to show the Duchess’s everyday-
ness. It was a natural human remark. Persons
from Milparinka and Boggabri would have insisted
that she’d say something like “Stewardess, take
your nothingness out of the path of the divine
circuit: Arouse the band and bid it play ‘ Rocked
in the Cradle of the Deep.’ ”
Duchess May had her teeth attended to whilst
here. Dr. Slate, of Macquarie-street, was the
happy dentist selected, and the royal toothache
took itself to his rooms to be cured. May’s teeth
look very pretty behind her smile, but no doubt
they had suffered from the salt horse on board the
Ophir.
There was a surplus of £52 after the Duchess’s
presentation fund. It will lie used to buy “some-
thing useful” for the children’s ward in the
memorial wing of Sydney Alfred Hospital, so
eally some little good has come out of that fund
ISaines of subscribers have been forwarded to. the
Duchess “in a handsome book of white vellum ”
and May said the conventional thing through the
lady in-waiting—“ expressed herself pleased.”
There won’t be a vacant corner in any of the
royal garrets when the couple get home with their
swags of addresses and views.
The snuff-box given by the Duke of York to
Mr. Barton is big enough to hold a couple of
small sandwiches quite easily. No one takes
snuff now save a few old persons in the Benevo-
lent Asylum, and I hope Toby won’t contract the
habit just in order to live up to his Box. I’ve
been trying to think how he could make it useful
°r does a thing so beautiful as that need to be
useful . The lid of this perfect work of art is a
plate of gold enamel led in royal blue, over which
are delicate gold scrolls set with six large dia-
monds The centre of the lid holds three Prince
of Wales feathers fashioned by over 200 tiny
diamonds, and surmounted by a crown of more
white brilliants under which shows the glow of
red enamel. The jewelled Garter with its Honi
soit legend encircles the feathers, and the buckle
looks as if done in diamond-dust, so tiny are the
stones. The sides of the box are in exquisite re-
lief work. It weighs close on 90z., and is said to
be worth about t'4oo. It is a wondrously delicate
example of the jeweller’s art.
he half-caste Minister Carroll, who stands by
tym , 01 their M.L. travels, translates the
Duke s speeches, putting in all the imagery and
poetry that the Maori likes. “ I thank you for
the splendid welcome you have accorded me, and
for the unflinching loyalty so nobly emphasised ”

the Jack had Jills in it. And how the originator

leak out before the Couple had left Australia ! It
would have been pounced upon. Lord Ranfurly,
the Gov. of M.L., who left Sydney suddenly after
attending the first royal dinner-party at Govern-
ment House, never explained his “ mysterious
disappearance” hence, but it leaked out that
there was no room for him at Govt. House. And
he couldn’t afford the Australia. The Ranfurlys
are far from opulent, and that lemon-ranch of
theirs at Mildura, where they long lived, is not
a mine of wealth.
Thrfee bachelor girls in Wellington, M.L.,
locked up their cottage on a recent holiday and
went on a triune picnic. But rain drove them
home in time to surprise two boy burglars, who
were in the eldest “bachelor’s” bedroom, where
the consolidated funds are kept. “We know
who the boys are,” she writes, “as one of them
was so green 111 the profession as to bring his dog
with him—a long, low, yellow mongrel, not unlike
a rat; and this animal was sitting at the gate
when we came home, and waited patiently for his
master for a long time, not knowing that the
youthful Bill Sikes had departed in a hurry via
the roof I knew the dog by sight, as it is so
nmd I told the police. A very handsome
detective waited on me yesterday and informed me
that I would have to give evidence against the
boys, as the foorce is anxious to break up
the gang which operates in our ward. They
had waded in to jam and cake—all home-made, so
1 think they must have suffered enough in conse-
quence to atone for the housebreaking, but the
police don’t think so. I wonder how I shall have
j 113I 13,ve 111 col| rt ? I have not been there before,
and do not know the etiquette.” Now if it had
been appearing before the Yorks

had to pay 15s. for the cab, so take it out in the
supper, Nelly.”

Miss Jennie Maynard, who is “Nursing” her
own co. at Sydney Royal, is an Australian, but
tor the last ten years, save for an interlude when
she came here with Musical Sketches, she has been
touring m England and Africa. Theatre-goers
who haunted Essie Jenyns’ performances will re-
call Miss Maynard as a girl (playing small parts)
in them and being often mistaken for sweet Essie
herself. A stage-struck school-girl came over
trom M.L. in- those days just to see the Jenyns,
and when she went back she talked also of one of
the company who had a most rare speaking voice.
Between then and now the Maori often wondered
it that beautiful voice had gone—whether out of
its owner or to foreign parts. Voice and woman
have not parted. You can see and hear them in
' Nurse ” Maynard.
N urse strikes me as one of the most
lU lj° roUS things we have had in this Duke-
saddened country for a long time. And it
was written by a woman, same woman,
Dio Graves, who wrote “Mother of Three,”
by which the Broughs are said to have

too, like a man, sometimes, in greeting her friends.
You won t go to “Nurse ” to see Miss Maynard’s

scape picture.
But Miss Minnie Brandon wears a gown and
toque that remind you somehow of an edition de
luxe of the Seven Deadly Sins. The toilette
harmonises beautifully with the charming hussy
grass-widow part she plays—a skirt and bodice
of canary glace silk barred from waist to hem, with
bands of wall-flower velvet, and draped down the
front with a transparent cloud of yellow silk.
Bodice of same color with like bars, and a lot of
rippling lace at the neck. It’s those bars, I think,
that label Mr.s. Walker Wilson “Sinister.” No,
I think its her big toque—the angle at which it is
worn—also her old rose-silken underskirts and
\xr* "I 1 ee^ shoes. These are so suggestive of the
World (a primrose one) the Flesh (pretty and pink
and ivlute) and the Devil (really not a very wicked
demon—just an imp). When Mrs. W.W. talks
of her flat you feel sure it is beautifully furnished
and that the light comes through rose shades
and that her tea is good.
