I took a tour round the Sydney Hospital dis-
pensary the other day, and have come to the con-
clusion that the great enjoyment of a certain
section of the community consists in taking
medicine, To many of them—women especially—
a visit to the out-door department of the hospital
partakes of the nature of a social call and a public
appearance combined. The social element is sup-
plied by the one lydy that the other lydy talks to
while waiting to have her case dealt with and by
the general exchange of symptoms, while the
sense of public importance arises from the fact
that the case may prove to be an interesting one
which will lead half-a-dozen well-known medical
men to gather round and make the lydy a kind of
scientific institution. Sometimes this blaze of
fame causes her to lose her head. “ Are you
married?” asked a doctor of one smiling but
flurried-looking dame. “ Oh, yes, sir.” “Now,
what about your husband?” “Oh, he’s married’
too,” she responded. A little girl, with torchon
lace pinafore and squashy hat, was an object of
admiration among her comrades outside because
she had got a big bottle of medicine to be took,
and had “ ’er nyme on the ’orspital books,” and
was altogether a public character. The joy of
medicine-taking, hovvever, was not visible in the
face of a worn-looking woman with an ana?mic
baby. “I’ve come for me ’usband,” she explained
on being questioned ; “’e’s lost an eye.” “How
was that? YVhere was he working?” “Oh,’e
wasn’t workin’; ’e was playin’ cricket, and the
u R an d the eye ’ad to betaken out because
the other was beginnin’ to go out of sympathy,
the doctor says. But, thank Gawd, the other eye
is pickin’ up wonderful now. And p’r’aps he’ll be
able to play cricket again, after all; and maybe,”
as an afterthought, “do a bit of work between
times.” Poor woman !
The Chinese people use their ears for recepta-
cles, but it was left for a Society lady, the other
day, to find a still more curious way of disposing
of her threepenny-bit. She was quite the genuine
thing, this Society lady, and entered the tram
only by a sort of accident. She had the fashion-
able silk-garbed undulating back (the old idea of
looking as if you were built into your clothes is
quite out of date nowadays, a small perpetual
wriggle all over one’s anatomy having replaced
the calm of Vere de Vere.) This lady’s wriggles,
however, became 30 pronounced as to rather
exaggerate the fashion, and, as the tram-man
waited patiently for his threepence, she was
observed to lengthen her features, then to shorten
them, while she seemed to be doing strange inward
calisthenics with her tongue. Then she despair-
ingly took out her top set of teeth, and extracted
the small necessary coin from behind two molars.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“ The dignified ‘Lady Munro’ departed from
Melbourne the other day, and now probably glad-
dens Sydney with her sweet smile.
“ Australians were surprised to find the Dook
such a doleful little dullard, ’cause last time he
came he was the merriest of lads. At Albany
where owing to a ship break-down, he and his late
brother spent a gay fortnight ashore, our recent
visitor used to play cricket like mad, and, though
a knock-kneed little chap, was an active fielder.
In the same pretty town, when the Royal boys
were yarded in to an ‘At Home,’ the younger
drew a bevy of gushing spring-chickens aside,
and, pointing to his brother enthroned on a chintz
sofa, said, * See here, you girls ought to cut out
that piece of stuff Eddy’s roosting on. A pin-
cushion, made of thingumbob sat on by the
future king would be awfully jolly ! ’ There was
a scissored rush for the seat as soon as it became
vacant, but the hostess firmly repelled the raid on
the plea that she wished to reserve the hallowed
spot as an ancestral holy-of-holies. When the
princes were out of hearing she was more candid
and confessed she couldn’t afford to destroy her
new cretonne couch.
“ Another time, joker George induced his mild
elder brother to have a design tattooed on his
nose. The picture was purposely not pricked in,
so that it soon wore off, but there was an awful
scare and corrobboree at Sandringham and
Windsor when the boys’ tutor first wrote the
tidings, and English newspapers were soon
officially inspired to announce that the heir to the
throne was not p :rmanently disfigured.
“A fashionable dress-builder with rooms in
Collins-street is basking awhile in the sunshine
of Fame. It happened in this wise. Janet Lady
Clarke, on hearing from the modiste that she had a
birthday-book with a real original V.R. signature
in it, suggested that she (Janet-Lady) should pro-
cure the glittering autographs of the Yorks.
Moieover, she bore the book (bolstered with
quotations from the ‘ works ’ of the late Princess
Alice) to headquarters, and secured through
Captain Better Not the desired names. Last week,
by which time the news had leaked out, there was
a perfect bombardment of Press pars recounting
the sweet story of the birthday book, and so the un-
expected heroine kept her notoriety warm by giv-
ing a big tea and sing-song at her rooms recently,
when machines and needles were still for the day.
Practically, the invitations to friends and custo-
mers was to meet the Honored Book. They came
early and gazed at the signature.
The book aforesaid supplies some droll impres-
sions. Large as life, in picturesque characters, is
‘Richard PlantagenetNevill,’ probably the oldest
family represented, royalty included in the asser-
tion. Another droll thing is the names of Lord—
um—avv —Brassey’s first and second wives. The
former was secured in England many years ago.
The dainty De Vere signature was one of
more recent addition. ‘My sister in Sydney
introduced me to Earl Beauchamp, who offered,
when hearing of the birthday book, to write his
name in it,’ says the enterprising possessor. ‘He
asked me if 1 would like any others.’ “Cer-
tainly,” I replied, “if you can vouch for their
respectability.” The quiet and business-like lady
has some humor. At one time her family were
wealthy station-owners in N.S.W., but times of
travel and luxury, like all uncertain joys, come to
an end, and the girls have been doing for them-
selves. Miss Darchy, long associated with Sydney
Daily Telegraph, is a sister of the birthday book
owner.
“ A Collins-street*dressmaker was the first to
pass a cheque signed by Royalty into a Melb.
bank. Her entrance with the document marked
an epoch in the history of this young country, or
something like that, and the officials gathered
round in numbers and gazed with awe and admira-
tion on a previously ordinary customer.
“Italian Opera grows exciting. When scribe
sampled ‘ Lucia,’ a man understood the singers to
object to Australians wearing swords. He took
this so badly that a policeman and an usher had to
drag him out, and the trio came tumbling over the
d.c. seats like an avalanche. As the fighter went
through the door, he tore down curtains and rods;
next day he was fined £4.

a cat lost her temper. Perhaps she looked upon
some of these vociferous de Benedetto notes as
take-offs of feline music. Anyway, she ramped
round the d.c. miawling distractedly, jumped
from balcony to stalls, and when last seen, was
still running.

Mdme. Sapio and hubby of the bald head and up-
turned moustaches. The vocaliste’s cream gown

off the stage than on. Janet Lady Clarke with

pressive white-brocade pelisse, and appeared the

“ Although the Wiedermann party on the open-
ing night of the Williamson Opera Co. took the
treat without any apparent enthusiasm, but seem-
ingly rather with the critic’s pinch of salt, Mar-
shall-Hall sat in one of the stall-seats and holloa’d
out ‘Bravo’ at every opportunity, clapping in a
way that would put Jove and his thunder-bolts to
shame; he generally behaved himself as if he
could relish heaps of ‘ Aida.’ In fact the apostle
of Wagner set all the stallites crazy with enthu-
siasm. Can the House of Israel be divided against
itself ?
“A well-known society-woman, smarter than
the majority, is starting a palmistry-business in
town. Spiteful people declare she picked up the
tricks-of-the-trade by seizing everv opportunity of
having her hand read at swagger “At Homes”
where she longtime shone. Since station-property
has been eaten into by mortgages and things, the
dame has declined in glory, and now she is going
to pitch her tent in Collins-st. and ‘ take the palm ’
for pluck.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Premier See, whom Sydney Daily Tele-
graph so persistently abuses, holds a substantial
share interest in that paper. f
Australia follows American example in
becoming “a. nation of colonels.” In Sydney,
the Laue-Mullins family already numbers three
live captains.
There is a second George Dibbs in Aus-
tralia. No. 2is a farmer at Bungaree, near Bal-
larat (Vic.), and his hobby is not carving walking-
sticks for crowned heads, but growing giant
turnips.
According to Sydney Freeman’s Journal
no coadjutor has yet been appointed to the R.C.
archdiocese of Sydney. The announcement of the
appointment of Monsignor Kelly, rector of the
Irish College at Rome, appears to have been pre-
mature.
Premier Seddon was very ill again quite
recently, but he now feels better. All the same,
he works and travels day and night, has a hard
session in front of him, and will come to a sudden
stop very shortly should he not take a strong pull
at himself.
The Sydney R.C. clerical organ says that
“ Cardinal Moran speaks fluently all the European
languages. Modern Greek and Italian he knows
like a native. Of course he is a well-known
Hebrew scholar, having been Professor of Hebrew
in Rome.”
Some members of the Commonwealth
Ministry wish to give the Federal Chief-J ustice-
ship to slippery Sam Griffith ; others favor Synmn,
who fought so manfully against the Privy Council
solieme, and there is said to be more or less of a
deadlock on the subject.
June 15 was the 58th anniversary of the
first N.S.W. Parliamentary election: Only one
of the 24 members then elected now lives —Dr.
Charles Nicholson, who was one of the five repre-
sentatives for Port Paillip. Dr. Nicholson will
be 93 years of age Nov. 23.
When York quitted Brisbane without
leaving so much as a K.C.M.G. behind, the dis-
appointed grovellers said : “ Wait until his birth-
day.” And they are still waiting and hoping
against hope, and not even a star of the 47th
magnitude shows on the hoi’izon.
The late James McKean was, in his day,
probably the tallest man in Melbourne. His son
and partner ran him close, but both had to look
small alongside young 'Kiddle (also in the law),
who is a growing lad of 23, measuring 6ft. Sjin.
He is evidently destined to be a very high legal
authority.
King O’Malley, revives the yarn that
the old Victorian Parliamentary Mace passed
into the hands of “some of Solomon’s lilies” at
Boom-time. One meets many men in Melbourne
who have seen the Mace in mysterious places, and
for a long time it was reported to be the favorite
weapon of a Nicholson-street “ cliucker-out.”
Bertram, Brough’s clever necromancer at
Melb. Athenaeum, tells an Age interviewer that
he taught King Edward (when the latter was P.
of W.) howto do the three-card trick. Albert
Edward may have been hard-up at times, but The
Bulletin will never believe he defied his fellow
subjects to find “the little lady Queen for a quid.”
Four distinguished Australians have their
birth anniversaries this week, Archbishop
Murphy, of Hobart, was born on the day that
Waterloo was fought, so that’he is now 86 ; Chief
Justice Griffith dates from 21st June, 1845 ; Presi-
dent Baker from 22nd June, 1842; and Premier
Richard Serldon also was born on that date but in
an earlier year.
THERE AND BACK.
TICKET-COLLEOTOR : “ Have you a return ticket? ”
EXCURSIONIST: “Yes! ar' so ’us my lunch!"
Federal Trade Minister Kingston has been
slaving over the making of the tariff, and he has
often kept his subordinates in the Custom House
until past midnight, while, on one occasion, he
went on until 4 a.m. South Australians know
this industrious trait in Kingston’s character, but
it is a revelation to the Victorian politicians and
officials. All the same, the Minister is looking
thoroughly worn-out, and his friends, are very
anxious concerning his health.
Lieut. S. S. Reid, of the Westralians,
seriously wounded what time Bigjohn s nephew
was killed, was the Scotchbyterian meenister at
Boulder when the war started. Got a taste of the
business as chaplain to an early contingent; and,
no professional man of God being wanted with the
last lot, went as a professional man-killer instead.
Difficult to go right in: the presence of
royalty. Mayor Ware, of Adelaide, is much
laughed at for having kissed the Duke of York’s
hand when it was given him to shake. Yet a
recent book of reminiscences bases one of its
funniest stories on the fact that the Mayor of
Oxford shook Queen Adelaide’s hand when it was
extended to him to kiss.
Had Senator Higgs’ motion, debarring
M’sP. from taking office as Supreme Court Judges,
been the law in New South Wales, that State
would not have had Martin and Darley as Chief
Justices, nor Wise, Hargrave, Faucett, W. M.
Manning, Windeyer, Innes, or G. B. Simpson as
puisne Judges. Several of these were valuable
men ; the others could well have been spared from
the Bench.
A breezy Maoriland paper started a yarn
that Premier Seddon was to be appointed to a
colonial Governorship, and other journals took
the matter up seriously. Of course the thing is
all flam. Seddon, a born fighter, would be quite
out of place as a peaceful vice-regal ornament.
Also, it’s about the last position he’d hanker after,
so long as he could hold his present autocratic
control over M.L.
“ David Blair ” (writes a Sydney Catholic
Press contributor) “had a big head like every
intellectual Irishman, but lie told me that he put
Deniehy’s hat on one day and it fell on his shoul-
ders, and then Deniehy put on Parkes’ hat, which
nearly hid him from view. Parkes had the biggest
head in Australia, and it is a pity some of his old
hats have not been kept for the museum of the
Commonwealth.”
More, O'Sullivanesque glory. The N.S’.W.
Works Minister has now ordered statues (to cost
£l5O each) of Parkes, James Squire Farnell,
Daniel Henry Deniehy, Dr. Lang, and Chief
Justice Martin, to be fitted in the niches outside
the Sydney Lands Office building. “ Sandstone ”
is indeed appropriate to the case of James
Squire Farnell. Who of the present generation
ever heard of him, and why is he to be com-
memorated at an annual cost of £5 or £6 ?
There isn’t a Knight left in S.A. Leg.
Council, and the only one in the Assembly is
Coles, the Speaker. Moreover, unless several
people are romancing, offers came lately to
Premier Jenkins, Gordon, and Catt (old poli-
tician and Chairman of Committees), and all
were declined with thanks. Gordon has his eye
on a Judgeship; and so can’t afford to en-
danger his political life ; Jenkins is too cute ;
Catt is too poor, or thinks he is.
Giant Jimmy M‘Kean, the Melbourne
solicitor, had been palpably marked for death
some little time before his end came. The spirit
of the stentorian battler, who, in days of old,
denounced the members of Vie. Assembly as “ a
drunken and immoral lot,” was tamed by the
chronic rheumatism that had bent his huge frame,
and it was sad to see him pottering about his old
haunts. Yet, until quite lately, the ancient, bent
as he was, carried his years very well.
Said that Dr. Cockburn, who will cease to
be Agent-General for S.A. at the end of next
month, may possibly return to the practice of his
profession, which he gave up when elected to the
Assembly in 1884. He is a gold-medallist of
London University and was making several
thousands a year when he left physic for politics.
It is not unlikely that he will start as a consulting
physician in London, but there will be an effort to
induce him to re-enter S.A. Parliament.
A. S. Cowley, the defeated Queensland
pro-Kanaka Federal candidate, was reared in
Natal, a British colony in South Africa.
Natal contains (besides 787,000 odd abori-
ginals) 53,000 white inhabitants and 61,000
imported cheap Hindoo laborers. This
latter variety of British subject is only
allowed to come in under contract.. He is
allotted to a master by the Protector (! !) of
Hindoo immigrants, and must remain with
that master at least five years. After
that he may hire himself out (with the
consent of the Protector) to a new
master on an engagement of at least two
years, starting at 16s. a month, and
rising by an annual increase of Is. per
month to 20s. per month. He doesn’t get
more than 20s. per month. He isn’t
allowed to leave Natal without a license,
and he can’t get one till he has re-
sided there for at least ten years. He
must wear a badge showing whose servant
he is. If he has no employer he must
pay the Government £3 a year for a
badge. He isn’t allowed in the streets
after dark, and even in the day-time he
isn’t allowed on the pavement. He can’t
have a vote, can’t own land, or hold a
mining claim ; and he can only engage in
trade of any kind under severe restric-
tions. And many of these restrictions
apply also to the Kaffir, the native of
the land. Cowley was brought up amid
this kind of British freedom, and coming
from a country where the imported cheap
black laborer already outnumbers the
white settlers, a piebald community pro-
bably seems to him a natural and de-
sirable kind of community.
King O’Malley’s revival of Victoria’s Miss-
ing Mace scandal gave Matthew Davies an easy
chance to score the advt. his soul hungered for.
The previous Mace Inquiry Board, which decided
that the loss of Matthew’s bauble was a respect-
able mystery, didn’t command much reverence at
the time, but now that the story of the disappear-
ance is crumbling into decay the Mace Board’s find-
ing is the only thing left to swear by ; and Davies,
in challenging King O’Malley to prove the Parlia-
mentary tribunal a farcical thing, threw down a
gauntlet that the other fellow had to leave undis-
turbed. All the same, O’Malley was under no
obligation to look as though the gauntlet had hit
him in the eye.
The largest Queensland Federal con-
stituency is Maranoa, which contains 300,000 sq.
miles, and is, consequently, about as big as N.S. W.
Realising its immensity, said constituency appro-
priately sent Laborite Page to represent it in the
first Commonwealth Parliament. The fitness of
that gentleman is partially due to the fact that he
weighs 18st. 91b., and therewith scores as top-
weight in Australian politics. As a set-off, the
four lightest members are Batchelor, Hughes,
M‘Donald and Tudor, all in the Labor Party.
These four are such lightweights that no two of
them taken together can balance the champion
“ fat ” from the Prismatic State.

Kingston is about the only member of the
Federal Ministry who is not willing to babble to
reporters. His reticence is of long standing, but
is none the less bitterly resented by the searchers
after gossip on the Melbourne press, who are
privately breathing slaughter against him. Under
these circumstances, any little nastiness which
may appear in print concerning Kingston may be
prudently discounted.

has been commissioned by Premier Seri'lon to

M.L. Seddon, by the way, intends turning the
visit of His Royal Shortness into a huge advt. for
the country ; every movement of the bored and
wearied tourists, from arrival to departure, is to
be cinematographed, and the pictures sent to
Hingland and ’Murka.
Symon’s accession to Federal politics means
great financial loss to him, for his legal practice
was worth something like £5OOO a year. His de-
parture, with that of Kingston, Downerand Glynn,
gives several young Adelaide lawyers big openings
Attorney-General Gordon, who, unlike Kingston,
declines to neglect private for political business,
is now practically the leader of S. A. bar, and is
hardly ever out of a big case.
The Dukeryork came into contact with
one Australian who did not go weak in the knees
and betray a desire to squirm in a characteristic
attitude before the Royal boots. He was an old-
mari cockatoo on aGippsland station when the wee
Duke was being led out to shoot tame quail. The
cockatoo, from his perch, spoke treasonable and
derogatory language at the King’s son, and
screamed blasphemy at the whole show in a
terrible falsetto voice, till a local light went with
knocking knees and a clammy brow, and, stifling
the clamor of the seditious bird in his robe, bore it
swiftly from the Presence.
From Sydney Star :
One of the pure-bred bulls recently imported to Maori-
land is to be sent to the West Coast. The settlers intend
christening it “ Dick Seddon.”
A compliment indeed ! Which recalls that an
Australian city newspaper once published an
enthusiastic leading article based on the assump-
tion that a certain local politician’s sobriquet of
“ Honest Tom ” was derived from the public’s ap-
preciation of his conspicuous personal rectitude.
Whereupon a beastly country correspondent wrote
to say that the statesman in question was re-
christened after a well-known stud bull.
In “ Recollections of Government House,”
by G. S. Fort, recently printed in- the Empire
Review, there is a story of how a girl, introduced
at a dance in England to a young Queenslander,
auspiciously led-off by asking the astounded
youth: “Is your tribeat peace with the white
man now?” Related also how, when some Jap
naval officers made a semi-official call upon the
A.D.C., they sat on for weary hour after hour,
until it occurred to somebody that, according to
Chrysanthemum etiquette, the unfortunate little
men would have committed a gross rudeness if
they had taken their leave without being asked to
do so.
The Bulletin now hears that Premiers See
and Peacock were never offered knighthoods at all
—plainC.M.G. was the bauble, the only bauble, ten-
dered for their acceptance. Heaven and earth were
moved to make Chamberlain relent, but he was
immovable. The investiture ceremony was ac-
tually postponed, in the hope that a second
imploring cable might soften his adamantine
heart, but he again refused to budge. Very likely
the Colonial Office, narked at the derision now
poked at Imperial titles by the populace of Aus-
tralia, and smarting at the Australian press
assumption that titles were to be as common as
dog-mange during York-time, has determined to
bull its bauble-stock.
Lord Hopetoun is, apparently, making a
strong bid to become a genuine Australian in the
f u n Ben se of the word. Casual visitors to Mel
bourne Govt.-House during the recent Royal visit
were surprised to find the Governor-General
personally supervising everything, and issuing
directions to unintelligent subordinates with a
vehemence that would have done credit to an old-
time bullocky. The quality of His Ex.’s blanki-
ness is not strained, and vivid adjectives now roll
from the vice regal mouth with the fluency that
comes only of long and arduous practice. People
who have opportunities of observing him in Lis
hours of ease say that he had difficulty in restrain-
ing himself from referring to the transcontiblanky-
nental railway in his first speech to the Common-
wealth.
Acting-Judge Pennefather, of Westralia,
to whose appointment strong objection was taken
on the simple ground of lack of qualification, has
done one sensible tiling—granted an applica-
tion for the hearing of a Supreme Court action on
the goldfields, instead of dragging parties, wit-
nesses, &c., down to Perth. But the goldfield
accused must still lake his chances from the resi-
dent beak. Judge Pennefather will be re-
membered as the lawyer who, when a very young
man, so strenuously defended Cordini (he was
convicted and hanged) for alleged murder, before
the late Mr. Justice Windeyer. Mr. Pennefather
shares with G. H. Reid and E. P. Field the dis-
tinction of being the only counsel who ever
really “stood up to” Judge Windeyer in a cri-
minal case.
Hardly a Senator fails to stumble hope-
lessly when mentioning in debate Senator Sir
Josiah Symon.
The deaths'of Walter Besant and Robert
Buchanan were cabled on the same day last week.
The former was—barring, perhaps, Anthony
Trollope, who used to go regularly to his office,
and work at his novels as if he were a bank-clerk
—the ideal British Workman of Letters. He
followed, longo intervallo, in the steps of
Dickens when Dickens was haunted by the
spectre called Moral Purpose. Besant never re-
covered the loss of his collaborator, Rice.
Dick Mortiboy and Mr. Brinjes will keep alive
when the other lay figures constructed by Besant
have been tenderly carried out into the lumber-
room of oblivion. Robert Buchanan was another
sort of writer. He was born in Stafford—the pot-
tery county—of Scotch parents, and was a fine
embodiment of the irascible perfervidum ingenium
Scotorum. His best novels—“ The Orchardsons, ”
“God and the Man”—were models for Hall
Caine. R.B. wrote the current language of the
British people better than most Scotsmen, who,
when they are not writing dialect, are too beauti-
fully correct, and remind one of a pretty Welsh
girl talking book-English. Buchanan’s poetry is
finer than his prose. Some of it had a certain lilt
and gallant Border-ballad air, very refreshing in
these times of teacup emotion and greenhouse pas-
sion. Buchanan had also a certain gift of humor—
“ Widow Maisie,” for instance. He was, more-
over, a somewhat irritable person who took him-
self seriously. But he made a mistake when he
quarrelled with Yates, of the World. Yates’s
retort is famous : “ This man caine to me a hungry
and scrofulous Scotchman. I gave him food for
his belly, and sulphur for his back—and see how
he turns on me ! ”

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —Always give, in ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., exact date and exact
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
COMPREHENSIVE.
APPARTTION AT DOOR : “ For God’s sake, boss,
give us a drink. I’m that hungry I don’t
know where to sleep to-night.”
“G.R.” : The Cannonbar (near Warren,
N.S.W.) blacks have—or had—an albino gin, with
white skin and pink eyes. She is absolutely
blind, and the colored community are so proud of
their freak that they never let her walk a foot
but carry her everywhere. Have heard that a
tribe near Queensland border includes another
specimen. ,
• * * *
S.C.O.J. : An old bachelor gentleman
had a station on the Condamine, where scrub-
turkeys were plentiful. He managed to secure
a y? un S 0110 > and to rear it. The garden in front
ot the house was a very fine one for the bush, and
the old chap took a great pride in it. When his
pet turkey was still in the flower of its youth ho
went to Brisbane on business. When he returned
his garden was in a heap in the centre. The
turkey had found a mate, and, unbeknown to
anybody, had started in to make a “ nest.” There
was not a flower left, or a bed left untouched, and
the garden was about 20yds. square. There was
no “ Hamlet’s soliloquy” about the old gent when
he saw how matters stood. He shot ’em both
with one barrel.

Dear Bulletin. —Was recently surprised
to see a very distinct rainbow on a cloudy moon-
light night. Always thought that only the sun
caused rainbows.— Wilcow. [Moonlight rainbows
occasionally occur. The subject was here dis-
cussed at length about two years ago.—Ed.]
“Pelican”: Belyando niggers run totem
bm forall it is worth. For instance, if an able-
bodied nigger dies up there, witchcraft is always
held accountable. The cadaver is buried; fine
wood-ashes are carefully smoothed over upturned
soil, and for a certain time friends daily visit the
giave in search of evidence. Should tracks, say
ot a curlew, be found on ashes, then assuredly one
of the tribe which owns the curlew as a totem has
been guilty of black art. Thus feud arises ’twixt
the bandicoot and curlew factions, if deceased was
a bandicoot man.
* * * *
“A.H.L.”: In reply to “ Boka ” (8.,
1/(5/ 01) re ant-lion, I _ much doubt whether his
method of capture, viz., blowing sand away, is
practicable. When constructing his trap the
lion ” invariably provides a tunnel into which he
very rapidly moves when disturbed. Have tried
many ways to catch the insect (blowing among
them), but the only successful one was to fasten
an ant to a piece of cotton, drop it into the trap,
and steadily haul out ant with “ lion ” hanging-on
tenaciously. Further, the bite of the “lion” is
very deadly. Even the wiry bull-dog-ant is a
corpse within 10 secs, after being attacked.
* * * *
Pine Hill (C.Q.) 15 years ago was a pros-
perous town of 2000 inhabitants and 27 pubs. It
was then the head of C.Q. Western railway. The
hne was pushed on a few score miles and Pine
Hill wilted away. To-day not a vestige of the
old town is visible, scrub is again taking posses-
sion, and all is gloom.

“ Cecil P.” ; Does any Bulletin reader
know anything of a timber discovered by
Buchanan and Collins at head of Xmas Creek,
Beaudesert district (Q.) Consists of one big-tree
with half-a-dozen smaller ones growing round it—
all from the one bole, boss, or root. Finders call
it “ red beech.”

“ D.H.” : Re travelling. I know a man
who came from Barraba, via Cobbadah, to Wal-
gett, a distance of about 240 miles, in eight
days. He carried a fairly heavy swag, and lived
on bread and water the whole time. Between the
two places he spent Is. 3d., and that was in grog.

* » * *
A lot of mummified remains have recently
been found in catacombs under some high lime-
stone rocks near the head of the Awaroa River
(M.L.). In the dark recesses of a cave into which
two tourists from Auckland peered they beheld
bodies with scattered locks of long black hair.
Some of the bodies, upon closer inspection, proved
almost as complete as when laid away shortly
after death in the unknown past. It is said that
the late Captain Fairchild always expressed a
doubt as to the existence of these mummies, and
some of the oldest European residents have
shared in his disbelief, but from the necessarily
superficial peep into the catacombs mentioned,
and what was then seen, there is sufficient warrant
for further investigation. The remains are be-
lieved to be those of a race unknown to the
Maoris.

“J. W.C.” : “Scotty” (B. Ij6/’01) states
that well-placed rabbits can't be shifted, and gives
a lot of calculations to controvert my assertion.
(B. 16/3/’01) that I can exterminate every rabbit
on a sq. mile of country with one bag of pollard.
Now, where “ S.” makes the mistake is in assum-
ing that on, say, a run of 900 sq. miles the whole
would be so infested that it would need a bag of
pollard to every mile, and also that it would be
necessary to fence the country into small areas..
This is not necessary under my method. I can.
get the same result on 1000 sq. miles as on one sq.
mile. Again, “ 8.” states that rabbits will, after
a time, get tired of phosphorus. Well, my experi-
ence is that once I have laid the poison for the
bunnies tl.eie are none left to get tired.
Dear Bulletin.— “Ben
Sun’s ” statement (B. ll/5/’Ol)
that “ Gorang’s” remarks on
the Australian “ chaffing ”
habit, brand the latter as
out of sympathy with Aus-
tralian character and
“prurient-minded ” is un-
satisfactory. Those who
find no zest in life, even
Out-back, save in the
cracking of coarse jokes,
are justly condemned and better
dead. Further, it is open to
argument whether persons of
weak mind are properly the
prey of others who lack
sufficient intellect to dis-
play a deficiency or eccen-
tricity. The fact that the
practice of chaffing is fre-
quently malicious and at-
tended with disastrous re-
sults is undeniable. Many
unfortunates, merely “soft”
at first, are quickly driven
to extreme lunacy by no-
thing else than the brutal
badgering defended by
“Ben Sun.” Example—
and this is a real case: Bill-
jim is “ weak ” on women.
Someone starts the ball by
telling the butt that Miss
Soanso (living in the
township) is “ shook ” on
him. At the instigation
and dictation of his
‘ ‘ mates ” he writes a
letter, the contents of
which would stagger a
Moll Flanders. This, of
course, never leaves the
station, but. an answer is faked by people in the
know. This sort of thing proceeds for a time.
Finally, an elopement i 3 arranged. At the
last moment comes a letter from her father;
all has been discovered, and dire and awful ven-
geance is threatened. Flustered and cruelly
“prompted,” he assigns his turn-out, dog, and
even his credit-balance in the station books. He
shaves, changes his name and makes a general ass
of himself in the way of disguises, to avoid detec-
tion. Next he is sent out to a distant tank to
“plant” on his own, also to escape detection.
(Just imagine the poor, distracted wretch —his
addled brain teeming with grotesque theories as
to pursuit, capture and punishment—alone in a
solitude, that is truly desolation.) A jovial
trooper is retained to intensify the ecstasies of the
baiting ; chases are indulged in that give the most
exhilarating pleasure to the pursuers and keen
distress to the pursued—and so on, ad lib. All
very amusing, maybe, but pitiful too, I think.—
Louis M.

Humors of the drought : 140 inches
(nearly four yards) of rain has fallen at Geraldton,
N,Q., since Ist January.
* • * *
The break-up of the drought 1 Yet on
one Lower Darling station they opened June with
30 men constantly engaged cutting and carting
scrub to keep life in what is left of the flocks.
The sheep-man knows that at this time of year
such means the loss of practically the whole of the
lambs. Artificial feeding is out of the question.
The river is a mere ditch of green water, impas-
sable by even a rowing flat-bottom, and chaff could
not be landed there at much under £lO a ton.

Scraps from the letter of a farmer in the
drouthy Victorian mallee, published in a Melb.
daily:—
Your letter came to ua aa we struggle in the tentacles
of a drought. We are now carting water for 1300 sheep,
65 head of cattle and 12 horses, and nearly all this water
has to be raised 120 ft. and carted four miles. . . No rain
here for nine months. Plains like rolling deserts of sand,
blood-red and bare. . . While Melbourne has gone
screaming mad over a crowd of ducal satellites and vice-
roys with their feasting and champagne, here in the grim
depths of the mallee men cart their tankful of filthy
liquid home and thank God if they can give their stock
one decent drink a week.

Charters Towers papers tell how a young
man was recently run over by a bullock-waggon
bearing a six-ton lead. The unfortunate fellow’s
feet were terribly crushed, and he was 150 miles
from the nearest hospital— at Ingham. Twenty-
six comrades (miners) made a litter and carried
their poor mate, in relays of four, to this distant
haven.

“Snell” : Baldwin Spencer, writing from
Alice Springs or thereabouts, remarks upon the
unimpressionable nature of the Australian
aboriginal, who is rarely perturbed or even sur-
prised at the wonders of science. He takes the
telephone, the phonograph, the camera, or the
magic lantern just as they come, as either simple
tricks or manifestations of spirits. I remember
an effort being made to amaze a tribe at Alfred-
ton, near Ballarat, 30 years ago. A wandering
Italian was bailed up by a number of drovers, and
paid to grind away at his barrel-organ before the
tribe. The nigs listened and were fairly amused,
but King Billy only grinned far and wide when
the drovers tried to convince him that there was
something supernatural about the organ. “No
fe’ I no fe’ !. ” said his Majesty, “ mine know how
you work him! there, look!” He sprang up, seized
a tomahawk, and cut a small hole through
the shell of a hollow log. Then he grabbed one of
his mongrels, crowded the protesting animal into
the log, pulled his tail through the hole, and
turned industriously at the tail as the dago
had done at his organ-handle. Result: Fearful
howls from the log, and triumph of Billy’s demon-
stration of the true inwardness of a hand-organ.

A RECORD .
DOHERTY : “ That's a foine hen, Flannigan.
Sometimes she lays double-yolked eggs.”
FLANNIGAN : “ Thot’s nothing. Don't I lay drain-
pipes, begobs, wery day in the weck!”
“ anything that talks.” David Christie Murray,
when appearing in Geelong with a distress-
ful dramatic co., went out shooting with Harry
St. Maur. They tramped for hours without find-
ing anything fit to kill, and finally struck a dirty
old man cockatoo dreaming in a tree near an
isolated pub. The litterateur was vicious after
many disappointments, and, taking careful aim,
banged both barrels at cockie, who fell to the
ground. Running to secure his booty, Murray
was met by a blast of profanity. The cockatoo,
who had only been frightened off his perch, stood
up, every feather curled with righteous indigna-
tion, crying shrilly :—“Oh, you ! you !
you ! ” Harry St. Maur used to nearly kill
himself laughing over this incident. He used to
say : “By gad, for the moment Murray thought
it was the devil—or his wife ! ”

“A.A.A.” : Have heard that old-time
carriers used to put a sheet of iron on top of a
cask of spirits (brandy) and build a fire on it.
After a time the fire could cause the spirit to press
up through the seams, ultimately filling the space
on top of the.cask. The extraction of beer from a
cask is mere child’s-play. One method is to knock
the top hoop up—thus opening the staves a little.
The cask is then tipped over a tub and let leak.
When enough has been withdrawn, a bit of
coopering soon replaces the hoop, and nobody
could swear the cask had been tampered with.
“ COBBERS
A sketch on the Mungindi road.
“ D.H.R.” : I have noticed that poison-
weed grows mostly after rains or floods. It
seems impossible to eradicate it where it has once
obtained a root. I know abend in the Macquarie
River, where poison-weed grew thick 30 years ago ;
and, to this day, never a mob of stock camps there
without losing some of its number. On Stony
Plain, on the Warrego River, poison-weed first
grew after the ’64 flood, and it’s still there, but it
never spreads to the adjacent country. It is puce
underneath, and green, tinged with red, on top.
Is known amongst carriers and horsey men as
“ tricky-weed,” or Puce Devil.
HIS COUNTRY—AFTER, ALL.
He was out of work and hard-up, and he yearned to get
away,
So he ’listed with the Bushmen for the four-and-six a
day ;
He’d grown deadly tired of everything ’twixt Sydney-side
and Perth,
And he reckoned that our townships had the queerest
names on earth:
For there’s Adjungbilly, Bean Ba, Ballengarra, Bamarang,
Oabramatta, Cambewarra, Cullubumbung, Carrawang,
Durren Durren, Dilga, Dungog, Eurongilly, Enwylong,
Ghinni Ghinni, Goonoo Goonoo, Goondiwindi, Gerringong.
He had roamed o’er all Australia, but had never been to
sea,
And the mal de mer that gripp’d him when the winds
were blowing free
Sent him groaning to his cabin, where he lay and wished
him back
’Mong the dusty .towns and townships on the swagnian’s
weary track :
He had sweetest thoughts of Gilgai, Grabben Gullen,
Goolmangar,
Goorangoola, Grogan, Grong Grong, Gumble, Gum Flat,
and Gunbar ;—
Of the beer he drank at Howlong, Heilman's Tank, and
Illabo,
Ingebyra, Isabella, and that sweet town, Ingledow.
Though he fought as strong men battle in the thickest of
the fray,
Still his heart was but half in it, and his thoughts were
far away;
And above the conflict’s clamor, softly to his straining ear
Came the syllables of old names, silver-sweet, and low,
and clear :
Jerilderie, Jembaicumbene, Junee, Jiggi, Jindabyne,
Joadja Creek and Jillib.v, no names were e’er so fine 1
Killawarra, Kookabookra, Knorrit Flat and Kerrabee,
Micketymulga, Milbrulong, Nunnagoyt and Nymagee.
Back again in old Australia, as a hero he was hailed ;
And he proved his heroism, for he faced, and never
quailed,
All the beers they “shouted" for him at the pubs, in
every town —
Never Boer took cover quicker than that tanglefoot went
down !
He got full at Adaminiby, Bongongolong, Cooyal,
Dondingalong, Kurunderee, Fassifern, and far Groongal ;
Humula, Ilford, Jamberoo, Ifoorawatha, Tallarook,
Mulleryndgery, Numbugga, Coolongolook !

“ Larrikin ” : Recently struck a cocky’s
homestead. Was hard-up for a read, so asked the
MONS. AND MADAME GERARD Y.
daughter of the house for a book. She asked me
if I had read “Filthy Lucy,” and I was a bit

“ Melb.” : To-day I had the pleasure of
seeing “One Pfeller” —aboriginal, of course —
operate with the boomerang on a vacant acre
almost in the heart of Melbourne, near the corner
of Swanston and Victoria-streets. He had one of
those angular-looking boomerangs, with which
they do the best feats. Leaving his hand, the
weapon skimmed eastward towards the Gaol,
southward towards the old County Court, west-
ward over Swanston-street, northerly to a jam-
factory, and came back right into the thrower’s
hands, like a fluttering pigeon.
Sandy Mac., an old N.Q. identity, is a
“ whisky-terror,” and t’other day, when he was
“ paralytic,” Mrs. Sandy stole his clothes. The
pair have a fat, 13-year-old son, rather short
for his age, while Sandy stands Oft.
2in. —and when the old man came-
to and realised his plight, he
promptly seized the lad, stripped
him of his togs, and presently the
town beheld the apparition of a
long, bony Scotchman quarter-
clad in knickers and a small boy’s
shirt, with a straw hat like a six-
penny-piece surmounting a deter-
mined Caledonian visage and a long
grey beard. In this array Sandy rushed
madly along till he reached a clothing estab-
lishment, whence presently he issued clothed
in smiles and a complete suit of dungaree.
S.M. Herald, June 6 :
Behind the transport, which extended in an irregular
line for miles and miles, came the rear-guard of infantry,
officers and men alike plodding painfully along, ankle-deep
in dust, with dry tongues hanging out and with emply
water-bottles, with blistered feet and tight-strung nerves.
One often hears the expression, allegorically used,
that a “ man has his tongue out for a drink,” but,
in the above extract, it appears to be stated as a
literal fact. Things may be different in S’Africa,
but in Australia men do not put their tongues
out when suffering from thirst. Cattle and dogs
indulge in this little luxury—men and horses do
not. Men and horses perspire through their skins,
cattle and dogs through their tongues. A bullock
is warm and dry to the touch when dead ; a horse,
like a man, is cold and clammy after death. Taking
all things info consideration, a tired and thirsty
man would find it hard work to march along a
dusty road with his tongue out. If S’Africa is
the grave of generals, it is also the grave of the
descriptive writer.
“ Siandra ” on an insectivorous topic :
Re the blowfly maggot, an insect inquired about in
Bulletin (20/4/T). To the best of my belief, the blowfly
maggot bears the same relation to the blowfly that
caterpillars do to moths and butterflies. Every
winged insect, has, at one stage of its existence,
been a caterpillar, grub, or maggot. The larva of
the mosquito is a little, black, wriggling thing, some-
times seen in water drawn from tanks. I have never
seen the blowfly chrysalis, but have seen many of the
jumper-fly maggot, which infests ham and bacon. I am
certain about the dragon-fly, as I have watched it emerge
from the chrysalis, and a pretty sight it is to see the
insect unfolding its gauzy wings and shaking out the
creases. The cherry-green beetle is at one time a grub,
as also the ladybird. Some people may not be aware the
latter is a showy relation of a homely insect, known
variously (in England) as a B. flat, or a Norfolk Howard.
Some beetles, when in the chrysalis stage, remind one
curiously of something Egyptian—l do not know if it is a
peculiarity of them all, nor do I know if the “ average
bushman ’’ will understand this —but I know what I mean
myself, and that is saying something.
• » » »
“Pelican”: Up on Moray Downs (Bel-
yando country, N.Q.), there is a lake, or, rather,
lagoon, some miles in extent, but only a few feet
deep. At one end, the water is good, palatable
drinking fluid, but it gradually tails off in quality
by an admixture of sodium until, at the far end,
some miles off, it is quite impossible for human
use.

“Sabocq”: To “ Ignotus ” (B. 20/4/1),
about the Great Pyramid. The statements made
by Laing are approximately correct. Assume
diameter of circle 100; then radius or vertical
height of pyramid is 50, perimeter of base is 314T6,
and the side of base is 78*54. The altitude of
sloping triangle on this base is easily found to be
63*58, and its consequent area 2497; while the
square of the vertical height is 2500. These figures
are approximate (with the exception of the vertical
height), and if worked out exactly would give
results slightly lower than 2497.

The lately-cabled news that Prof. Picker-
ing thinks he has proved the existence of snow on
the surface of the moon, by means of photography,
is a violent shock to the last summer’s stock of
unsold astronomers. Snow, of course, means
water, and water means some sort of an atmos-
phere, and if there is one point on which science is
reasonably positive in its own mind it is that the
moon is airless. And Pickering’s name stands so
high that his alleged discovery can’t be set down
off-hand to drink.
Dear Bulletin. — To “Ignotus”(B. 20/4/1).
Adopting the rule that the circumference of a circle
is about 3 1-7 times diameter, it follows that the
height of the Pyramid is equal to 7-llths of a side
of the base. The apothem of the Pyramid is the
perpendicular from the vortex on a side of the
base, and forms the hypotenuse of a right-angled
triangle, which has the vertical height of the
Pyramid for its perpendicular and half the length
of a side of the square base of the Pyramid for its
base. Hence the length of apothem is easily
found, and half the product of the apothem and
the side of the base of the Pyramid will give the
triangular area of each of the sloping sides, which
will be found to be as nearly as possible equal to
the square of the vertical height, so that both of
the statements quoted cam be true. —Theodolite.
“ S.S.” : Have run up against three public
subscription-lists for the benefit of relatives of
killed or injured miners during the last four days,
and in each instance the accident was due to
“economic” mining, and the companies were
morally, if not legally, responsible. The mines
are worked cheaply, and the miners’ lives exposed
unnecessarily, in order to produce the largest pos-
sible profit, and then, when stopes come down,
the public is invited in the name of charity to
stump-up the difference.between the cost of risky
working and efficient ditto, for the relief of
the widows and orphans. In the old-fashioned
days stopes were kept filled, hundreds of thousands
of tons of dirt being sent down a blind shaft from
the surface when necessary, and under these con-
ditions the facemen were safe. But these were
antiquated methods; they had to give way to
cheap “ scientific ” systems which save money and
squander lives. There has rarely been a big acci-
dent of late years that was not long anticipated
by the men, and it is about time we had a law
compelling companies to “ bury their own dead.”
When a big mine’s pension-list is a yard long
directors may begin to realise that cheap mining
is only profitable while the public can be induced
to “ hold the baby.”
The war cables • now being dished
out make interesting reading. First we are
told that Kitchener, is drawing a tight “cor-
don ” round the enemy. Next we learn that
Methuen is making a “sweeping” movement
through the Transvaal. Just the other day the
news-manufacturer got a fresh idea. He informed
us the enemy was being “hustled” northwards.
In spite, however, of all these alleged successful
tactics, Hoopstad was besieged for nine months,
and now Bosh of has just been relieved after being
invested for several months. These sieges weren't
even mentioned till they were over. And after hear-
ing for months about the collapse of the Boer resist-
ance, week before last brought news of half-a-dozen
hard-fought engagements with heavy losses. The
casualty-lists issued by the War Office also prove
that the Boer is still-troublesome and still strong,
and in good fighting order.
In these days when modern invention
seems to supply and anticipate every reasonable
want of man or woman, one is tempted to wonder
how our poor forebears, who lived in the
“ glorious good old times,” managed to exist at
all. They, for instance were fain to content
themselves with the crudest of soap and water
ablutions, unsoftened by any of the luxurious
attributes with which we are wont to indulge
ourselves to-day, such as Scrubb’s Cloudy
Ammonia, whose refreshing and invigorating
qualities render it an indispensable addition to
the toilet table. Nor is this the only respect in
which it is invaluable, for its cleansing properties
make its influence on the complexion highly
beneficial. To the economical, this capital
preparation will commend itself as an important
factor in the renovation of old gowns whose front
breadths have become grease-marked or dis-
coloured. Busty black, too, is immensely ben-
efited by a smart rub with Scrubb’s Ammonia
and subsequent hanging up to dry, after which
simple process it will be found to have recovered
a considerable amount of its original freshness.
Altogether Scrubb’s Cloudy Fluid Ammonia
must be reckoned a household boom.*
A SEA FANCY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Oh, why do the steamers swerve about ? ”
She asked as she nestled near ;
“ And follow a course in wondering doubt,
And stumble and stray and veer ? ”
“ These things,” I answered, “ I can’t make out —
But maybe the crew can’t steer.’’
“ I think I know why they do,” she said
(And the man at the wheel could hear) ;
“ ’Tis not a fancy in each ship’s head,
But only a friendly fear
Lest waves be parted though newly-wed,
If courses are followed clear.
‘ ‘ They trip and stumble o’er pitfalls laid
By wavelets that peep and peer
In wonder great that the ships aren’t stayed
M.L.
Or checked in their wild career.
And steamers, hearing, are half afraid
They’ll hurt them unless they veer.
“ And thus do they try to pick their way ”
(Oh ! the man at the wheel could hear) :
“ They would like to stop, hut they must obey
The hand of their engineer ;
A nd the waves that die in a cloud of spray
Are slain hy the men who steer.”
I laughed at her and her pretty ways,
And her fancies sweet and queer,
Yet pray wdll never in future days
Be parted hy sharp hows sheet —
The steamer Death with her hows ablaze
And Fate on her bridge to steer.
QUILP N.


TEE TEMPTER OF RANDAL CAREY.
“The facts relating to Mr. Carey's appointment are very simple. He was repeatedly pressed by the head of the Lyne-See Government to accept the position of Chairman of the Harbor
Trust, at a salary of £2000 per annum for seven years, the holder of that office to be removable only by a vote of the two Houses of Parliament. Mr. Carey just as repeatedly declined ti.e offer.
Premier Lyne then asked him if he would take the Chairmanship of the Rocks Resumption Board, with fees amounting to £500 per annum, being only one-fourth of the salary attached to the
position which he had previously refused. Mr. Carey promised to consider the matter, and eventually refused the position of Chairman. He consented to take a seat on the Board as an ordinary
member, where the fees could not possibly amount to more than £400 per annum.”— Sydney DAILY Telegraph in explanation.

VV. B. Melville to The Bulletin :
In “ Answers to Correspondents ” (15/6,/T) appears this
mysterious announcement : “ C.M.L. : Mr. VV. Bede
Melville is not in any way authorised to speak on behalf
of Tub Bulletin.” Naturally, such an intimation to the
world at large brought me'to your Managing Director,
who assured me he, too, was in the.dark as to the mean-
ing of the message. He knew of no wicked representa-
tions on my part as far as the business and -financial
departments of Tiie Bulletin were concerned. I then
saw the Editor, who explained that the “ answer ” had
reference to inquiries as to whether The Bulletin had
countenanced my Federal candidature for Oanohlas. I
complained that the par should have been explicit. I
was then invited to write this note. Let me state at once
that I never sought the support of The Bulletin for any
candidature, and that neither in a speech nor in a con-
versation in the Canoblas electorate, or elsewhere, did I
state or insinuate that I had the support of The Bulletin.
1 opposed Mr. Wise fairly and openly, and every shilling
spent in doing so was earned with my pen.
I understand that The Bulletin, an admirer of the
Federal se vices of Mr. Wise, wanted that gentle-
man to win, and was annoyed to find that I had attached
to my printed ‘ Declaration of Principles ’ a little bill con-
taining ‘ Opinions of Melville’s candidature,’ in which,
among other expressions, were sets of verses by two of
the best-known of Bulletin bards. For the information
of electors who did not know, I had ‘ Bulletin office,
Sydney,’ attached to the date of the verses. The other
expressions of opinion were similarly identified to give
them localisation. In no way, however, did I desire to
convey that The Bulletin editorially endorsed my candi-
dature. I did, however, desire to point out that two literary
friends were with a fellow press-worker in a very difficult,
if enthusiastic, Federal campaign. The importance of
this matter to myself is my reason for removing a
misconception caused by the anxiety of The Bulletin to
jealously guard its reputation, even at the risk of hurting
the feelings and damaging the good name of one whose
modest contributions to its pages extend over a period of
14 years.”

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
So notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.
0.0. W. : We dunno, but we presume that Mr.
Dan O’Connor is on both sides, as usual... .Mackero : He
was “ travelling round the world moneyless for a large
wager.” Was he ? And you actually believe that vener-
able yarn ! It is almost incredible that people can still
be found to swallow the crude mendacities of such
notoriety-hunting bounders C.M.A. : Olive Schreiner’s
dreams had virile force ; yours show only feminine weak-
ness Katasha : Gloomy lines, apparently written in
a cellar by a frog sitting on a fungus Ah Sin : First
verse of “ Drought” good ; after that metre is as ragged
as if torn by 42 she-bears. “ Morn till Eve ” just plain
wattle verse P. Heagney : It isn’t the want of news,
but the shamelessly-incorrect comments thereon that
make the press the liar it is. Your tremendous scheme
of a State news-supply wouldn’t make Sydney Teleqrapii,
for instance, any more truthful over tariff questions ; and
it wouldn’t have abolished one of the stupendous mass of
falsehoods with which it tried to block Federation. You
are hardly attempting to touch the real difficulty. As
for your proposed State newspaper in N.S. Wales, it
would have sworn by Protection when Dibbs was
in office and bossing it, and sworn by Freetrade
when Reid was in office. And y r et that paper
is to be a model of consistent truthfulness!!....
Relative : Re trooper Swan. His parents live at Quean-
bey an, N.S.W. Letters addressed there will find them....
Y. : But wasn’t the treatment of the body of that brave
young Boer, as described by R.C. chaplain Timoney,
reminiscent of that accorded to the remains of highway-
man Ben Hall? H.N. : The Australians’ doings in what
you call the “ great Boer war ” aren't sufficient to demon-
strate that no foreign nation can ever conquer Australia.
It would be a mighty poor race that wouldn’t do some
bright exploits when fighting on the side that had odds
of eight to one in its favor, with unlimited money and
resources behind that—the side that was absolutely
bound to win. What we want to know is that Austra-
lians are the people who can fight a good battle in a
hopeless cause. They haven’t been tried in that direc-
tion yet. They haven’t even been tried long enough on
the winning side to fully test their staying capacity
Heimsdal: You ask “how many Australians know
whether Pembroke is an English county, and how many
could place Ashby de la Zouche or the Seine ? Well, if it
comes to that, what country is the Dirty Devil River in ?
And how long would it take you to find the Gape of
Eleven Thousand Virgins ?... .Wemsley : The movement
to suspend the Gape Colony Constitution, no doubt,
means that Jingcrdom would like to make all S’Africa into
a big Crown colony, practically under martial rule, until
it can dragoon the people into Imperialism... .Aobus :
The paragraph is inaccurate in several particulars, and if
published might cause unnecessary’ scare : —The number
ofp rsons killed was two, not several. The bodies of the
guff ring could not act as condensers ; the probability is
that they were still in contact with the wire. Death
would be instantaneous and not take three-quarters of an
hour ; also wet does not give an increased power to the
electric current, it merely affords better contact. “Blue
flames emitted from the sufferers’ bodies ” is purely
imagination. Finally', the Liverpool accident need not in
any way alarm the citizens of Si dney and Brisbane....

. ..E.S.S.: Mostly good. Thanks... .T.H.M. : “Smok-

that no man can attend to two occupations, each of which

that “if we are capable of dealing fairly,” we will “ pub-

“roars” with “ paws” that isn’t verse, surely! H.P.
(Ararat) : (1) Two good items. (2) Bulletin said “ the
less government that is needed the better.” Protectionist
laws are urgently needed. As to Protection involving an
extra set of officials, that is absurd. You require much the
same officials under a revenue tariff. Britain is supposed
to be a Freetrade country, and its customs’ staff costs
about £1,500,000 a year. If you have no tariff you require
ttie officials to collect direct taxation. The land-tax re-
quires three times as many officials in proportion to the
cash results as the customs... Cocky : “ Does it ever strike
us that if the Duke of York iiad his Way lie would rather
not be Duke of York at all.” Quite probably. Almost
everybody thinks he has got a hard job, and would be
happier if he was something else. Some of them
get a chance to be something else, and are
very glad to get back to the old job again
Quartpot: Well, where else was the poor bagwoman to
sleep if there was no other bed available ? W.W.
(Boulder) : (1) One-man-one-vote existed in several South
American States and elsewhere before it commenced in
Maoriland. (2) Woman Suffrage has existed to some ex-
tent in Austria for many years. (3) Maoriland did not
lead the world in the matter of old age pensions—in fact,
it is impossible to say when some of these things began,
they are so old. (4) M.L. has universal penny postage.
(5) A great deal of power formerly exercised by State
Governors now belongs to the Governor-General

POLITICAL
POINTS
Sydney D.T. and the Lyne-See Govern-
ment : Arcades ambo !
What a fool Sydney D.T. was to explain !
Just look at all the things it has told people that
they didn’t know before !
Ere long there must be in Sydney a new
daily paper—one with pronounced Labor and
Radical leanings. The situation simply howls for
it.
A question arises whether Victoria can
collect income-tax on the salaries of all Federal
members, these salaries, while the Parliament sits
in Melbourne, being “ money earned in Victoria.”
The war is over ! And De Wet has re-
e ppeared in the vicinity of the Rand with 1000
men. The war is all over—all over the place.
There are now (vide cable) only 17,000 Boers still
in the field, split up into 80 or 100 commandoes.
What a lot of harassment might have been
saved over the question of opening the Federal
Parliament with prayer by simply enacting that
avory member should pray separately before he
arrived, and that the House should take his word
that he had done so.
The first member who raised his voice to
demand that the Federal Government should start
borrowing money was Gould, of N.S.W., who
wanted a loan floated to build nice new post-
offices all over Australia. And the first man who
danounced the loan-disease was Symon, of 9. A.
Sydney Telegraph claims that “ it fought
tooth and nail against the proposal to hand the
Sydney tramways over to a wealthy syndicate ”
and that its “persistent hostility” had “some-
thing to do with the ultimate defeat of the
project.” Wanted, the address of that persistent
hostility.
Cable reports that two Boer prisoners at
Pretoria have been shot for attempting to escape.
Tavist that incident round, garnish to suit the
Jmgo taste, and dish it up to the Imperialistic
SAVING GRACE.
THE SHOUTER (in the sixpenny bar, who has just
discovered that he has only sixpence): “ You
can have what you like, you know, but the
beer’s very nice.”
press, and you get one of “the dastardly and
brutal murders ” so characteristic of the Boers
throughout the war.

Truth :

for the Commonwealth celebrations, and the celebrations
In collection with the visit of the Duke and Duchess of
Cornwall and York. These were unpalatable to them
because they had not the opportunity of doing it them-
selves.

if he claims it, to a higher salary than Gov.-Gen-

More signs of the approaching N.S.W.
State elections:
At the instance of Mr. W. Rigg, the Government has
granted 20 pairs of blankets to St. Peters Benevolent
Society.
Mr. T. Jessep has obtained for the Waverley Benevolent
Society 20 pairs of blankets.
The North Sydney conference of the St. Vincent de
Paul Society has received 20 pairs of blankets on the
representations of Mr. E. M. Clark.
That aged person, D. R. O’Sullivan, who
polled 13,000 votes in the N.S.*W. Senate cam-
paign, is out for Fitzroy for the State Parliament.
His former heavy polling was due to the fact that
thousands of ignorant electors thought they were
voting for E. W. of that ilk.
Should Griffith get the Fedexal Chief-
Justiceship a murky political atmosphere would
be cleared in Queensland. Rutledge, the present
local Attorney-General, would, no doubt, consider
it his duty to ask Hon. A. Rutledge, K.C., to
become Chief-Justice, and this in turn would give
Leahy, the syndicator’s friend, a chance of snar-
ing Philp’s billet. And then the voice of the syn-
dicate would indeed be heard in the land.
“Returned Soldier” writes a grievous
letter to Brisbane Courier, asking :
Was all this professed patriotism in Queensland
genuine ? Are the people of Queensland, after all, pro-
Boer? We find, among other hardships on our return,
that we are disfranchised.
Just fancy a man who went away to battle
(nominally) for political rights for other people in
S’Africa, coming back and finding lie’s got none
of his own. Nemesis !
Geraldton (Q.) World (organ of the
Cowley party) talks in this fashion :
Without antagonism against anything already sanc-
tioned and regulated by law, the N.Q. sugar-growers can
obtain as plentiful a supply of cheap and reliable labor as
they require from his Majesty’s subjects in India.
Perfectly true! Fiji has already tapped this
source very freely, and possesses as a conse-
sequence 17,000 Indian coolies (laborers, artisans,
shopkeepers, &c., &c.)
The present S.A. political craze is to
“foster the producing interests”—by returning
squatters and farmers. 9o squatter Riddoch
takes Laborite M‘Gregor’s place in the Council,
squatter Mortlock takes Shearers’ Union-organiser
Poynton’s in the Assembly, and farmer Russell
succeeds Holder. You see, both Adelaide dailies
feel safe in barracking for “the producer” ; anl
since Bonython was S’langdoned, the ’Tiser has
apparently been growing less and less anxious to
be seen in company with the Labor crowd. So
with no Kingston, M‘Pherson dead, Gregor
M‘Gregor gone to the Senate, and no Press help,
the S.A. Labor-party is about as sick as any party
can be.
After years of litigation, the great
M.L. native land case of Tamaki v. Baker
has been finally argued before the Privy
Council, and judgment given in favor of the
Maoris. The result is, as the late Judge
Richmond said, that “ a cloud hangs over
almost every title in the country.” M.L.
lawyers have always assumed that, by
right of conquest, all the land in the pro-
vince was vested in the Crown. The
Treaty of Waitangi they regarded as a
treaty only in name. Hence they assumed
that the Crown could deal with land that it
had not formally acquired from the native
owners. The Privy Council has reversed
this decision of the M.L. Court of
Appeal. It follows that if the Crown has
not formally acquired from the native
owners the title to their land, the natives
can go behind the grants issued by the
Crown and claim that the land still belongs
to them. An Act of Parliament will pro-
bably be needed to put matters straight.
It is reckoned that it will require
£1,000,000 to bring the dilapidated Aus-
tralian telephone systems into proper re-
pair, and the daily papers most unneces-
sarily state that “ the money will be pro-
vided by loans ” —not paid by instalments
out of the’phone revenue. Yet, what kind
of industry is it that can’t provide the money
to pay its own repair-bill ?
The opening of the Federal House of
Reps, with prayer makes it obligatory that, in
appointing a Speaker, a man not only of decent
life but of admitted theological opinions must be
chosen. Speakers in various Parliaments of Aus-
tralia have been atheists ; most of them have been
Rationalists. The imposition of a prayer-formula
will be discovered to be, in the Australian Com-
monwealth Parliament, as big a blunder as, in its
own sphere, was Edward Vll’s coronation oath.
The new Westralian Ministry consists
mostly of goldfields members—there are four of
them in Premier Leake’s Cabinet. Leake himself
is a Groper, a member of the Six Families, and
nephew of old Speaker Luke Leake, but apart from
him the Groper element in the Cabinet is weak.
Leake’s Government consists largely of South
Australians and Victorians. Treasurer Illing-
worth is English by birth, but his best years were
spent in Victoria, and he is anex-M.L.C. and ex-
boom wreck of that State.
Cabled that Sir William Vernon Harcourt
in a vigorous speech remarked that the British
Parliament, though newly-elected, was already
suffering from all the symptoms of senile decay.
The war he denounced as the greatest disaster of
modern times. It had originated in a subsidised
insurrection fostered in a friendly State (the
South African Republic), accompanied by an
armed invasion, for both of which Mr. Cecil
Rhodes, then Premier of Cape Colony, was
responsible.

MANAGER (to applicant for billet): “Do you
possess any knowledge of the silks and satin
department?”
APPLICANT: “Spent all my life among ’em, sir.”
MANAGER: “And sheets, blankets, dec.?’
APPLICANT (forcibly): “Born among ’em, sir!”

doing a lot of the good democratic work
of the Federal Parliament, has a motion on

go extensively into the resumption of large estates
for Closer Settlement as provided in clause 51, sec-
tion xxxi., which empowers the Commonwealth
to legislate for
The acquisition of property on just terms from any
State or person for any purpose, in respect of which the
Parliament has power to make laws.
“ Vulcan ” : Iti Luidor’s “ conversations ”
I struck this:
“ Alfieri ” : The lumber of our Italian courts, the most
crazy part of which is that which rests upon a red
cushion in a gilt chair, with stars and sheep and crosses
dangling from it, as were Artaxerxes and Domitian.
These automatons, we are told, nevertheless, are very
condescending. Poor fools who tell us it! Ignorant that
where on one side is condescension, on the other side
must be baseness. The rascals have ruined my physiog-
nomy. I wear an habitual sneer upon my face, God con-
found them for it 1 even when I whisper a word of love in
the ear of my donna.
The IS.A. Tories did better than they
knew when they put enough property in the
names of their wives and daughters to qualify the
latter to vote in Leg. Council elections; for the
defeat of Laborites, and Radicals with the Labor
support, besides keeping these horrid people out of
the Council, prejudices the chances of candidates
running on the same ticket for the Assembly.
“ Nothing succeeds like success”; and the Tory
trick, intended only to capture the Council, is
capturing the Assembly as well, or seriously
affecting its character, anyhow.
David Syme’s "great daily” has been
much more fearfully Jingo over the Boer war than
the Argus, and David’s anti-Boer fanaticism
grows fiercer. The Argus prints all Major
MTnerney’s generous praises of the Trans-
vaalers and Orange Free Staters, but the Age,
while printing the speeches, edits out of them
every word of commendation lavished on the
enemy. This is a sad exposure of the methods of
the Age, but Syme trusts to his rival’s compara-
tively small circulation, and is rarely put away.
News is the thing that isn’t printed.
Hearken to the Argus, which a few years
ago bellowed shocking prophecies about the buck-
toothed British tourist who would sit on the ruins
of the Pink Terraces and overlook desolated
Maoriland, ruined by democratic legislation :
The true causes of the prosperity of New Zealand have
nothing whatever to do with what Mr. Lloyd calls the
“ demoeratisation ” of legislation. The same process
might be followed elsewhere without any improvement in
the circumstances of the country occurring. There has
been hardly more than a coincidence.
The prophet who lives to explain why his pro-
phecy did not come off doesn’t know his business.
S.A. Democratic party, as a result of re-
cent by-elections, has gained a seat in the Assem
bly, but almost irretrievably lost its position in
the Council. Coombe, a strong Radical, succeeds
to Downer’s seat, and Russell, one of Kingston’s
old-time supporters, follows Holder. In the
Council, however, squatter Riddoch and Racing-
Secretary Von Doussa, both Ass. nominees,
succeed Baker and M‘Gregor. Instead of having
a slender majority of one in the Tory Chamber,
the Ass. party has now 14 nominees out of 24, and
democratic measures will get short shrift in the
Council for years to come.

lot of indignant Jingoes have been supplying to
that paper the names of the N.S. W. public schools

bunkum enthusiasm.

but in all the other districts there are rival .Labor

some cases the Central Broken HiIIP.L.L. Execu-

ning as independent Laborites.
Hughes, for West Sydney, wants
Freetrade in sugar and the abolition, of cheap
colored labor. Now Hughes surely doesn’t expect
the white man to work for nigger wages if he
does he is a disgrace to his race and his color.
And he can’t expect the sugar grown in Queens-
land at white man’s wages to be as low-priced as
imported Fijian or other cheap nigger-grown
sugar, or to be able to compete against it on Free-
trade lines. Therefore, Hughes, M.H.R., is
advocating the destruction of the Australian
sugar industry without the courage or honesty to-
say so. And every Freetrader who votes for a
White Australia is doing the same.
Within the last ton years the population
of Maoriland increased by 23 5 per cent. ; United'
States, 22 per cent. ; Australia, 19 per cent. ;
Germany, 14 per cent.; Great Britain and Ireland,
10 per cent. ; Italy, 8 per cent. ; India, 2£ per
cent. These are ail the returns available as yet,
and they are by no means a bad advertisement
for Maori land’s policy of Protection, democratic-
legislation, State socialism, and heavy taxation in
preference to heavy borrowing. They are especi-
ally good, in view of the fact that Maoriland
started the 10-year period with a thicker popula-
tion tlntn any other Australian State bar victoria,
and with six times as many persons to the square
mile as Australia in general.
J. J. Hynes, candidate for Denison (N.S.
Wales) strongly advocates extending the Old-Age
Pension system so as to make the pension age 00-
years instead of 05. So does Thk Bulletin, but
this paper also recommends finding the money to
pay the pensions with. Up to now. the N.B.W.
Parliament, well-knowing that the Treasury can’t
meet even existing demands upon it, has merely
ordered, in a lordly way, that about £400,000
a year extra be paid in Old-Age Pensions, and
hasn’t supplied one copper of extra revenue to pay
with. The large-hearted sympathy for the poor
and suffering which is expressed by ordering that
the money which isn’t there shall be divided
among them is a pitiably cheap way of buying
popularity.
Victoria’s Old Age Pension bill is about
£335,000 a year, so it may be fairly assumed that
N.S.W., with its larger population, will have to
find between £350,000 and £400,000 a year. This
year, even after paying every item of extra expen-
diture (war expenses, plague expenses, celebration
expenses, &c.) out of trust funds, and annexing
the rents of the Sydney resumed area and the
extra railway receipts of the Commonwealth time
as revenue, there is a shortage ; and nobody has
the courage to make any provision either for it or
for next year’s extra outlay. • But it has been de-
cided to start the old age pensions from Ist August
instead of Ist July, and dodge one month’s pay-
ment. That is the See Government’s nearest
approach to making provision for the future.

One G-. T. Bruce writes to N.Z. Times
urging that Maoriland should not join the Com-
monwealth for ten reasons. Most of the first nine
create a suspicion that Gr.T.B. can’t read good
large print; for instance he has a wild idea that
the Federal Constitution would disfranchise the
Maoris ; abolish the woman vote and re-establish
twopenny postage. But the tenth one is under-
standable enough. Federation, says G.T.8.,
would
Hitch an enterprising progressive race like the Maori-
ianders on to the lackadaisical degenerating Australian —
the motor car on to the tail-board of the old stage coach.
It is refreshing to read a yarn like that, anyhow.
This paper gets tired sometimes when 24 local
members get up in succession after the banquet
to state that we are the salt of the earth.

“ Melbourne is the mitred Metropolitan
of the Horse-Racing Cult.” — American.
The cheek of the local professional military
gang ! A correspondent of a Sydney daily “ hears
on very good authority that it has been decided
that the Krupp gun captured by the N.S.W.
Mounted Rifles in S’Africa, is to occupy a perma-
nent position in front of the Royal Australian
Artillery officers mess, at Victoria Barracks. . . .
Why this gun should be placed where only
a few officers of a regiment which took no part in
its capture can view it is inconceivable, while two
Russian cannons (in the capture of which our
troops took no part), should occupy a position in
such an appropriate place as the Domain.”
The recently - published statement by
N.S.W. Public Service Board that they declined
to retire State Under-Treasurer Kirkpatrick at
the demand of then-Premier Lyne is flatly denied
by the latter. Lyne says he didn’t ask the Board,
and the Board says he did. This time the Board
is believed by the service. The incident is two
years old. When Lyne got office he swore that
there was no money in the Treasury, that Reid’s
balances were fictitious and his books unreliable.
For these statements he expected the loyal sup-
g>rt of Kirkpatrick, permanent head of the
epartment. But as Kirkpatrick for five weary
years had applauded the Reid system, the cash
surpluses, good old Cash Basis and so on, he could
hardly be expected to give the lie to it all at a
week’s notice. It was too sudden. So Lyne and
Kirkpatrick fell out, and the latter’s resignation
was expected daily for some time. But the Board
stuck to the Under-Treasurer, and the financial
rumpus was transferred to royal commissions, ex-
perts and outsiders. The Under-Treasurer and
the financial tangle are still both with N.S.W.
If the British Government suspends the
Constitution of Cape Colony (the cablegrammer
states that such an idea is mooted) tliesp what is a
Constitution? In theory it expresses the right of
the people to govern themselves on certain fixed
principles, but supposing the British Government
suspends the Constitution of any colony, then it
appears there is no self-governing right, in the
case. The act would reduce things to the level
of Russia, where Poland had a Constitution till
the Czar took it away, and where Finland had a
Constitution till the Czar took away as much of it
as lie darned well pleased and left the rest. And,
if it is held that the Sovereign, with the advice of
his Ministers, can suspend the Constitution of one
part of the British Empire, then how about his
right to suspend it in some other part—-say in
Britain ? Of course, he might come to grief over
the exercise of the right, as Charles I. did, but
that is another question.
Joseph Dolton to The Bulletin :
S.M. Herald, in her telegraphic reports of the
Federal Parliament’s debates on the Postal Bill,
carefully omits most of the discussion upon free
newspaper postage. Sydney Telegraph's report states
that the Postmaster-General intends to limit the excep-
tionally small postage-rate on newspapers to those issued
at intervals of not more than seven days. These pro-
posed exceptional rates are on account of the claim to
educational value! Nevertheless, really educational
journals like the Review of Reviews, Scientific Aus-
tralian, Agriculturist, Surveyor, Medical Gazette,
Photographic Journal, Law Times, Chemist & Druggist,
etc., are to be taxed on behalf of the Presbyterian,
Truth, Jewish Herald, Courrier Australien, Deutsche
Post, Chinese Herald, Meat Trades’ Journal, Church
Reporter, Temperance Foister, Shopkeeper, Bad Debts’
Collector, Barbers’ Pole, Undertakers’ Record, etc.
These latter come out 62 times as against 12.
Ye gods!

New Glenelg,
26th July, 1900.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir, —I am pleased to say that I have tried your
VITADATIO, and have received great benefit from its
use. lam 73 years of age. Two years ago I was suffering
severe internal pains, and seeking medical advice, was
told mine was a ease of cancer, and was advised to go
into the hospital for an operation, which I did, but my
age being so great, it was not thought advisable to do
anything, so I left, feeling that I would not live many
weeks. I was in a very low condition. I could not sweep
my room or do any of my usual work, and was expected
by neighbors to soon pass away, when I was advised by a
lady who had a relative cured of tumor, to try VITA-
DATIO. I have done so, and now I feel as well as I did
ten years ago. I can do all my own house work. I can
walk miles now where I could not walk 100 yards for the
pain before, and feel generally well and hearty, to the
great satisfaction of myself and relatives around me.
You are at liberty to publish this, as it may be of use to
others who suffer. I am thankful VITADATIO came to
Adelaide. I remain, yours truly,
EMILY ANN ROSE,
New Glenelg.
Witness : M. P. Murphy, Storekeeper, Glenelg.
~ Geo. R. Drvie, Adelaide.

CANADIAN-AUSTRAUAN LINE
Under contract with the Governments of New South
Wales, Queensland, and Canada, leave Sydney every
four weeks as under, for
BRISBANE, HONOLULU, VICTORIA and VANCOUVER.
Connecting at Vancouver with the
CANADIAN PACIFIC RAILWAY.
This is the Imperial Route Home, as well as the most at-
tractive. It avoids alike the heat of the Red Sea and the
cold of Cape Horn. The railroad journey between the
Pacific and Atlantic Oceans (Vancouver to Montreal)
occupies about 4 days, and is made
WITHOUT CHANGE OF CARRIAGES,
through the grandest scenery in the world—over the
Selkirk and Rocky Mountains, across the Prairies, and
along the North Shore of Lake Superior.
Passengers have the choice of a number of overland
routes, enabling them to visit the principal cities of
Canada and the United States.
