German commercial proverb One boy
(for purposes of office or store work) is a boy ; two
boys are half a boy ; three boys are no boy at all.
At a recent Vic. municipal council meet-
ing :—“There will never be any peace while Cr.
W is allowed to sit here. He’s a nuisance
wherever he is. If there was only me ana him in
the world I’d kill him and live by myself.
M.H.R. Hughes, annoyed at some of
King O’Malley’s'interjections (which, being a bit
deaf, he had not heard correctly): “It is sur-
prising, Mr. Speaker, that a person with such a
massive intellect as the interjector was not in-
cluded in the first Federal Cabinet. OMalley,
impressively. “ You’re quite right, brother.
Chief Justice Madden, in the course of
granting decrees nisi at Melb. Divorce Court,
stated that during the present sitting he had
heard more than a dozen petitions by wives
“ apparently able and willing to fulfil marriage
obligations,” whose husbands had deserted them
in a casual sort of way. And his judicial
Waxellency was pleased to suggest sarcastically
that the Commonwealth Parliament would have a
lot of social blots to wipe out before it could make
a “white” Australia. From which one may
suspect that Madden considers one giddv Aus-
tralian husband to be ample excuse for the im-
portation of twenty black Kanakas.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Veteran Maori footballer Joe Warbrick visited
Wellington recently and started the springs of reminis-
cence. The old-timer first appeared as an Auckland rep.
in 77, and last played for that district in ’96. Joe skip-
pered the native combination that went through Aus-
tralia and subsequently tuned up ’Ome Rugb.vites to the
extent of winning 49, drawing 5, and losing only 20 of 74
matches played. In M.L. and Australia they lost 3 of
26 contested all others were victories. “ Barlow ”
Madigan, Maynard, Anderson, Webster, Goldsmith,
Karauria and Rene are dead. Alf. Warbrick is a tourist-
guide at Taupo, Arthur is an East Coast fanner, Billy runs
Lavender Bay (Sydney) swimming-baths, and Fred is in
Brisbane. George Wynyard (“ Sherry ”) is a carpenter in
Sydney, brother “ Tabby ’’ lives in Auckland, and “ Pye"
Wynyard is at Petone (M.L.), “Bully” Williams is a
policeman, Lee is a reader in the Govt. Printing Office,
Dick Taiaroa and Pat Keogh still cling to Dunedin.
“ Davie ” Gage is at Blenheim, Dave Stewart affects the
turf at the Thames, and dapper M‘Causland, the dead-sure
place-kick, is said to be in some N.S.W. branch bank.
Ihimaira (“Smiler”) is in Napier or thereabouts, Billy
(“ Mother ”) Elliott is at Auckland, and Nehua is a settler
in Whangarei district.
• • * •
“Scrip”: One of the chief causes of the decay
of the Rugby game in M.L. is the introduction of the
wing-forward system. Off-side half the time, opposing
wings push and spar round scrums, which are ill-packea
and disorganised sort of arrangements. The good old
“ passing ” game seems almost an impossibility under the
new order of things, it being a case of “every man for him-
self.” When a player chances to get a sniff of the
malodorous leather he does a little sprint until scragged
and flattened-out. Suspension for slaughtering opponents
is more conspicuous than aforetime. Two got passed out
at Dunedin t’other day ; another got two years at Wel-
lington ; and same week there was a glorious “stand-up ”
during a goldfields match, but people took no notice—
thought ’twas all in the game. There’s also a dearth of
good drop-kickers—a visit from a ’ome team that plays
modern scientific football would be productive of good.
Meanwhile, those obnoxious wing-forwards should be
poll-axed.
• • • •
Saturday’s round of Sydney senior Rugby saw
the competition half-way through ; all clubs having so far
met each other. Big crowds were the rule everywhere.
Five thousand witnessed South Sydney oust ’Varsity from
its unbeaten position by 4 to 3, which, however, still
left it top of the list, with the South next. Losers
evidenced some falling-off in form, otherwise they must
have won. It speaks volumes for their defensive powers
that in the last four matches no side has notched a try
against ’Varsity. South Sydney’s score on Saturday was
a goal from a mark, and ’Varsity’s, a penalty goal. Glebe
triumphed over Balmain in a run-away affair—23 to nil—
and yet the vanquished combination (a week before)
drew with the early “fancy” for the final—Eastern
Suburbs. Western Suburbs had a soft thing (10 to nil)
with North Sydney (a surprise), and Eastern Suburbs put
Newtown down, 13 to 6.
* » • *
Cricket cranks are crazed with excitement over
the probable constitution of the next ’Ome team for Aus-
tralia. Cable tells of four already selected (McLaren,
Jessop, Hayward and Lilley), all top-notchers, whose
inclusion was anticipated: Jessop will be the sensation
of the tour, as he is the hardest hitter known in cricket
history. Lilley has no compeer behind the sticks; and
Hayward’s previously solidly-built reputation was much
enhanced by his achievements in the last lot of test-
matches during the’99 season. If the remaining members
of the prospective combination are near the standard of
those already selected, Australia will need to have it all
on to keep her end up, but matters may be rendered less
difficult if the “down-under” reps.’ bowling strength is
no greater than it promised to be at the time of writing.
Cabled that A. A. Lilley, the Warwickshire
pro., accompanies M‘Laren’s team as wicketkeeper. All
Australians who have visited England place Lilley as the
cleverest “keeper” the old country has had for the last
decade. Darling once told writer that Lillev’s catching
of Hill off Richardson in one of the ’97 matches was the
best thing he had ever seen. Lilley is 34 in Nov.
• « * *
During the 35 years (1864-1900) that W. G.
Grace has been playing first-class cricket he has had 1243
completed innings, and scored 50,672 runs. Average for
whole period, 4076 per innings. Since 1866 he has headed
the batting averages 12 times. Only four other players
have secured the distinction more than once, viz
Shrewsbury (five times), W. W. Read (three), Ranji
(three), and Gunn (twice). The “ Doc.” owned the highest
aggregate on 15 occasions ; nobody else has had i# more
than twice. In bowling “W.G.’s” figures show that he
took 2620 wickets, in doing which he bowled 27,258 overs
at a cost of 44,488 runs ; average, 16'98 per wicket.
• * * *
Annual lacrosse match between S. A. and Vic.,
at Adelaide on Saturday, went, as most followers of the
game expected, to the more seasoned side (S.A.), who ran
rings round the visitors from end to end, excepting during
the third quarter, when Victoria rallied in great style,
and from the “ face-off ” till near the close had much the
better of things. But it availed little, as the score closed
6 to 3 in S.A.’s favor. S.A. has won seven of the nine
games played. The first (in ’88) went Victoriawards, and
that State didn’t get its only other win till 10 years
later.
• • • •
Fist fights ahead in N.S.W. just now promise to
boom the game. First is Sturgeon and Parker, then
Sturgeon and Herb M‘Kell (just back from S’Africa), with
Mick Dunn and Otto Cribb to follow'. Well-built and un-
questionably clever ink-hued bruiser Tom Mitchell was
expected to hitch on to one or other of those named, but
he left quietly for ’Frisco in the Sierra, Wednesday last
week. We’re Sure to see Sturgeon and Gribb at it again,
and if Dunn settles the M.L. cyclone this time, he and
Sturgeon should supply a tip-top go, for Sturgeon is
clever, and that’s the class of man that will suit Dunn
down to the ground.
• • » •
Bruisers Melbourne Jack Willis and Sydney
Harry Dawson dished up a big surprise for Sydney' pug.
patrons at Gaiety A.C. t’other night. Most put the scrap
down as a cert, for Willis, who was being tipped as a good
thing in brine for Cribb or Sturgeon. Odds of 4to 1 were
freel.v offered on Willis, and only' accepted in a few in-
stances. * Willis made the pace, but Dawson moved round
coolly and warily—forcing his vift-d-vis to do the bulk of
the work, and ultimately laid him out in the Isth round.
Daw'son is cool, tough, and likely-looking, but he’ll have
a hard row to hoe with other middleweights having
claims to be pitted against him in the future.
* • * •
Proof that M.L. bruiser Otto Cribb would have
done infinitely better at home than in Yankeeland comes
along every day. He is matched to meet Mick Dunn next
month, and will draw a huge house. Then there are
Snowy Sturgeon, Herb. M'Kell, and others to follow—all
big “gate” affairs. W.A. speculators offer a £2OO purse
and ex’s, for a match with Cribb as one of the principals,
but Otto isn’t taking any of the “ West,” averring that
there’s much more money for him in Sydney than else-
where.
* * * »
“Jab.”: Tripped to Botany, t’other day, and
saw Yankee “ Kid ’’ Parker have ’em on with Mick Dunn,
who did what he liked, though the ’Murkan worried
away at top-tension all through. Parker is a strong,
well-built fellow, but much too muscle-bound, short in
the reach and stature, and heavy in the under-pins (each
of which is a huge load in itself) for a M.Q. fighter. No
man with such a weight of leg could make a clever boxer.
Besides, he doesn’t know too much about the game—not
nearly as much as the man (“Snowy” Sturgeon) he has
to meet July 1. Though Parker might score by chancing
a few punches when getting in, and then (like Cribb)
hustling and keeping atop of his man, he stands off.
Parker may fight better than he boxes—he’d need to, a
lot—but present scribe can’t see how ’tis possible for him
to live three rounds in front of Sturgeon.
Once premier light-weight pug. of N.S.W., Tom
Meadows, died of consumption, at Bathurst, Wednesday,
last week. Little more than 12 months since Tom (two
years short of 40) looked to own a long lease of existence,
out went down with a run once the disease so unaccount-
ably gripped him. He hadn’t trained for many years and
held aloof from excesses of all sorts. Tom acquired his
initial notions of glove-fighting from Peter Jackson (now
in a bad way from lung-trouble, at Roma, Q.), and went
to Murka” with the famous black and that other Aus-
tralian top-notcher of the time, Paddy Gorman, perhaps,
15 years ago. Meadows pulled off two or three battles
straight-way, then went to England and settled down as a
teacher for years, but ultimately came to Sydney where
he opened rooms, and did well teaching boxing and
breeding bull-dogs. Told that for some days prior to
death, Tom used to dream he saw his corpse at the foot
of the bed, and a little while before the end played a game
of euchre with his brother, suggesting that i'f he (Tom)
won twould be taken as an indication of his ultimate re-
covery and the reverse if fortune went t’other way. Tom
lost. AprofK is, it’s marvellous what a number of athletes
have gone off through consumption lately, and how many
are suffering from it now.
• • * *
“ Melbournian ” writes: “ Spare-tne-Days ” (B.
15/6/01) has the Foley craze bad, or else he was born
yesterday. Undoubtedly Larry Foley was, and is, excep-
tionally clever, and made some men more skilful than
they might have been, but not everybody—he didn’t
teach Fitzsimmons. Fitz was a good man when he came
to Australia from M.L. and went to Jackson. Foley never
taught “ Griffo ” either. “ Griffo ” was a natural fighter
(there was and have been heaps of them in the world),
who did things rolev never dreamt of and couldn’t repeat.
Griffo” owned more original methods than any other
man in the biz. before or since. Foley didn’t teach either
Jack or Jem Hall. Former (nearly the cleverest light-
weight Australia has seen) came from London a better
map than when he went away. Jem Hall got the whole
of his knowledge from Billy M’Carthy (writer heard him
tell this at Costello’s “ Lounge,” and paragraphed it when
on Melb. press). Foley didn’t teach Mick Dooley, either;
they were running opposition boxing-schools iri the old
days, Dooley says. Any odds, also, that Foley never
taught George Seale, for Seale shapes too unorthodoxly
(right hand and foot forward)—the way the new hand
does it at the first attempt. Seale was probably a good
man before Foley knew him.
• • * •
“Cocker” Tweedie and .Tack M‘Gowan have
their second match at Kalgoorlie (W.A.) this Saturday
night, and the Sydney Rocks development expresses
extreme confidence in his prospects. It was the same in
Brisbane a year or two since, but miniature giant
M'Gowan caught Tweedie playing to the gallery in the
13th or 14th round and, hey presto ! Such a happening
is always a possibility where Tweedie is concerned ; they
settled him that way twice in ’Frisco.
• • * •
Inter-State bout between “Kaffir” Easier and
Melbourne hard-fighting “ feather ” North at Golden Gate
A.C. (Sydney) Sat. night drew a full house. North was
on wires from the jump ; Essler coolly waited till oppor-
tunity offered, and then put all his stored-up energy into
one slaughterous punch. North got home a few times,
but there wasn’t enough sting in his work. The square-
built, square-living black was all power and go right
through. The towel went up in North’s corner at the
end of the fourth round as a heavy “ right cross ” had hit
the point of his jaw.
» * * *
“Milky White”: The top-flat of the Demo-
Club’s rooms in Swanston-street, Melb., is now
occupied by the Y.M.C.A. as a gymnasium. The other
night, I heard a couple of “the lads” (one had just
returned from a long spell in “ New South ”) talking
about it. Here is what I made of the conversation :
“X. : Melbourne “Billy” Williams tells the
story of how Paddy Slavin took on Peter Jackson for that
memorable fight in London years ago. Peter was in a
bar, lounging about in his usual slouchy style and looking
played-out, when Slavin and Williams'came in, called for
wine, and Jackson joined them. After leaving, Slavin
referred to Peter’s limp appearance, and said “ Why, he’s
half-dead now—l’ll make a match with him in the morn
ing” ; and he did. Previous to this the black had been
doing all possible to yard Paddy, without success.
Neither ever fought a better fight than he did that night,
and each got so much belted out of him that neither
did any good subsequently.
* * * •
Erstwhile Australian middleweight pugs. Jack
Slavin and M'Auliffe were at it again last month for the
heavyweight premiership of India at Rangoon. M'Auliffe
won in five rounds —which should just about make once-
clever Jack (brother of famous “ Paddy”) give the game
best and tackle something he is more suited for. M'Auliffe
was never, by any means, a champion in Australia.
* * * *
Discussing the recent death of a boxer at the
National Sporting Club, London, one print said : “ Bun-
garee, the Australian pugilist, died from injuries received
in a fight with Broome, at Newmarket, in 1842.” No 1
Bungaree fought several subsequent battles with Broome,
and thereafter returned to Australia.

The latest “ strong man ” —a local product—has
started out in M.L. Only just turned 21, Sampson Spry
(calls himself “ Samson ”) puts 2201b5. over his head with
one arm and juggles with other weights of heavy calibre
in all sorts of ways. His chest expansion is 7£in. Spry
is a good advt. for Sandow, whose muscle-making system
he adopted only two years ago, with present results’.
* * * *
A cause for a recent appearance in insolvency
did not come out at the official examination. Some time
ago, insolvent, who thought himself smart and extra
keen-edged, was advised by a friend of a wonderful pony
down at Newcastle which could do six furlongs in “record.”
Accompanied by his friend, X went to Newcastle full of
ardent dreams of hitting Ikey Mo. very hard. Ponyseen,
tried at dawn and beat the watch easily. £159 paid.
Lillie Bridge- a plunge with all the beans to be borrowed
or begged. A race in which “ the good thing” acted as
sheep-dog, taking extra care that no horse got behind
him. Only then did X find that the 6-furlong peg on the
course at Newcastle had been shifted. Old trick, new
mug.

Aged Southern rural performer, Mermaid,
sprung a huge surprise on those not in the know by abso-
running away with Warwick Farm Stakes (Satur-
dav), and finishing a “ street ” in front of everything else.
Mermaid is !»y lago from Union Maid, and had previously
gathered in several country races.
• * * •
Young Cornwell, whorode Edna in the Warwick
Handicap at Warwick Farm on Saturday, is the third
generation of the name to figure on local racecourses.
Albert Cornwell, the head of the family, a Hawkesbury
native, has been identified with horse-racing in N.S.W.
from the early' days, and anecdotes of his turn for practical
joking could be told to fill a volume. “ Cracker ” Corn-
well, his son, is scarcely less well known on N.S.W.
courses than the “ old man,” and young Albert, the
midget who rode Edna on Saturday, is “ Cracker’s ” son.
Albert the elder’s name has been prominently before the
public lately by reason of the phenomenal success of the
popular Mr. R. Richards’ horses, which he trains.
* « * «
Mo’s sleek sides fairly heaved with joy over the
result of Newcastle Cup on Saturday ; it proved an abso-
lute “ skinner ” for most, and very little less for others.
Winner (Salute) was the biggest outsider of all (IS) that
.lined up, and deservedly, for, though well-bred (by Grand
Flaneur from Salutation, by Segenhoe from imported
Welcome, by Dutch Skater), he had done nothing to war-
rant putting on more than a small “ saver.” Salute has
been in many stables and accomplished little. His last win
was the Commonwealth Handicap, at Newcastle, in Jan.,
this year. He is owned by the prominent Singleton
(N.S.W.)sport, Mr. E. Curtis, and was bred by “Bill”
Long.
* * * *
Looks as if four-year-olds are going to have a
big say in coming A.J.C. Epsom and Metrop. Of 117 in
former 42 will be that age on the day, and 31 (of 96) in
latter. One owner has put five in the Epsom, another
four, and four more three each, while 19 have two each
nominated. Two owners have four each entered for the
Metrop., and six others three each.
• * « •
“ Punt ”: Fact of so many apparently no-chance
nags entering for Melb. Cup this year has been com-
mented upon. Owners probably imagine that there’s a
possibility of their coming on “ out of sight” before the
day—which recalls that 200 to 1 was obtainable about
each of last two Cup winners soon after weights were
announced.

After Miller’s hunter (Tonbridge) had won a
Hunter’s Cup at Aloonee Valley, carrying 13.5, he was
rushed in all sorts of doubles for G.N.’Steeple. He was
duly entered, but punters got a shock when it was noti-
fied in the papers that Tonbridge was to be offered for sale.

M.L. trotter Jessie Palm, sent out to cover a
mile (saddle) in 2.20 or under in a trial at Christchurch,
put up 2.18 —a saddle record for ALL.

Among matters for M.L. Trotting Conference’s
consideration this year are suggestions that dead-heaters
be compelled to divide, and that protest-money be in-
creased. Former is a provision badly needed at S.vdney
pony meets, for heaps of mischief is frequently done in
the run-off; but why' protests should be made more
costly than at present needs explaining. For one frivo-
lous kick there are at least a dozen well-grounded ones.

notorious nag, Parthenopieus, is doing stud duty at
Elderslie (Q.), and is responsible for the existence of a
dozen or more promising youngsters from 7 to 8 months
old.

• * * *
“ H.G.” : The introduction, in U.S., of oxygen-
inhalation to sustain invalids has produced the “oxygen
habit” in the form of a craving for artificial stimulant to
the blood. The fact suggests the possibility of oxygen
as a valuable aid to athletes. A small bag carried on the
handle-bar of a bicycle would weigh but a few oz., and
would have the effect of lettinga man finish the last mile
of a century as fresh as he began, so that the phrase,
“ dropping his bag,” would have additional signifi-
cance. The long-distance diver, after taking a dozen
deep breaths, would have his lungs so oxygenised as to
easily outlast all unscientific competitors, while the up-
to-date seconds who first carry the oxygen-bag to the
ringside and squirt the invigorating gas over their bat-
tered hero between the rounds, mav probably find a
world-beater in the ruck of an obscure novice compe-
tition.

pionship sculling match between Gaudaur and Australian

Annual Australian ’Varsity 8-oar race was de-
cided over the Yarra three-mile course on Saturday, and
again went to Sydney, which got half a length best in
16.43 2-5 after a hot struggle. The fixture, as such, was
inaugurated in Centennial year. Prior to that period
4-oar races were contested in ’7O and ’7l, when the crews
included Messrs. “Toby” Barton, “Dick” Teece and
Judge Fitzhardinge. Sydney has had 8 wins in 13 starts ;
Melbourne, 4 in 11, and Adelaide, 2 in 10.
* * * *
N.S.W. Bowling Association’s “Champion of
Champions ” tourne.v, for which only defined club cham-
pions are eligible, is at an interesting stage ; the two left
in to fight the final are “Jim” Hogue, ALP. (winner of
N.S.W. B.A.’s individual supremacy this season), and
“Joe ” Bradbury, present holder of the title in question.
It isn’t often form pans out so truly. Hogue accounted
for a long list of competitors in the absolute premiership
this year, and follows that up immediately* by success-
fully going through another almost similar test against
the best available. Bradbury’s showing this season en-
dorses his play of the previous year. It would be fitting
if Hogue won, as he probably will, otherwise we’ll again
have last season’s spectacle of two champions—each one
owning as good claims to the blue riband as the other.
• * * *
Atop of successes mentioned (8., 15/5/1), Aus-
tralian bowlers at ’Ome have since accounted for Chi-
chester, Southampton and Cheltenham (latter by 80
points), making five successive wins in seven matches
played. Hanigan (Manl.v, N.S.W.) has been the most
successful skipper, followed closely by Victorians Bentley,
Wood and Cudden (manager). ALL. men, in Scotland,
have gone under thrice in four matches, but they’re
meeting far better bowlers than those of England, whom
N.S.AV’s grand sachem of bowls (Mr. John Young), after
some experience, said wouldn’t want much beating.
Results seem to endorse his view, for, at best, the Aus-
tralians are but a scratch team with a big “ tail,” which
has evidently been judiciously kept away.
It is a great game with Wellington (M.L.)
cyclists to take boasting visiting wheelmen over the
Rimutaka, the mountain separating Wellington from
Wairarapa district. It is a long, weird sort of road aj>oiiC
Bft. broad, winding in and out, right over the top of the
mountain, among the clouds. Not a vestige of green, only
dry fern scrub ; no birds or rabbits. It is a long, silent
climb, with a sheer wall on one side and long clear-cut
precipices on the other. Even on a calm day on the flat
the cj r clist will occasionally feel a puff of wind, wh ch. if
he is not prepared for it, will take him off his machine.
The Wellington boys will ride down (and up where ride-
able) with ease, but the stranger can he distinguished
creeping along the wall, leading his machine.

According to a Waikato (M.L.) paper, four
years ago it was not uncommon in that district to see
herds of from 10 to 100 deer, roaming over an extent or
about 30,000 acres. Not more than 3000 deer have been
slain since the protection was removed, and there must
now be, it is stated, at least "400,000 between Cam-
bridge, Tirau, Matamata, Richmond Downs, and Scotch-
man’s Valley, taking a circle.”
An officer lately returned with the Citizens’
Bushmen writes to The Bulletin as follows with
reference to the alleged mutilation of a Boer at
Pietersburg, as related by N.S.W. army-chap-
lain, Rev. Father Timoney :
I saw the Boer in question just after he was killed. He
had received a number of bullets in his body and one
bayonet-wound in the back. That was all, and, though
quite enough, yet it is a gross exaggeration to say that
the men, “after he had long ceased to live, used the
bayonet in a most shocking manner to disfigure
and rip-up the corpse.” The N.S.W. Bushmen, I
may state, had nothing whatever to do with this
incident, except that two of them, at the re-
quest of one of their officers, proceeded to dig
a grave, hut before this was completed an ambulance
came and removed the body, which was buried in the
public cemetery. Father Timoney, I feel certain, did not
see the body at all, as I was present up to the time it was
removed, and he was not there, lie got his information
at second-hand. The men whose officers had been killed
were furious, and it is idle to speak of humanity in such
circumstances. This Boer was hidden in the grass and
behind a white-ants’ nest, and shot his victims down at a
range of about 50yds. As Father Timoney correctly says,
having shot one officer, he shot a second who was about
to assist the first. He also killed a trooper and several
horses. Having done this, when discovered he cried for
mercy, but at least a dozen men fired on him in their
natural wrath, and one rushed him with the bayonet. It
is a gruesome subject to write about, but since it has
been made public let us have the actual facts. It was
asserted at the time that this man was a deserter from a
certain South African corps.
N.S.W. Premier John See complains to a
Star interviewer about the dashed injustice of
his critics :
Mr. See gave an assurance to a Star reporter on Satur-
day that the State finances were in a highly satisfactory
condition. “ The revenue for the financial year,” he
said, “will quite cover the expenditure. The extra-
ordinary expenditure consequent on the outbreak of the
plague, and the cost involved in sending the contingents
to South Africa and China will amount to about
£320,000. That will be provided for, and provided for
out of revenue. It was never contemplated that the
whole of the expenditure in connection with these matters
should fall upon one single year. It would he just as
reasonable to expect the British Government to charge
the whole cost of the South African war against one year
as to expect us to provide for these extraordinary expen-
ditures out of one year’s revenue. The contention is, of
course, absolutely absurd, and would not he maintained
by any fair-minded, reasonable man.
There is a difference, however. For one thing,
the N.S.W. war-hill would have to be five millions
instead of £320,000 before See’s parallel would
hold good. And Britain has paid, in one year,
more war expenses in proportion to population
than the whole N.S.W. bill that See makes
such a song about. The British Govern-
ment pays part of its war-bill out of this
year’s revenue, and provides, by special taxation,
for paying a portion every year. John See, in his
anxiety not to unjustly charge the whole bill to
this year, hasn’t provided for paying any of it.
And if it is “ absolutely absurd ” to suppose that
N.S.W. could pay £320,000 of war and plague
expenses this year, then how is N.S.W. to
pay about £350,000 or £400,000 of Old Age
Pensions next year and every succeeding year ?
If John See conducted his private potato and
shipping business on the lines which he thinks
good enough to run the public finances on, he
would now be standing in two broken boots out-
side the Treasury door asking for a soup-ticket.

The Bulletin hears that that promising Tar-
coola yield (2Joz. average from 90 tons) came from very
carefully-picked stone, and was not up to the owners’
expectations.
Golden Link (W.A.): “Development work is
practically at a standstill, owing to a heavy flow of water
at the rate of 100,000 gals, daily, but the pumps are hard
at work, and it is hoped that the flow will be considerably
diminished in a few days. The manager is a firm believer
in the value of his mine, and has every reason to believe
that his confidence is well placed.” But, in Abigail’s im-
mortal words, “ What are the fax ? ” The crowd running
this mine once owned the Golden Horseshoe as well, and
sold it, preferring to work the Golden Link, because of
its position between the Lake View and Great Boulder
mines. Over £lOO,OOO spent to date, and little or no gold
found, although a shaft has been sunk over 1000 ft., and
much prospecting done at various levels. Has ample
working capital left, and it is not reasonable to think the
authorities’ confidence will cease before the cash runs out.
Golden Links have been up to £6, and there are only
350,000! Present price 215., and of course they are not
worth it under the circs.
• * * *
Central and West Boulder: “Preparations
being made to sink the present shaft from 400 ft. to 1000
ft.” In print 600 ft. of sinking is nothing, but it takes
considerably more than a year to perform. A small lease
surrounded by the Lake View and Associated mines, cer-
tainly one of the best position-blocks of the field. It had
one good lense of ore in the oxidised zone, which was
worked by open-cut. Up to date it has found nothing in
the sulphide zone, and the mine is now gouged by
tributors, whose last crushing of 155 tons gave 178 oz.
Shares have been up to £3 10s. and down to 3s. 6d. The
mine will probably be abandoned, or acquired by one of
the surrounding mines before the shaft is sunk 1000 ft. It
is onl3’ another proof that on this line of the Hannan’s
wonderful gold belt the gold occurs in pipes or funnels
of ore—good enough if they were not so far apart.
•** , •
Associated : This after much useless detail—
“ Taking it all round the Associated is now looking well
both above and below ground, and with the new plant in
running order, a series of good monthly yields should
shortly be initiated.” The public has been hearing this
story for a long time, meanwhile the mine has been work-
ing at a big loss. Shares a few weeks ago were over 605.,
and are now 505., a depreciation of over a quarter of
a million. Present price represents a valuation of
£1,250,000—a very fair thing for a mine the sulphides of
which have averaged under an ounce, which has been
working at a tremendous loss for over a year, and has
only a slight hope of ever working at a profit.
* * « •
Great Boulder : Last month’s production
12,1580 z., made up by the battery crushing oxidised ore,
the cyanide plant working on the old tailings-heap, and
5110 tons of sulphides milled for 84510 z. Working costs
were given at last meeting of shareholders in London
seven weeks ago. Mine expenses 21s. 3d. per ton. This
seems very heavy and, no doubt, will be considerably re-
duced when the development work is lessened, more
money having been spent in this direction than on any other
mine on the field. Battery, 11s. per ton. This shows the
dearness of fuel, labor, etc. Running 30 head of stamps
on soft oxidised ore, and yet costing 11s. per ton to crush.
Sulphide mill, 255. lOd. per ton. This includes rock-
breaking, dry crushing, furnace roas’ing, grinding
and amalgamating, and filter-pressing, and it is
doubtful if it will ever be materially reduced.
Tailings, 9s. 2d.—refers, no doubt, to the cyaniding
of the old tailings dump. General charges, 7s. 10d.
Writer does not know what this means, unless it is
directors’ fees, and management in London and elsewhere.
Total cost of treating oxidised ore, 40/1; ditto sulphide
ore, 54/11- There is no mine on the field more economi-
cally-worked than the Great Boulder, and its lodes are
above the average in size arid value. To expect any
material reduction in these costs would mean a complete
change of system, of which concentrating in lieu of
roasting would be the distinguishing difference. How
unreasonable it is, therefore, to hope to have cheap
mining and milling in a place where the most economi-
cally-worked mine, with a production of upwards of
10,000 tons a month, cannot be worked under an average
of about 50s. per ton, or 12piwts. of fine gold. Sulphide
mines out there cannot make ounce ore pay expenses
unless they are worked in a big way.

Cosmopolitan, W.A. : 1358 tons milled for
1088 oz.; 1980 tons tailings cyanided for 6290 z. Total pro-
duction for last month 17170 z. This is 500 to 600 oz. more
than the average of the last 18 months, and no doubt this
will be increased to over 2000 oz. a month when the equip-
ment is complete. Theco. paid one div. of 6d. last year, and
it is about time shareholders asked what has become of
all the money. Understood that new shares were issued
for the requisite new machinery. It is controlled by two
London mining firms, but it looks as if Bewick Moreing
and Co. occupy the position of “ top dog.” Shares 275.,
an advance of 4s. in a few weeks, and likely to be over
30s. within the next few months. About the only good
Westralian mine working in granite.
* « * *
Bank of England, W.A. : “ Announces a grati-
fying development which should pul a good deal of heart
into the owners of other properties on the eastern fringe
of the belt.” The report proceeds to show that a rich
chute has been struck in the winze below the 200 ft. level,
2ft. of which assayed Boz. 17dwts. per ton. “ The stone
is exceedingly nice-looking sulphide, carrying quartz
bands. Free gold in splashes of telluride is very much in
evidence.” Well, it is about time that some mine on the
eastern side of the belt struck something. Up to date
the eastern mines have been more or less tantalising
duffers. The best of them, the North Boulder, had a
runof good oxidised ore,and paid several divs.,since which
time more than the money thus distributed has been
spent in fruitless prospecting. The western “ fringe of
the belt ” occupied by the Horseshoe and Ivanhoe seems
to be the bast; undoubtedly the eastern fringe is the
worst.
• * • •
Hannan’s Oroya : The hope of this mine is now
centred in trying to locate the chute of ore worked in the
Northern Associated. Reported that the lode in the
south block continues payable, and that in driving north
and south at the 500 ft. level in some places the ore-body
attained a maximum width of 25ft., worth over an oz. to
the ton. That is the yarn, anyhow ; there is no sulphide
plant to disprove it, and there are no dividends in that
kind of rock if it were. This mine ran several Hunting-
don mills for a long time on oxidised ore ; only closed
them down when the average was under sdwts., and, as a
rule, when the oxides are so low as that it is a poor look-
out for the sulphides. However, it is in a good position
to pick up the Northern Associated run of gold at a con-
siderable depth. With that prospect in view shares have
been 255. and 20s. Reconstruction in some form or other,
is inevitable should the mine develop any payable sul-
?>hide ore. Should it not locate the N.A. chute, peaceful
iquidation is recommended.
s » • •
Lake View Consols: 9020 tons, for 13,6020 z.
There should be a big profit in this. It was the custom
up to some months ago to give the working costs, but for
some reason or other (and it cannot be a good one) that
has ceased. Sooner or later it will be acknowledged that
the ljoz. per ton of this and other mines of the field can
only be maintained as long as the wonderfully rich
chutes last; that as soon as they are exhausted (if
others are not found) and the mines have to depend on the
average of the permanent ore bodies, the average will
come down to about an ounce per ton, making a wonder-
ful difference in profits and divs. Some six weeks ago
the Lake View struck a continuation of a rich chute
within a few feet of the Perseverance boundary, and this
is serving to keep up the average. The Perseverance,
hard up for a bonanza to bring up the average, is making
a B-line for it. Rumored some months ago that the
bonanzas in the Lake View were good enough to ship.
They are probably used to keep the average up to l£oz.

Great Fingal Consolidated (W.A.): 3740 tons
milled for 50050 z., 2160 tons of tailings cyanided for 10390 z.
balance from concentrates; total production, 65280 z.
If writer took any pride in himself as a mining tipster
(“ which he don’t ”) he would refer to the fact that a few
weeks ago, when these shares were about £7, he remarked
that they would probably see £lO within a few months.
Last week they were up to £9 10s. This tipping offence
must not occur again, as it is interfering with another
person’s business, and is a poor game, anyhow.

Hannan’s Brownhill: 3165 tons sulphides milled
for 43850 z., and 3538 tons tailings re-treated for 7200 z.
If this could be kept up it would be a big mine, but it is
well known that it cannot, and it is about time the man-
agement admitted the truth. Probably a few more good
returns, and possibly a few more divs., and the mine
will be a back number.

Sons of Gwalia, W.A.: 10,430 tons milled for
4,0390z5. Tailings and concentrates brought the month’s
production up to 5,6360z5. This has been going on for a
long time without a div., indicating that it either takes
an enormous amount of money, to run the mine or it has
a large accumulation of money that should be distributed
amongst the shareholders. Now, which is it? There is
not much money in running a Joz. mine in a country
where wages are high aud water and timber are scarce.

Golden Horseshoe : Another month’s produc-
tion of 15,487ozs.—made up of 6071 tens crushed for

2010ozs.; 2162 tons sulphides, 63400z5.; concentrates,

Perseverance : Last month’s production, over
10,0000z5., is the largest ever recorded from the mine,
and additions are being made to the machinery to further
increase it. In the face of it shares fell 10s. within a
week. If this mine is making anything like the profit of
the others surrounding it, it should by now have nearly
cleared off the enormous debt it had when the sulphide
plant started, and if, as certain newspapers have de-
clared, the mine never had an incubus of debt, then it is
about time the Perseverance started paying divs. The
manager has gone to London to explain things. It badly
wants a bonanza ; also, a place to dump its tailings.

“ Frances Ross, the badgered good girl of Bland
Holt’s new play, ‘ln London Town,’ which is
quite the usual kind of Blandoltian thing, has to
begin with artless curls hanging down her back,
virginal pale-green frock not long enough to hide
buckle-shoes, childish muslin collar, and white
rustic hat. Miss Ross’s figure looks youthful
enough even for this salad-dressing, but her fa.ee
isn’t quite in the picture with that babyish
coiffure. As troubles thicken, the harried country-
mouse lengthens her skirt, and dresses in dingy
drab to match her feelings. The sole spectacular
remnant of happier days is her 18in. waist. Mil-
lionaire Walter Baker, not having seen Frances
‘ere sorrow her young brow shaded,’ spied her
swooning in the street one day, and diagnosing
her complaint erroneously from her genteel belt-
measurement, comments sympathetically that he
himself has known the ‘ hunger-pain.’
“ Harrie Ireland, as a young person with a
past, looks quite a beauty in battered grey.
When she foists herself on millionaire Baker as
his long-lost daughter, she is promoted to dia-
monds and white brocade, the bodice cutueri/low,
the sleeves tight to the elbow, thence flying; out
in stole-ends. In high-life Harrie is over-addicted
to lounging. Of course she represents a free-and-
easy female, but common, garden awkwardness,
even though true to life, is seldom welcome on the
stage. By the way, ‘ the pleece,’ as Baker and
some of his colleagues call them, are much in evi-
dence ‘ In London Town.’
“Less hackneyed than most shows was that
Ragged Boys’ Entertainment in Melb. Town
Hall, Tuesday, June 11. Mabel Mattingley, with
other well-known performers, provided attractive
music, but the mission youngsters, some of whom
were strikingly pretty, were the plums of the
pudding. In the hall, near me, were a 10-year-
old girl and two younger brothers amicably
sharing in common their presentation sweets. All
three had dark eyes, curved red mouths, tight
yellow curls, and smooth skins, so they made a
charming group, though Sis flaunted faded finery
and the boys were got up like stage-burglars in
plaid neck-shawls, &c. This knowing trio took a
most embarrassing interest in the touzled auburn
hair and warmly-tinted cheeks of a lady-musician
who sat near, writhing under their scrutiny.
“ While grown-ups played and sang, rapturous
excitement prevailed amongst small-boy gym-
nasts awaiting their turn. The management pro-
vided tidy white sweaters, which were worn over
the athlete’s ordinary clothes, so that the regi-
ment looked like a corps of stout sausages. The
baby of the batch, an extra-chubby three-year-old,
suggested a pocket edition of Big John Forrest.
The audience cheered and clapped whenever poor
little Poddy started to swing his clubs. Fatty,
evidently inured to the ‘rude World’s jeer and
joke,’ bore up for a time, but at last his tears got
the better of him and his knuckles went to his
eyes. The instructor cheered him up, as man to
man, and by the end of the ‘ turn ’ the gritty
little mourner was swinging his clubs with the
rest. Some of the ‘ turns ’ were ruined by people
flinging coppers on to the stage, thus demoralis-
ing the performers. When honorary workers toil
and slave to coax waifs into paths of discipline
and decorum it’s inconsiderate of outsiders to
breaks the bounds by, virtually, calling for pen-
n’orths of horse play.
“ Gerardy’s farewell matinee (Sat., June 15) was
attended by a chilly, shivering audience. Why
doesn’t some philanthropist end the martyrdom
of musical Melbourne by building a snug concert-
hall? At the matinee, the women round about
were audibly praising the ’cellist’s ‘ beautiful
stroftg face.’ True enough with regard to the
young Belgian’s upper countenance, but there’s
an ill-tempered pout lower down. One wouldn’t
care to offend that mouth by an overdone roast or
an underdone pudding. Miss Kingston, in an
effective blue-and-white frock, sang a dainty little
chansonette.
“The engagement of Miss Florrie Roberts,
Melbourne’s most photo’d charmer, has just been
clinched. The happy man—Mr. Archibald Burt
—hails from the West, is middle-aged, and boasts
an income and a family-tree. Miss Roberts is a
dark, laughing, southern beauty, with luminous
black eyes and a rich complexion. Her father —
the late Mr. Roberts, M.L.C.—was at one time
member for Williamstown, and the wealthiest
man in the place. One or two Melbourne men
are considerably upset by the engagement.
“ People are congratulating ‘ little Langford ’ —
so one hears—on his recent discomfiture of a six-
foot citizen, who, like himself, is in the mining
way, but is not so big, financially speaking. Mr.
Langford, executor for Mrs. Crotty, and bride-
groom of ‘ Miss ’ Clara Merivale’s daughter, is
never seen upon’Change except in a tall hat and
a long frock coat ; so when the six-footer called
him ‘nothing but a little —term of contempt,’ his
dignity wouldn’t stand it. He challenged his in-
suiter to say it again, whereupon Goliath repeated
the offence. The dapper capitalist having replied
with acts of violence, the tall gentleman sped like
the wind from the Collins-street pavement,
followed by Mr. L., and shouts of ap-
plause from the brokers. The retreating
figure is even said to have been assaulted
rearwards by his vanquisher’s immaculate patent-
leather boot. If stockbrokers are fair samples of
the oaks that girls are expected to cling to for
support, it looks as though the Duchess of York
would probably fare better in time of peril if the
small Jook were present—than the wife of her
father-in-law’s burliest subject.
“The consort of ex-Premier Forrest, who is
credited with brains and much tact, is a regular
visitor to the galleries of the House of Reps. She
sails over from dinner at the Grand and takes a
prominent seat, occasionally letting a bare arm
(dinner-dress being donned) protrude from a
dressy cape. Very closely the wife of Roly
Poly follows the debate, while Roly Poly himself
describes a semi-circle with his arm around the
sly Kingston and whispers, with great gusto, the
good things that cause the Minister of Customs to
laugh somewhat in his beard. But that is King-
ston’s way—he smiles and smiles, and what he
thinketh is unknown of men.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
The Belated Heroes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By the s.s. Morayshire 11 officers and 277 men
for N.S.W. and 10 officers an 1 164 men for Queens-
land arrived in Sydney. In a splendid dying
spasm of Jingoism, S.M. Herald sent a reporter
to meet them. And the first question asked them
by that practical scribe was: “Is there any enteric
fever aboard ?”
First at the feast and last at the fray,
Was a saying fine in the Viking day.
Now it is changed by the changed Zeit-Geist,
Last from the fray is last at the feast.
Where are the banners that flew so free ?
Where is the scarlet trumpery ?
Why does it lie inert and dumb,
The thundering, thumping, loyal drum?
Where are the girls with their eyes aglow?
Where are the kisses they used to throw ?
Where are the glory and gallant cheer?
Gone with the drinks of yester-year !
Heroes who first from the war came here—
They took the froth from the Loyal Beer.
Took it and drank it, in casks and kegs—
Those who come after must drink the dregs.
They had the kisses, fresh and new ;
Khaki then was the colour true.
Khaki was worn in hats and boots,
Khaki was worn in summer suits.
Over the land spread the craze like a flood,
And girls wore clothes of the colour of mud.
Banquets were spread for those fortunate
First-back Heroes. In ev’ry State
You could hear the plop of the quick-drawn cork
From Leuwin to Howe, from Howe to York.
Mayors, as fat as a Berkshire hog,
Stood by the table that groaned with grog:
Stood and said, “ Gentlemen, take your drinks—
We toast the health of Corporal Jinks.
“ Corporal Jinks, I may say, has been
Given the Order of the Canteen.
“ Corporal Jinks, of Quangbeyan,
Never was found a lazy man.
“ Corporal Jinks, when the bugle sent
Summons quick to his regiment,
“ Rose like a lion with work to do,
And simply went out and slew, and slew t
“ What are we toasting him, gentlemen, for?
What has he brought us back from the war ?
“ Look at the noble scar on his nose 1
Look at . . . the rest are under his clothes.
“ Civilisation, with fire and sword,
He’s helped to teach to the Boer horde.
“ Here, for a sign, he has brought us some
Of the guts of a Boer’s harmonium.
“ Corporal Jinks is the only man
Who saved the Empire —and Quangbeyan.
“ Corporal Jinks, if my health keeps sound,
Will caretaker be of Quangbeyan Pound."
Who then so fine as Corporal Jinks !
Plenty of kisses, and lashins of drinks :
Never was seen so proud a man
Walking the streets of Quangbeyan.
And O the stories he had to tell
In the Quangbeyan chief Hotel.
“ Chargin’ down on the Kop we came :
Rifles in front spittin’ death and flame.
“ Twelve Boers I sees in front of me—
A Commandant and his Company.
“ ' Billy ’ —l calls my horse by his name—
‘ Billy,’ why, this is our blanky game !
“ Straight we goes for the bloomin’ lot—
My word, I tell you, we were red-hot.
“ * Surrender! ’ saj-s they, with their blanky cant—
But I cut down their blanky Commandant.
“And ev’ry other man living there
I made him a blanky prisoner.
“ I tell you, boys, and this is no jest,
If we Australians—me and the rest—
“ Had not been back to Australia sent
We’d have eaten the African continent.”
“ Right," says the Landlord ; “ Liz—more drinks !
Quangbeyan’s Hero—Corporal Jinks."
That was the way in months of yore,
Lashins of liquor, and girls galore.
Now for these heroes, returned in June,
There is no Bacchic Honeymoon.
Kisses and liquor and love are done—
Kisses and liquor and love and fun.
Lips are straight and taps are dry ;
Kisses and beer are both to buy.
When the Morayshire steamed in free—
What said the Herald ? Ay de mi l
Her.au> that led the Loyal Horde,
Herald that yelled for gun and sword—
“ Have you enteric fever on board ?”
Peace, noble warriors, you may be
Remembered in a year, or three
By Premier—if he lasts—John See.
C REEVE ROE.

Dear B.— After the last six months read-
ing the two biggest cosmic problems seem to be :
(1) What is bigotry ? (2) Whatever bigotry is,
how is it that the church opposite got the com-
plaint in such a virulent form ?
Among the great steamship companies of
the world the (German) Hamburg-American line
is now the largest, with its fleet of 541,083 tons,
and the (German) Norddeutscher Lloyd comes
second with 454,930 tons. The Norddeutscher
Lloyd has also the biggest fleet in course of con-
struction (94,380 tons). It is evidently no idle
boast the Germans make when they promise to
fight Britain for supremacy on the sea.
The mansion of one-time great Westralian
mining boomster, Whitaker Wright, in Park
Lane London, is for sale, and it was rumored
that ’Kaufmann intended to buy it. Baron
Grant, Colonel North, Barney Barnato, and
others, mostly mining-boomsters, built mansions
in Park Laiie, only to see other people live in
them, and their fate seems to have fallen on
W right.
In Governor Falloff Brassey’s time an
endeavor was made to get up an Australian book
of Snobs— a list.of persons imagining themselves
to be something above the ordinary. It was
laughed down completely. A Code of Cads or
Book of Bounders was so ridiculously out of har-
mony with Australian life that it subsided m a
yell of ridicule. But lo ! it bobs up again. Writer
has a circular inviting participation. It is a gem
in its way. The previous sudden dropping of the
blue-mouldy hot potato is thus explained :
This intended publication, “The Australasian Society
Blue Book,” initiated during Lord B-issey’s office as
Governor of Victoria, was postponed owing to the
expected alterations in connection with the Common-
wealth of Australia.
There is a tempting bit at the end of the circular:
As the interest attached to armorial insignia is being
revived, the proprietors of “ The Australasian Society
Blue Book’’intend to promote this knowledge through-
out the Australian States, thereby assisting a tribute to
the memory of ancestors, as well as considerably increas-
ing the interest in genealogical researches.
Reproduction will be afforded .to valuable family
memorial ivy means of heraldic illumination.
Thereby assisting a tribute to the memory of
ancestors” is bad policy and worse grammar.
A book for forgetting ancestors at any is
mucli more urgently wanted in Australia. Ihe
promoters of ttiis remarkable human stud-book
announce that it is to be published “ by permission
from ” Gov.-Gen. Hopetoun. As Hopetoun can t
legally prevent the publication, and isn t big
enough to fight the publisher, he can’t avoid
giving his permission—his tacit permission, any-
how. “The Australasian Society Blue Book”
will also come out by permission from Pompey,
the Lam i of Thibet, Budda, Mahomet, and The
Bulletin’s hick door cat.

themselves as infants in scientific invention and research
and modestly tell us that they have but a smattering of
knowledge of the possibilities, extent, and development
We have only to look at the tine buildings that occupi
the corner of Elizabeth and Bathurst streets to gain some
idea of the extent and importance of their profession d
work But to have the privilege of being shown through
the building by the Specialists themselves; and to see amt
learn something of the real character of their wide prac-
tice, is a treat that only conies the way of a fortunate in-
dividual like the writer. .
The instrument-room was, as usual, one of particular
curiosity. Laymen always have a penchant for eyeing
and examining the polished steel articles with which so
many extraordinary acts are performed upon the human
body. The collection in this room is in every way up to-
date. Whenever a new instrument conies to the front,
and its worth is proved beyond question, it is added to
this rare assortment. Another room full of absorbing in-
terest is that devoted to the galvanic batteries, of winch
there are several magnificent kinds. The noted Lame
batteries, the finest French makes, are here in perfection,
irrespective of their high cost. If any article or mechani-
cal and electric contrivance is found necessary m main-
taining the fame of this institution, the price is never
allowed to stand in the way.
The correspondence department is a veritable ei e-
opener. Its sacredness is a bar to a stranger’s admission,
but a full view of the work going on is had through the
glass doors. Half a-dozen typists are seen driving their
machine-writers at electric speed. To the principals only-
are known the names of the persons throughout Australia
who are being treated as skilfully in their homes about
the metropolis and away in the interior as if they were
present in personal consultation at the institution.
The pa '•im>-rooms are likewise shut off from curious
and pryiri, dances, and the work of one department is a
mystery to he others. No better system for holding
secret the nature of sufferers’ troubles, for safe-guarding
their interests, and giving due heed to the feelings and
sensitiveness of patients, could be imagined. Packages
are not plastered with advertising labels, they are put up
in such a way that only the names and addresses are m

thev are permanently curing and restoring to lit a th and
happiness thousands of people whose lives were a burden
to them. The young, the middle-aged, and the healthy
are treated with the same amount of success. Ihe pro-
digious nature of the work .accomplished by these

of electricity, and only a fringe of imagination of the un-
told stores'of strength and wealth wrapt up in this

Therefore it requires no argument to convince the
most pronounced of optimists that the field in any one of
the great and honored professions of the day is far too
vast for the thorough comprehension of the most per-
fectly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
men. Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult of
all professions—that of prescribing for the ills and
diseases of mankind—we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, off-handedly and without careful, minute, and
searching examination and diagnosis—without any know-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippantly dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
Thanks to the march of civilisation, culture, and edu-
cation, people are getting to know that modern medical
men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessness of
mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
ing that “ gullibles ” are not so plentiful as they used to
be. the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of cases, and work out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
bility.
What better and more striking illustration of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, Liver
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent continent, Australia, have
than the enormous practice of the famous practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
doubted masters of the classes of diseases and complaints
which have been the study of their professional lives.
When we speak of electricity we think of Edison. Why ?
Because he dwarfs other men in this branch of science.
In the same way, when we talk of specialists, w'e at once
associate the names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
tute. So it is in every branch of scientific medicine;
some one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows.
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
be pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker with a host of Complaints about which
they can have but a hazy notion, and superficial know-
ledge. To use a hackneyed but expressive phrase, they
are “ Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never
since the time of Ricord, the noted French Specialist,
have any men conducted so gigantic and successful a
practice as that conducted i the Freeman-Wallace
Electro Medical and Surgical Inautute.
of hard work than any other medical men ; we do it in
the best interests of sufferers. We don’t keep them
waiting longer than we can help, and we suffer many in-
conveniences for our patients’ sakes.”
Noble sentiments, thought the writer, as the time came
for his departure, and the Specialists returning to the'

The simplest way out of the Sydney Daily
Telegraph’s dilemma is for J. Randal Carey and
Co. to resign their billets. There would be no
compliment then on either side. This is a friendly
suggestion.
N.S.W. Labor-party throws down Wise’s
Arbitration and Conciliation Bill as a gage of
battle. If the Council rejects the bill, the
Laborites, declares Arthur Griffith, M.L. A., will
force an appeal to the country. “We are going,”
he says, “to sell our votes for compulsory arbitra-
tion.”
Smart’s 1120, in the bye-election for
Groom’s seat, was the best Labor vote yet given
by Toowoomba. Tolmie, the Ministerialist, who
beat him by 81, is a local school-teacher turned
newspaper-man ; Smart a local plumber. Both
are well liked ; but it is a pity, in the present
state of Q. politics, that the vote should have gone
in favor of the Govt. hack.
The See Government is trying to carry out
a well-known trick of G. H. Reid—charging back
delinquencies to a predecessor. It is sought to
fasten the Sydney D.T. appointments on to Lyne
and to repudiate the responsibility of See, Wise
and Ca Lyne violently objects to this and in an
interview praises up all the appointments as the
best ever made, refers to others which were pro-
posed but didn’t come off, and definitely fixes the
responsibility on See and Wise in shares with him-
self. Verily Lyne has enough to carry without
being the solitary scapegoat of the whole trans-
action.
Two important witnesses for the defence
in the case of Frederick Locke, aboriginal half-
caste—now under sentence of death at Sydney
for rape—have made statutory declarations that
they were told by the police that they were not
required to attend on the day the trial came off.
Thus when called for the defence they were
absent. Under the circumstances the convict
ought not to be hanged. The evidence of these men
might have been worth everything or nothing, but
it should have been heard. All the Crown
witnesses were rounded-up by the police and duly
appeared.
Cabled that 250 Victorians have been sur-
prised at half-past 7 in the morning, 18 of them
killed, many wounded, and most of the rest cap-
tured, disarmed, and released by the Boers. The
press has, for the last 18 months, been booming
the praises of the Australians as scouts. This
episode ought to somewhat reduce our swelled
head, as, barring a,n improbable yarn about the
capture, while bathing, of General Buller and his
staff, there has hardly been a case of the despised
Imperial Tommy falling-in in this wholesale
fashion through bad scouting. British forces
have walked into ambushes, but these Australians
were apparently stalked in their own camp and
just yarded up. No detailed casualty-list is yet
to hand, and meanwhile thousands of people in
victoria are eating their hearts out over this
miserable affair.

preamble to the Commonwealth temporary
supply-bill): J
preamble begins with this magnificent flourish :
llost Gracious Sovereign, wc, j’our Majesty’s most duti-
ful and loyal subjects, the House of Representatives in
Parliament assembled, towards making good the supply
which we have cheerfully granted to your Majesty in this
session of Parliament, have resolved to grant unto your
Majesty the sum hereinafter mentioned, and do therefore
most humbly beseech your Majesty that it may be
enacted. And be it enacted, by the King’s Most Excel-
lent Majesty and the Senate and the-House of Representa-
tives of the Commonwealth of Australia in Parliament
assembled as follows,” etc. The other day Dr. Rentoul
lamented the obsequious deference to Royalty which is
permeating the formalities of the Federation. It looks as
though this were a specimen. No subject addressing an
Oriental potentate could abase himself more than the
Federal Parliament.

In connection with the refusal of the Im-
perial authorities to sanction the Bananaland
measure prohibiting the employment of colored
labor in mills enjoying the public bounty, Melb.
Argus anxiously insists:
There is not the slightest ground for a suspicion that the
Imperial authorities desire to intermeddle factiously with
our legislation.
The Jingo organ assures us that we are a self-
governing people, but points out that the little
Imperial game is evidently intended as a hint to
the Commonwealth Parliament to go slow in
framing its anti-alien legislation. This is self-
Govemment with a difference. We can please
ourselves so long as we also please J. Bull, and
don’t interfere with his efforts to. placate the
Japanese, or upset any other British pot in Asia.
The horse-coper again. At Colling wood
Court (Vic.), the other day, George Wenke sued
Alfred Smith for the recovery of £l3, amount of
deposit paid on a draught horse bought for £22,
and warranted to draw three tons. When plain-
tiff got the animal home, he tried it in an empty
dray.. In half-an-hour it was done, and blood ran
from its nostrils.
A vet. deposed that the horse had asthma, and was a
close roarer, the most dangerous kind of affliction, for an
animal was liable to fall down at any moment. The
bleeding at the nostrils was caused by the rupture of
small blood-vessels through the terrible coughing. In
order to hide the symptoms from a possible buyer,
horse-copers ” crammed balls of fat and leaden shot
down the throat. This exercised a pressure upon the I
flap which, in its movement, caused the roaring. A good
appearance could be given to such a horse by giving it a
quantity of linseed and slop food, that would fatten if not
strengthen. The horse looked, at a reasonable distance,
like an animal worth £4O at least, when, as a matter of
fact, it would be worth more dead than it was worth
alive. At the most it would only bring ss. The effects
of this treatment would last for about five or six hours.
The horse coughed up a quantity of shot while witness
had it in his care.
Verdict for amount claimed with costs, but as
defendant said he had no money, plaintiff’s
chances of recovery are nil.
What can “Our Boys,” fresh from the
slaughter of patriotic Boers, set off against the
story of Madden, the Ballarat miner, whose
heroism was brought to the front again the other
day by the effort to secure him the Old Age Pen-
sion? Madden was a faceman in the Star of the
Ea-t at Sebastopol. Recently lie and his mate,
Warnell, were injured by the explosion of a blast,
left by the previous shift, and Madden, after the
accident, ascended a difficult rise, and travelled
along 2000 ft. of drive to give the alarm. He was
seen, by the platman, groping blindly with his
hands, and when addressed, answered: “For
God’s sake, go to Warnell;” and fell insensible.
It was found that both his ©yes had been destroyed
in the explosion, and he was injured in other
ways. His mate had his arm torn out. Madden,
who was a member of the A.M.A., has been given
the Old Age Pension and was medalled for excep-
tional bravery in the execution of his duty, but
had half as game a thing been done by an Aus-
tralian swaddy in Africa the empire would have
been postered with his name.

At Poverty Point.
Melb. : Gerardy, the’celloist of genius, is only the
third eminent solo performer in that line Australia has
heard. The second was Hattenbach, established in this
city. The first was poor Douay, who travelled with
violinist Poussard. They were the first stars engineered
by Smythe. Many years afterwards, Poussard made a
Melb. operatic frost with his wife, Lottie Mental.
. Gerardy*s musical ability’ and expert management of
his bow are only half responsible for the rare charm of
his violincello playing*. He has a wonderful instrument,
perhaps the finest in the world, and a phenomenally
clever left hand for pulling* the string’s. The genius
seems to be in the fingers of Gerardy’s left hand.
Seems that young Gerardy’s visit to Australia was caused
by the death of his father, which befell some three or four
months ago. The “ Wizard of the ’cello ” felt his loss so
keenly that he determined to try a change of scene and
as Australia was not only a long way off, but also the
place where Mark Hambourg was idolised, he took
a passage for Alelbourne. Jean is a most un-
i affected wizard. He bends over his 'cello without any
preliminary to-do, and works away until his forelock get*
damp and the ends flop limply over his eyes and nose
And when he has finished he ducks his head and depart*
hurriedly, quite unconscious of the girls who are gloating
down upon him from the gallery.

Dear Bulletin,—Ella Russell’s gushful praise of the
girl singmg-pupds at Marshall-Hall’s Conservatorium
(Melb.) was not calculated to benefit those damsels
but it certainly served the purpose of Madame
Wiedermann, who worked Ella for the ad. “ When such
voices and such talent can be trained locally, Ella Russell
wondered that English singers should be so generally im-
ported, said your Poverty Point paragraph (June l/’Ol).
What a waste of wonder ! English singers are not gener-
Hr^u 1 1.u POrted ~? ertainly not Eddish women singers.
With the exception of, perhaps, Madame Slapoffski, there
is not an Englishwoman in either of the grand-opera cos
now appearing here. Williamson’s comic opera co.'
hasn t an imported Englishwoman on the bill, and its
principal soprano voices are .young Australians. Did Ella
Russell intend the pupils of Madame Wiedermann to
understand that managers ought to engage them at large
salaries, but are giving the preference to inferior foreign
singers i And does the paragraphist who quotes Ella’s
compliments find fault with Australia for rating
Dolores as a more finished artist than Marguerite Hen-
derson of the Conservatorium ? Let me whisper in your
ear that Miss Russell gave foolish “ taffy ’’ to the Wieder-
mann pupils, none of whom needed any “taffy” to
strengthen their self-esteem. What Australia really
wmits is a constant supply of visiting artists to show the
difference between singers and singers, and take, if pos-
sible, some of the conceit out of the present generation of
voices. Looking back a few years one is struck by the
modesty of Melba, Miranda, Crossley, and Saville. Then
didn t talk about their own “ interpretation ” of songs in
the glib, self-satisfied style of these Conservatorium girls.
They took to heart the lessons that a few imported singers
could teach them from the platform, and then went to
Europe for further tuition.—Cold Douciib.
According to Boston (U.S.A.) Morni.no News, there was
a scene at the local Opera House when tenor de Reszke’s
admirers sought to present him with flowers while Melba
was also on the stage. Melba stamped her foot and shook
her head, and would have none of it. Speaking to
de Reszke, she said, “ I will not allow it. I will not go on
the stage with a man, and see him receive flowers, when
there are none for me. If you wish to accept the flowers
over the footlights, you must go alone, for I certainly
shall not accompany you.” Meanwhile the audience were
applauding, and at the same time Melba was protesting.
There is no other star here above me, and I will not
submit to the indignity.” And she had her way, as women
generally do. M. de Reszke shrugged his shoulders, after
the manner of the French, and said, “ Very well • have
the flowers sent round to the stage.”
Walter Baker refused a tempting offer for an engage-
ment with the Nance O'Neil co., which, from a business
point of view, could hardly have been so attractive as his
billet of hero in the perennial Bland Holt melodrama
Always a good manager to work for. Bland Holt is just
now anchored in Melbourne. He lately renewed his lease
of the Theatre Royal, and his people are practically fixed
there for a long term, a much more profitable job than
the ordinary travelling engagement J. C. Williamson’s
dramatic co.’s have.
The late poet Buchanan was author, or part author, of
four plays that have been produced in Australia—
“ Sophia ” and “ Joseph’s Sweetheart,” adaptations from
Fielding's novels; “ Bachelors,” a rather rough-edged far-
cical comedy ; and a strong, gloomy melodrama, which
Buchanan wrote in collaboration with Harriet Jay.
Brough and Boucicault made money out of the three
first-named works a dozen years ago, and Bland Holt got
several revivals out of the melodrama.
“S.S.”: Marie Lloyd is A 1 at Rickards’ New Opera
House, and her vitality and vim are enough to cut up
amongst a score of the serio-comics with whom Melbourne
is familiar. But wh.v this fearful and wonderful striving
after suggestion ? Marie’s wink isn’t merely a suggestion ;
it’s like a thud in the eye with a hammer. With such
great talent why resort to the catch-penny tricks of the
squeaking variety “artiste” who has nothing to rely
upon but legs and double-entente? It is said the audi-
ence demands it, but Peggy Prvde managed to give
satisfaction whilst relying on fairly legitimate character-
business, and Marie’s coster effort proves she has in her
stuff of the same sort.
Nina Boucicault seems to have scored a big artistic suc-
cess in a three-act comedy “Lion Hunters," at Terry’s
Theatre, London. The Daily Mail speaks of it as* a
delightful work, and “ Miss Nina Boucicault’s triumph.”
The two short plays “ Beau Austin ” and “ Macaire,”
that the late Robert Louis Stevenson wrote in collabora-
tion with W. E. Henley, were recently staged by Beerbohm
Tree, at a hospital-benefit matinee.
“ That attractive and gifted Brisbane girl, Florence
Rudd,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“ who was burned to ashes in a boarding-house here this
week, owed her fearful death to the fire-trap she was
ljving in. The house is a portion of the Duke of Bedford’s
valuable estate in London’s dwelling centre. A few years
ago the 99-.vears’ leasehold fell in, and several millions of
property fell on the young duke. Four storeys high with
a basement, the squares of terraces have ‘century-old
stone staircases. The fire brigade’s expert told me that
immediately the hydrants splashed on the heated stone
the whole staircase collapsed from top to bottom, leaving
the poor girl to perish in a top room like a pigeon in a
roof-cote. Had the brigade been signalled for on the
first outbreak she might have been saved, for all alarms
in this dangerous district are answered with escape-
ladders. Visiting Australian* ‘ rush ’ these death-traps in
the Squares.”
“J.A.C.”: S.M. Herald’s recent account of an inter-
view with Madame Ella Russell contains various inaccura-
cies. By the “ greatest living tenor, Mazzini,” is probably
meant Masini—who, by the way, I knew well, having lived
in the same house, in Milan, with him—when he was not
making 7f. a day, let alone 7000. The tone of his voice
has never been of “ great volume,” but rather of marvel-
lously beautiful quality and of extreme flexibility.
Madame Russell is also wrong in saying that Masini never
sang in London. He sang at the first production of
Verdi’s Manzoni Requiem Mass, at Albert Hall. Asso-
ciated with him were Stoltz (soprano), Waldmann (con-
tralto), and Medini (bass) —a magnificent quartet—and
Verdi, in person, conducted. It is true that Masini broke
a contract with Mapleson, but that was because Costa
Mapleson’s conductor, snubbed and frightened him’
Jean de Reszke I also knew before he ever
faced a public. He made his first essays as a
baritone, and was anything but a conspicuous suc-
cess. I have heard Tamherlik, who was one of the
finest artists the world has known—his recitative singing
was a revelation, and his high notes were peculiarly bril-
liant, but the rest of his voice “ wobbled ” painfully
Tamagno has possibly the finest voice ever put into a
human throat, and stands ahead, in that, of all existing
tenors. I knew him when he was first “ discovered,” ana
was studying with Bonolu in Milan. Previously he had

NO MISTAKEN PRIDE.
The Truth for Truth’s Sake
The Case of WJrs. E. RUSSELL
{For the “ Newtown Independent”)
“There is nothing immodest or indelicate
in attesting to the truth, no matter what
the subject may be. lam glad to say that
my actions are not influenced by foo.ish
notions of mistaken pride, and as I may be
doing a little good by granting your request,
vou are welcome to take down every detail
for whatever use you may deem fL.”
These words were addressed to a repre-
sentative from this paper by a lady living
at No. 14 Francis-street, Enmore, named
Mrs. Emily Rosina Russell, who had, on
perceiving a note-book in the pressman s
hand, anticipated the object of his mission.
“ You are quite right,” answered the re-
porter. “I may take the particulars,
then ? u
“ Most decidedly. The first sign of de-
parting health was a shocking state of
nervousness. My nerves were so weak
that I started quivering and shaking on the
least provocation. I dreaded to be without
somebody to keep me company in tiie
house, and always had the back and front
doors locked when left alone. Ido not
know exactly what I was afraid of, but
there was a constant fear that some unde-
fined evil was going to happen. I folt
very dull and listless for two or three
weeks before the full troubles manifested
themselves. Then terrible headaches at-
tacked me, together with pains in the
limbs and back, under the shoulder blades,
and also in the lower part of my stomach,
where the agony was often so acute that I
thought they would cause my death. I
was intensely feverish, my skin being
parched, hot, and dry. Fearful night
dreams, resembling hideous nightmare,
also troubled me, and when I slepb for
about an hour I had to get up and walk
about in order to relieve my mind of its
terrifying thoughts. My mind was quite
unhinged through complete nerv >us ex-
haustion brought on through my constitu-
tion being run down. But for the fear of
death by starvation I would gladly have left
off eating entirely. As it was I never ate
anything solid, potatoes and meat being out
of (he question.”
“ Did your food disagree with you ? ”
“Everything I ate caused more or less
pain and discomfort. Each repast, even of
the plainest description, brought on painful
results. My food turned sour in no time,
and large volumes of wind were belched up,
or else I vomited a nasty yellowish fluid,
which tasted for hours after. My chest felt
heavy, with usually dull pains inside, but ]
frequently they were so sharp and severe
that I shrieked in agony. I was not even
free from these attacks when in bed at night,
aid the gravity of my posbion made me
depress'd and hopeless. An internal de-
rangement which gave me evil forebodings
caused palpitation of the heart to come on
"with the le tst exertion. My heart used to
throb against my ribs so distinctly that I
am sure it could be heard in the next room.
I could not real without my eyes aching so
bt l that I had to cease in a few minutes,
a >d I found that I could not remember what
1 had been reading about. Colour from my
chee -s had all vanished, leaving my com-
plexion pasty and dirty looking. My
a pe: ite was poor and fickle, and at length
I could not eat at all. I think the bitter
taste that lay on my tongue continually had
something to do with this, as it was so
nauseating that I felt habitually weak and
sick at the stomach. My sufferings were so
g eat that I used to feel as if I wished for
death to release me.”
“ Had you lost much weight?”
“ I was as thin as a wafer, and just as
weak as you would expect such a skeleton
to be. Even those dreadful nights I used to
have would have been acceptable to me now,
for I cou’d not get any rest. All night long
I turned and tossed in endless pain. The
sunlight of day was no comfort to me, as I
was too worn-out, melancholy and dejected
to appreciate it. One by one my domestie
labours were neglected, and instead of doing
a little woi k I had to look on quite helpless.
My weakness became so extreme that I
could not walk across the road.”
“ What did you do then?”
“Well, I was so alarmed at being unable
to Walk that I consulted a physician. Of
course, I had not gone on all this time
without taking medicines, but they all
proved worthless. The doctor pronounced
my case to be a very bad one, and said I
required skilful and careful treatment. I
placed myself under his care and took his
medicines till I could take them no longer.
My sufferings became more intense than ever,
and my stomach was too weak to retain the
medicine my doctor gave me. I had given
up hope through his treatment being so un-
successful, and did not care what happened.
A neighbour then urged me to give Clements
Tonic a trial. I did not expect to get well
again, but I bought some Clements Tonic,
and after a few days an extraordinary im-
provement was apparent. My nerves were
strengthened by each dose and my head
ceased to ache, besides losing the distressing
feeling of sickness at the stomach. Pains
from the chest and shoulders were removed,
and I never again retched or vomited after
I st rted taking Clements Tonic, which
also gave me a splendid appetite. I felt my
food was doing me good, and I soon looked
it, too, as a healthy colour came back to my
cheeks, which became more plump every
day. Clements Tonic made me sleep soundly,
and I got up every morning feeling fresh
and vigorous. When my doctor and all
other medicines had failed, Clements Tonic
gave me robust health.”
“ Can I let the public know ? ”
“ Yes ; do. I hope all sufferers may read
my case and benefit as I have done. ”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Emily Russell, of 14 Francis Street, Enmore,
Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that 1 have carefully
read the annexed document, consisting of three folios,
and consecutively numbered from one to three, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account of
my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my full permission to publish in any way my
statements—which I give voluntarily, without receiv-
ing any payment; and 1 make this solemn declaration
conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of an Act made and passed in
the ninth year of the reign of her present Majesty,
intitule l “An Act for the more effectual abolition of
Oath 9 and Affirmations taken and made in the various
Departments of the Government of New South Wales,
and io substitute Declarations in lieu thereof and for
the suppression of voluntary and Extra-judicial Oaths
and Affidavits.”
Declared at Newtown, this Bth day of October one
thousand nine hundred, before me,
FREDK. JONES i.i
