Re the escape of Edward VII. at the time
of the Shamrock 11. disaster, Brisbane Courier
Wrote thusly : “ The late Queen’s reign was re-
markable in this respect, and the escapes of Her
Majesty were so numerous that it is not difficult
to understand why she was able to keep a serene
temper in the most trying circumstances.” One
of these “escapes” occurred at Windsor when
Victoria slipped (in descending from an upper
room) and hurt her leg. The loyal press of the
day spread the rumor that O’Donoyan Rossa, the
famous Fenian, had soaped the stairs —in accord-
ance with a Plan of Campaign.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to tlieir advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
Strange rumors float round the Russian
warship sent on a complimentary visit to the
Commonwealth. Russia is a peculiar country,
where the Tartar is only covered up in patches,
and numerous streaks of Barbarian continually
rise up to ask Civilisation “ whaffor ? ” Public
opinion in Russia is still a very tender plant, and
private liberty spends a good deal of its time in
evading the gaol. S.M. Herald noted that the
Gromoboi’s sailors who had outrun their leave
seemed to be very sorry for themselves when they
were being taken back ; and some of the warship’s
visitors have brought back a queer tale of a
nautical offender of long standing who lives per-
manently in a cage, and glares dismally through
the bars when the hospitable revels proceed. It
would be interesting to know just how much truth
goes to the lb. of yarn about the Gromoboi.
It is hard to understand how it pays any-
one to patent small articles. M‘Lean Bros, and
Rigg, Ltd., have brought out a handy little thing
which they sell under the name of the “Duchess
Collar and Cuff Drier.” No one who sees it but
would buy, for the convenience is undoubted, and
the price is only sixpence. They must expect to
sell them by the thousand, but even then, how
does it pay ?*
“H.” : The King’s yacht adventure is
singularly like one recorded of Charles 11. He
gave the Duchess of Portsmouth a yacht. The
Duke of York, afterwards James 11, was an
alleged Admiral in those days. He took the
King, the Duchess of Portsmouth, and a little
party in the yacht off the Thames mouth. They
struck fearful weather, and just escaped a general
drowning. Gostling, the celebrated tenor of the
Chapel Royal, was of the party. His account in-
spired Purcell to compose a fine paraphrase of
the psalm “ They that go down to the sea in
ships.” Gostling sung this in the Chapel Royal,
and it was a great favorite with the King.
Some observations (possibly inspired by
drink) in Victorian country paper, name of which
is mercifully withheld :
The comet, as seen last night by the residents, re-
sembled the letter V, and was a beautiful sight in the
western sk3' before the moon came on the scene. It seems
a happy augury to the Royal couple visiting our shores,
for although the days of superstition are past, yet there
are many who foresee in this resemblance to that letter
that our late beloved Queen looked down, as it were, and
wafted a blessing in spirit to the future of the Common-
wealth of Australia in the person of her grandson.
What a cheering idea, that the spirit of the de-
parted should be tearing through the unutterable
frosts of space like Tomlinson’s ghost ! Yet the
country editor laboriously digs and dynamites
these poetic fancies out of himself in all serious-
ness. And, after all, they are probably as good
as could be expected at the salary.
Dear Bulletin. —About your expulsion
from Maoriland Parliamentary Library, I dropped
in there recently and found you roosting in your
accustomed place. What is the reason ? Did the
Library Committee relent, and commute your
sentence? Last session, by the way, Premier
Seddon, from his place in the House, declared
that he had not been a party to your removal
from the Library.— Kiwi.

The Vic. Government, with a cheerful
disregard for economy, has repeated at Melbourne
Exhibition Building all the luxuries of the Par-
liament House, which lately passed into the tem-
porary possession of the Commonwealth Legisla-
ture. _ Everything that Peacock and Co. enjoyed
in their old place is to be found in equal splen-
dour at the “ temporary ” establishment. It is as
though the Leg. Assembly and Council Chambers
had been lifted from the top of Bourke-street and
dumped down in the Carlton Gardens, together
with ante-rooms and official quarters even more
commodious and more carefully furnished than
those of the temple whence the mace was
stolen. A little bill of £30,000, or thereabouts,
will be duly passed on account of this new Vic.
Legislative Club. And when, in the fulness of
time, the Commonwealth Capital is established,
and the Parliament of Australia clears out of the
stone edifice that represents more than £1,000,000
sterling, the Vic. Government will have a pecu-
liarly useless property To Let for evermore.

“ Castro’s Last Sacrament and Other
Stories,” by Albert Dorrington (“ Alba Dorion ”)
is the best book of short stories yet produced in
Australia. The S. Daily Telegraph recognises
the author’s “absolute genius.” The S.M. Herald
says the volume “ contains dramatic pictures
which the best of our artists in words might well
envy.” A handsome cloth-bound volume, 350 pp.,
post-free from Bulletin office for 4s. 6d.
“ At Hughenden, on last Primrose Day,’
writes Sydney D.T.’s London correspondent, “it
was noticed by the pilgrims to the grave that for
first time since ‘Dizzy’s’ death no Royal wreath
lay thereon. Very appropriate is the explanation,
in a character sketch of Queen Victoria, which
appears in the Quarterly Review, of Disraeli’s
ascendancy over the Queen. ‘No one, it is cer-
tain, ever amused her so much as he did.’ She
permitted to him what she permitted to no one
else, a ‘reckless disregard of Court etiquette.’
He was never in the least shy ; he did not trouble
to insinuate, he said what he meant in terms the
most surprising, the most unconventional ; and
the Queen thought that she had never in her life
seen so amusing a person. He gratified her by
his bold assumptions of her knowledge, she ex-
cused his florid adulation on the ground that it
was ‘Oriental,’ and she was pleased with the
audacious way in which he broke through the ice
that surrounded her. She loved the East, with
all its pageantry and all its trappings, and she
accepted Disraeli as a picturesque image of it.”

that you desire.
In appearance the .ZEOLIAN is like a Cabinet Organ, as shown in
the illustration below. The Stops are used to vary the expression and
form a never-ending delight to those who love music. The two Knee
Pedals open respectively the Swell-box and Great Organ. By one you
turn on immediately, without drawing a stop, the full power of the
Organ ; with the other you vary the power from pianissimo to fortissimo.
A time lever working on a dial enables you to vary the tempo at
will and phrase just as if you were using your own fingers.

My Birthday Hat.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I saw a toque yesterday at Grace Manners’ I
rather liked,” said my married sister. “Let’s go
there first.” And since she had that day tele-
phoned to me “to meet her in the afternoon to
see about the hat she had promised,” I “was
willin’.”
It was my first visit to Grace Manners’ since
the crash that killed my father. Visits to
any milliner since that event had been but few
and far between, and the mere idea of new head-
gear was a delight.
Only when we reached Grace Manners’ and
Amy removed her hat-pins did it dawn on me
that it was on her, and not on my, account the
toque had been put aside. For a while she was
undecided between it and another, marked 655.
She debated long on the merits of each, and ended
by choosing her first love, which was 84s.
“My allowance is really so small,” she whis-
pered, as she resumed her hat, “ that I feel quite
justified in paying the difference from the house-
keeping purse.”
Amy has only at the rate of £5 ss. a week to
dress on !
My soul meanwhile had gone out to a creation
whose simplicity was its chief charm. It is rarely
Grace Manners descends below the level of two
guineas. This, to my delight, was only 255.
In imagination I had it on, just found it suited
me to perfection, when Amy asked :
“Are you ready, dear? We have plenty to
do, you know.”
Maybe I looked as flat as I felt. Anyhow, she
added soothingly : “There are real nice shops in
Jones-street, and we’re bound to find something
pretty.” , ,
But between Grace Manners’ millinery and
Jones-street millinery is a great gulf fixed, and no
wonder I felt flat. _ .
From a spectacular point of view, Amy is an
undoubted success. Hers is that broad-blown
comeliness, red and white, which always attracts ;
added to which her taste in dress is beyond re-
proach. It was possibly on account of her ob-
vious silk foundations that, in the next shop we
entered, the head-milliner herself came forward to
serve, and on my account produced the straw
shapes, which I was made try-on until too bewil-
dered to distinguish one from another. But there
was one in which Amy and the milliner decided
lay infinite possibilities. Only, declared the
former, its price was prohibitive. Five shillings
for the toque alone was absurd; so, after much
talking, she got it for 4s. 3d. .
“ It’s really no use getting an expensive shape, .
whispered she. “They’re old-fashioned before
you know where you are. And, as I’m buying it
out of my own purse, of course I want to keep it
as low as I can.”
How I resisted saying—“ One usually did buy
presents out of one’s own purse,” is still a mystery.
“I’m ninepence to the good now, you know,”
she continued, “and, of course, the less I spend
on the hat the more I can give for the trimmings.”
I was doing my best to appear interested, when
the milliner mercifully came to the rescue.
“No,” said she-; “they never sent-out goods
themselves. Of course, if a lady particularly
wished it, the carrier could take things out, but it
wa3 always extra.” The mere word “extra”
frightened Amy. “ We’ll take it,” she announced.
And we did —or, to be more correct, I did. I
thought it only decent to offer.
All mv interest in it had long before evaporated.
I had not even given its trimmings a thought till
Amy said, “There’s another shop further along
where the ribbons are dirt-cheap. Come and see
if we can pick anything up; ” and I blush to say
I went.
The ribbons, the man in the shop declared, were
given-away, and so they were. Amy, unfortu-
nately, remembered she had seen them elsewhere
for id. a yard less, and refused them as a gift.
“ She did not believe in being done,” she told the
shopman. I was inclined to offer him the extra
id. to save another walk, but thought Amy might
be hurt. Jack declares she would only have been
too pleased. Finally, we came to a shop contain-
ing, as the posters everywhere announced, “a
damaged stock ; ” but from its general frowsmess
that fact, even to the most unobservant, would,
without any posters, have been at once apparent.
Amy discovered in the window the exact shade of
ribbon she wanted, bore down in triumph on the
sole girl visible, and—think of the extravagance !
bought two whole yards of it. I almost begged
her not to be reckless.
“It’s soiled,” she admitted; “but what else
could you expect for 4d. a-yard ? ”
“ What, indeed ? ” said 1, and went on to regret
she allowed the hat to give her so much trouble.
“Well,” she confided, “I have been worried
about a wing. I don’t want to buy, of course ; and
now I’ve just remembered there’s one in that felt
hat I had the winter before last. I’ll never wear
it again, I daresay, and it really isn t any better
worth.”
For a second I wanted to cry ; then I longed to
laugh ; only Amv, excellent as she is in many
respects, is unfortunately without a sense of
humor, and I did not dare. “ And I’ve got an old
steel belt-buckle that will do for an ornament.
It’s broken, of course, but who’s to know?
Who, indeed ?
“And ’’—glad, no doubt, to find me enter into
the spirit of |the thing at last—“ if you happen to
have a bit of velvet anywhere that would go under
the crown, that, of course, would save.
Amy looked distinctly relieved when I assured
her that either mother or I had.
“ I’ve kept within the 55., too,’ she said, as we
left the shop. ‘ ‘ That’s the best of knowing where
to go. And it’s not likely you’ll think the less of
the hat because you know what it cost.
“As if anyone would ! ” , , . .
“Those silly notions of mothers about people
not caring to buy their own presents are quite out-
of-date. The day of sentiment is over, thank
goodness. gaid j And Amy , a 3 j s her
amiable habit, took my irony for acquiescence.
“It’s too late for tea,” she announced as we
once more found ourselves in the heart of the city,
and, tired as I was, I was not sorry. For, to march
into a crowded tea-room, carrying the bag with its
obvious Jones-street brand, was—though I am not,
I trust, a snob-a feat to which I felt myseif un-
eq Amy was to trim the hat at her house, and, as
we live in suburbs far apart, I eagerly looked for-
ward to the moment when she would relieve me
from it. I had reckoned without ray host. For,
when we reached the corner, and I held out the
bag, she only said, “Oh, I’m going to buy fish
and really can’t carry it and the bag, both. You
see,” with a sudden inspiration, “I might crush
it ”
That’s what I myself wanted to do, and to jump
On it as well.
"So you wouldn’t mind very much taking it
home to-night, and bringing it down to-morrow,
would you, dear?” But, as she had not the
faintest intention of carrying it, it was not much
use my saying so, if I did. . . ,
I travelled home second-class. I was in no mood
for friends. At the end of the journey, as the
train left our station, a man, leaning far out of a
carriage- window, was to be seen, waving wildly
a paper bag, which, strange to say, bore the
same name as the one I had formerly carried.
Many people seemed interested. I am thanktui
to say I am above vulgar curiosity, and took no
n °At dinner that night mother and Jack (there
are only three of us) dragged from me an account
of my afternoon. .
“Pretty pure!” was Jack’s comment. And
though we are hard-up, and a hat is a hat, mother
did not scold. ..... .i •
“ It’s better where it is,” I explained. Nothing
under the sun would have induced me to wear it,
and Amy’s feelings might have been hurt.
“Amy’s feelings!” snorted Jack. Like to
know where she keeps them. She was born
pachydermatous.”
And mother softly wondered, as she has done
more than once, “who ever does she take-after .
Later that night, Jack, who had already remem-
bered my birthday, slipped two sovereigns into
my hand. 46 To get that hat you liked, he ex-
plained. , ....
“Jack !” I gasped. “ It’s far too much. You
want heaps of things yourself, and, besides, you
can’t run it.” .
“ That’s my affair, I hope. And look here, Lil,
if you don’t buy it to-morrow and wear it down
to Amy’s the first show you get, you and I’ll have
a row.” . , , ,
My nightmare was, that when Amy heard what
had happened to her gift she might offer to make
the loss good. I need have had no such fear.
And, if Jack imagined he was going to turn her
eves into her soul, he also was mistaken. All Amy
did with her eyes when I turned-up on her next
day was to open them wide; and if possible,
when she heard the price of Jack’s gift (which
she inquired), to open them wider.
But she did not forget, the next time she saw
mother, to say, when she spoke of my getting: so
expensive a hat, that she had always heard it
was the poorest who were the most improvident.
E. M. DAVIS.
Larry Petrie, Anarchist.
Killed by accident recently at Villa Rica, Para-
guay, the only working anarchist Australia has so
far produced.
Larry Petrie was well-known in Sydney in the
early nineties as an agitator of a particularly
aggressive brand, and the most cheerfully irre-
sponsible person imaginable. He lost his left
arm (previously injured) at a Riverina woolshed ;
a non-union shearer didn’t like Petrie’s union
arguments, and fell upon him. Result: Petrie’s
arm was amputated in Wagga Hospital. He
afterwards did some organising among the N.S.W.
woolshed laborers, then drifted to Sydney to
make things lively for the various Works Minis-
ters and other friends of the unemployed.
When the seamen’s strike was on in ’92 (or ’93)
Petrie booked a passage for Brisbane in the s.s.
Aramac. On the voyage the side of the vessel
was blown out. Fortunately no one was injured,
not even Petrie. He long afterwards calmly told
writer (in a letter from another country) that he ex-
pected the vessel to become a total wreck, but
that the bomb (if it was a bomb) had partially
failed because of the absence of weight on its top
side.
Petrie was arrested when the Aramac got to
Brisbane, kept in gaol three or four weeks,
charged with the crime, and acquitted for lack of
evidence. The detectives were wholly at fault ;
all they seemed to be able to do was to go round
the Sydney explosive-stores and ask who had
bought dynamite. As if a real anarchist would
buy dynamite while Govt, blasting operations
were in progress in half-a-dozen Sydney suburbs,
and the storeman in charge o’ nights could be
enticed to an adjacent corner hostelry for a much-
needed drink!
Petrie naturally lost many labor friends by his
supposed connection with the Aramac outrage; but
he said he had reckoned on that beforehand. “Of
oourse,” he remarked, “if the ship had gone down,
so would I ; then my friends could only have
shown their disapproval by refusing to wear
mourning. I daresay my wraith would not have
wept therefor.”
Under “proper ” conditions Petrie would have
made an appalling anarchist or a successful soldier.
Consider 1 its courage and resource. Finding
Sydney “hopeless” he walked to Newcastle;
no money, no tucker but what he could borrow by
the way. Writer next heard of him at Hono-
lulu ; he had worked his passage from Newcastle
in a coal-ship. (He had but one arm and was
anything but strong). A few months later he was
seen in ’Frisco selling bootlaces in the streets. He
wrote from there saying he intended to battle
down through Central America, across Brazil to
Paraguay, where he would knock at Lane’s door
and ask to be allowed to join Cosme. But in a
few weeks he was heard from at or about New
York. He had “ beaten ” the railroad companies
for a ride nearly the whole way across the con-
tinent ! Some of his written experiences of
stifling journeys, packed in among the merchan-
dise on freight trucks, and hairbreadth ’scapes
from heavily-booted and irate railway conductors,
would make sensational chapters in a tramp’s diary.
Somehow —Heaven knows how —he got from
New York by sea to Monte Video, then wrote
to Australian acquaintances from Cosme,
saying he reckoned he had found a per-
manent home at last. But he hadn’t.
Two years’ communism was more than enough
for the restless firebrand, and he _ went out into
the world again. He got a situation as railway-
watchman at Villa Rica, and expected to shortly
have enough money saved to take unto himself,
“with my one remaining arm,” a soft-eyed Para-
guayan wife. “ I am qow,” he wrote quite re-
cently, “ Senor Lorenzo de Petrie, and am almost
persuaded to become an individualist. But I
guess I’ll be a one-winged angel first.”
He probably guessed correctly.
J.D.
When Parnell, the originator of the eight-
hours’ movement, died at Wellington (M.L.) ten
years ago, a memorial-fountain was erected by
public subscription on the wall of the Public Lib-
rary. Since then it has been allowed to go to
rack and ruin. The bust of Parnell has been
chipped and broken, the gilt has been scratched
out of the lettering, and the marble slab is used to
record the unclean wit of the local larrikin. Better
to be altogether forgotten than have your ghost
come-back in after years to r see the uses your
monument is put to.

The Utterly Damned.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now, this is a song for the Lost to sing—
A hymn for the Utterly Damned ;
A lusty lilt for the sailor folk
Who the ships of sin have manned ;
A sneer for the men who might have been,
A curse for the things done well,
And a cheer for the straight-away course we
steered
Hard-down for the Port o’ Hell.
We’ve struck in the mouth the god of Hope;
We’ve spat in the face of Fate ;
There is no law for the Damned, messieurs,
But the law themselves dictate.
Friendship and love are a coarse-thrown jest
In the ranks of the outcast corps;
And truth lies rotting—a sacrifice
We made in the years of yore.
We throttled the Faith our fathers reared,
We left it a foul Thing, dead ;
We stared back hard in the eyes of Shame,
Till Shame hung down its head ;
Honor we stretched on a self-made Cross,
Laughed grim while the nails we drave,
Then buried the corpse where the blind worms
creep,
And covered with filth the grave.
But stick your hat on the side of your head—
’Tis treason to shrink with us ;
Stand stiff for the whip when the whip falls due—
Though it lash to the bone—no fuss 1
Let no man see by a twitching lip
The torturing power within—
For this is the pride of the Utterly Damned
That the least recruit must win.
A brave song this for the Lost to sing,
The hymn of the Utterly Damned;
A capstan chant for the sailor folk
Who the Ships of Sin have manned ;
A sneer for the Men we might have been,
A curse for the Things Done Well,
And a cheer for the straight-away course we
steered
Hard-down for the Port o’ Hell.
U.L.
J. BARR.

the man who insisted on kissing the Jook
of York’s hand when he was C.M G’d.,
seems to have enjoyed himself most grovel-
somely in Melbourne. After his return to
a !i , ? w °rshipful dignitary, from the parlor
at Adelaide Town Hall, wrote a letter to a livery-

Dear Mr. Blank.—l am writing these few lines to express

A Black Blot on White
Australia.
S'r West?- S ° N wri *- ea plainly on doings in the
John Forrest (for 10 years Premier of W.A., and now
wardTs hTa ° f the Commomve al t h) has come for-
ward as defender of the W.A system of aboriginal
ment'of nfl 6 A ° E that “ W..A. Govt.’s treat-

cal What'll-vi Btatement as false as ungrammati-

anting cheap labor? Forrest seems to think that
WbirVwf J r hU “ n bfger’’-slayers were Victorians
y birth, W.A. Govt, and people had no share in the
nrfm u Bay th ® . W-A ’ Govt ’ and its Jp - officials were
primarily responsible because they assigned the natives.
Even the birthplace argument is shallow. Bunbury may
produce a murderer some day when it awakes. I landed
almost S th Ck n 1" ““ N ° r ’ WeSfc ’ some years back, and
almost the first sound I heard was the clank of the chain-
gang of aboriginal prisoners at work on the W.A.
Govt, tram-line. They were chained round their necks
in pairs, and were repairing a line which a “ Willy Willy ”
(blackfellow’s name for cyclone or rainstorm) had
arnage . If two were engaged at wheelbarrow work
when the barrow wasfull one gothold of the handlesand his
attached” mate, dropping his shovel and gathering up
t e slack of the chain (connecting them), so that he
wouldnt trip over it, accompanied his black “brudder”
to where the barrow was tipped. He also had to go if
?w e A°^ thC svndicate had reply to a call of nature.
( v.A. Govt, makes no allowance for weak stomachs).
_ * *
Forrest says “the general opinion of those who visit
country portions of W.A. is that the natives are really
well-treated, and I know very well there is no cruelty
about it.” The Church of England Bishop of Perth
visited the Nor’-West some months before the “ Bendhu ”
case, and was the guest of various squatters there, yet in
a letter to a Perth paper on his return he said
"that from his observation” (you bet squatters
aid not let him see the worst of their system),
and from what he had heard, the Nor’-
West natives were not well treated.” John Forrest says
all Ji si. are native protectors, and the police are speci-
fy charged to repress any cruelties to natives that come
under their notice.” But most of the J’sP. are squatters
and squatters friends—the squatters being the“slave-
W. of the Nor’-West. I ask any reasoning human being
what justice will an unfortunate “ slave ” get if up for an
offence, when, as is mostly the case, the complainant is a
brother squatter and a brother slave-driver? As to the
police, they are weak human beings; and if a bush trooper
on his beat of 200 or 3 i 0 miles from station to station
rides up, and the squatter welcomes him effusively (for is
he not representative of the W.A. Govt. ?), invites him in-
side and makes much of him, is the said trooper likely
to favor the squatter or the down-trodden slave of the
squatter? Also, the squatter has “whips of time”
(appropriate phrase) to “deal it out” to niggers between
troopers visits, about every two months, and it’s certain
the squatter must inform on himself—if he is to be in-
formed on—the slave is too much “ frightened fella.” He
believes the squatter owns him body and soul, and with
reason. Talking of the Bendhu case, Forrest says it was
one of the worst that has ever occurred, and that the
Bendhu case was dragged before the world. Yet it took
three trials in W.A. to bring the killer of three people
(two women and a man) in guilty of—manslaughter 1
* * * *
The “ indentured ” natives are kept near starvation-
point on most N.W. stations. Camp on a station, and the
‘enslaved” natives (tied by the “indent” to that
station) will pester you for food. A man on tramp can’t
carry much, but for the bit he can give, he can pick his
choice with the native women and girls. It’s hunger
drives them, not lust. If you camp (I’ve done so) within
miles of a nigger “assigned” to one of these stations,
he 11 turn up almost every night for what food you can
give him, and he will bring his gin with him, if she’s
wanted, each visit. Again, a common practice on these
stations is what is known as the “ Pinki ” (native holi-
day)—a picnic where the tucker is flying in the air, or
crawling the earth, the natives having first to catch
Defore they can satisfy their hunger. This “ pinki ” takes
place when the kind “ master ” has no immediate
work for his “ indentured ” black goods, such as fencing,
clearing, &c., and he wants to save the stingy allowance
of low-class flour and damaged rice, which he is compelled
to give his “ stock ” to keep sufficient strength in them
so that they can toil. The bulk of these stations have
iar more natives than they really require, but the
squatters are so greedy that they get all the lot
assigned ” to them ; it allows for annual losses, break-
ages, sudden deaths, &c. So the “master” tells his
natives to go for a “pinki” for one moon, two moon, or
until he requires their services. The native can’t go where
he likes, and he must return at arranged time or else—
trooper neck-chains—trials for the runaway “assigned ”
native, who when guilty (he always is guilty)
1? «?n nlshe<1 ‘ T ! le magistrate will ask the employer
Will you have him flogged or imprisoned ?” This ques-
tion is as natural to a Nor’-West court as is the “guilty
or not guilty” of civilisation. If the “master” requires
the services of the native just then he will say, “ Oh, flog
him, and it is done, and the native handed over ; if the
master” does not require the native, “Oh, imprison
him, and then the native gets worked and fed at VV.A.
i? Vfc i. expense until sentence is served. To continue
about the “ Pinki.” The natives not needed just then
? lre ~uu c ks, gins, and picanninies—turned into the bush
to hunt the game that has so largely departed through the
squatters’ sheep. The natives hunt and eat everything
almost with life in it, and gather seeds, roots, &c., and
stick up every traveller they fall in with for tucker. Not
a , P ac * .move on the squatter’s part, if his
station _ is any way near a mining or digging
camp, is to let his spare natives go for “ Pinki ’’
every now and then so that they mav live on the diggers.
Women and men return to the station when the “ Pinki”
is over, carrying 601 b. flour bags, containing flour, tea,
&c., and clothed in the diggers’ cast-off shirts, pants and
hats—in fact, physically stronger and their nakedness
covered, without costing the squatter a bean.
* * * •
.Again, I’ve seen a native sent on the road with a mob
of killing sheep (native name, cookinjerry) sold to a pub-
lican about 20 miles off (owner of this station a small
squatter ; most of these Nor’-West squatters are only
cockatoos). The trip would take at least two days, as
the sheep must not be hurried. The native’s entire outfit
for the journey was a waist-strap round his middle, a
dirty handkerchief knotted to it back and front and
passing between his legs, and a quart-pot. He
would have to shepherd mob night and day, as there was
no yard on the road, and, while shepherding, find his food
on the bare road if he couldn’t cadge it from a chance
traveller.
• * • •
Again, I’ve worked on a diggings, nearest station 25 miles
oil, and I ve seen a native waik in with a pack-bag on his
head full of corned beef, and about 701 b. in weight. This
was in summer time (the Nor’-West is in the tropics,
average heat about 115 in the shade or more). The first
water on the road from that station was 16 miles (the
station-owner could find no water-bag for a “ damned
nigger of course). I’ve seen the squatter’s son take
delivery and tell the native “ walki morianti ” (meaning
travel quickly ”) and send that native back the
road he came without a mouthful of food—so miles
walk between meals—in Hell. That station had plenty of
horses, but horses are worth money and natives are not.
ve seen a native travel same journey and arrive with a
lib. tin of butter. I asked the squatter’s son if he would
not give native a feed before sending him back, and he
replied “ No ; he might loiter on the way.” So, unless
he scored a feed from a miner or at one of the pubs., he
travelled back as he arrived—empty inside ; and a fat lot
he received when he arrived back on the station—l li-.ow
it. I spoke to a white man in Roebourne, about five or six
years ago ; he drove the Govt, watercart up to the well,
where I was filling a waterbag. He wore the broad-
arrow suit, and I gave him a piece of tobacco (regret
doing so ever since), and asked him what was he
domg time ” for. Said he, “ I suppose vou read about
that nigger-shooting case at Nullagine? Well, what is the
blank-blanky country coming to when a white man gets
six weeks for shooting a blank - blanky nigger.” He
thought, really and truly, he was doing no harm in put-
ting a hole through “ his assigned servant ” ; he thought,
no doubt, the nigger was his, “body and bones”; tor
what else did the \V.A. Govt, mean by assigning “ the
nigger” to him,? Hence his very natural astonish-
ment at the severity of his punishment. The shooting
was done so openly, right in a diggers’ camp, that there
was no option but to take notice of it. Otherwise
* • • *
It is not necessary to go to South Africa to see niggers
flogged ; you can witness the flogging any day in “ Free
Australia,” and the police-court records of VV.A. will
prove it. What a splendid opening for the late-lamented
Mr. Legree, were he still to the fore ! I fancy I can see
him fixing a new wire cracker on his stockwhip, and
giving a preliminary crack or two to be sure it was in
good nick. I fancy I hear him exclaim with a shriek of
intense joy, mingled with pleased astonishment, “ What!
nothing to pay for the darned niggers ! By gosh, let me
at ’em !” And yet Mr. L. aforesaid would require to be
in good working fettle to beat the Bendhu record estab-
lished by the Anderson Bros.—that is for quickness and
thoroughness ; he would want a whip with a heavy fall
to smash up shoulder-blades as prettily as the Bendhu
pair did. But slavery ! cruelty ! In golden W.A. “ there
is none, of course,” says John Forrest.
• * • •
I’ve waited for Federation to raise my voice on the sub-
ject—it was no use before, whilst the W.A. Govt,
ruled and regulated the slave-trade in W.A.,
and paid no attention to papers, or crimes reported
against squatters, &c., unless compelled to do so, as in the
Anderson case. But if the Commonwealth Parliament
will appoint a Commission of Inquiry I will give names of
the stations and individuals (whites) I have referred-to,
and confirm all I’ve said on oath.
Which is a challenge that oan, The Bulletin
thinks, hardly be declined.
“ Agra ” tells an interesting bit of history,
or else a most deserving and cheerful lie :
Dear Bulletin. — Apropos the recent suppression of an
Irish newspaper : Here is a legend of India, showing that
the pen is mightier than the gun. Many of the States of
India are still semi-autonomous under their own Rajahs.
Now, these Rajahs were strongly opposed to the liberty of
the press (they are still, but they don’t show it with their
old candour), and when any native editor dared to criti-
cise them, by Allah 1 he was Suppressed. But some Of
the editors found that treasonous rags sold well, mainly
by reason of the shindy that everybody knew would
follow; so they would issue a flaringly re-
revolutionary newspaper, and, after collecting
the proceeds, bolt. Then when the angry Rajah sent the
native equivalent for the bobby to jug thated., they could
only seize ss. worth of an old rusty printing press. But
then that editor could not come back, and after the ex-
isting editors had bolted from all the existing States the
supply ran out. And things would have gone against
free speech, had it not been for a few Englishmen in
neighbouring British territory, who, when they heard
that there was money in it, swore that the right of the
enlightened press should not be trampled upon. There-
fore, they bought a few of the fastest available
horses, with a portable little printing press,
and were ready to battle with the enemy.
They proceeded thusly : Entering a native State, they
unpacked and issued a rag, which they called the Flying
Column. Now this paper contained nothing but the
grossest libels upon the Rajah and his Court, calculated
to boil the blood of an ice-chest and raise the ghosts of
his ancestors. They sold their copies (which were eagerly
bought-up). Then they “ got 1 ” A story is told of how
they were once pursued for three nights and days by
cavalry, but so far out-distanced their pursuers that they
were able to issue a special hotly-pursued Flying Column,
which sold at fancy prices. As the local half-monarchs
still remained obdurate, other Flying Columns came into
the field, until at last the Rajahs and Maharajahs realised
that the old stationary libeller was cheaper and more
respectful than his rushing substitute ; so they let him
come back, and killed the sale of his special seditious
edition by leaving him alone. The new horseback press
gradually died out after that, and to-day the legend of
the Flying Column is not as well-known in India as it
deserves to be.
